

# **MUNICIPAL GOVERNMENT HISTORY BOULDER, COLORADO 1975 - 1979**

**COMPILED BY  
VIRGINIA BRADDOCK  
FOR  
THE BOULDER PUBLIC LIBRARY FOUNDATION  
INC.  
AUGUST 1986**

## PREFACE

This pamphlet was made possible through a grant from the Boulder Public Library Foundation. Any errors or mis-statements are the responsibility of the compiler. Sources are primarily the newspaper clippings files of the Municipal Government Reference Center. In most cases, the items were found in the "Boulder Daily Camera". The few were taken from the "Town and Country Shopper", the "Denver Post", the Longmont "Times-Call", or the "Colorado Daily". In all direct quotations, the reporter has been cited within the text. Other sources are minutes and packets of Council meetings.

The format of this history is that under each chapter title, the years 1975 through 1979 are in sequence. Within each year are the sub-titles listed in the Table of Contents. If any heading is not found, the subject was not a substantial news item during that year. This format was chosen so the history may be expanded at some future time.

Some departments of the city may not be headliners, but provide invaluable support to all other governmental functions. More information on any department or any subject heading may be found in the files of the Municipal Government Reference Center in Boulder Public Library.

## CHAPTER I

### GOVERNMENT AND ELECTIONS

1975

#### GOVERNMENT

#### CITY COUNCIL

In a "Camera" article dated January 10, Ken Frizell wrote that Planning Board member Jay Tracy had sent a message to the City Council. That message was "City Council should get off its collective butt and make a decision on the annexation of Gunbarrel." The Council has been reluctant to put a definite time limit on annexation pending further study.

During April, a group formed by City Council held meetings to acquaint residents of the Gunbarrel area with their options on the future of their area. The Gunbarrel Area Citizen's Advisory Committee heard that the city administration favors annexation. According to John Lannon, reporting for the "Camera" on April 17, Lou Dow, assistant to the City Manager, said that under current negotiations, if Gunbarrel residents decide to incorporate, the city might provide them with utilities in exchange for land-use controls such as zoning and density regulations. When asked why the city was against a regional shopping center in the area, Dow replied that the 20,000 vehicle trips generated daily by such a center would disrupt the quiet residential atmosphere of Gunbarrel. He also said it would cost more to provide services to a center located in Gunbarrel than it would if the center were located in Boulder's central area.

At the same meeting, a resident presented the case for Gunbarrel's status quo, saying that annexation to Boulder wouldn't significantly

improve the services that Boulder and the county were already providing.

In another article by Ken Frizell, it was reported that D. W. Pettyjohn, a Gunbarrel resident, presented a pitch for incorporation saying that the costs of that versus the costs of annexation are insignificant. Pettyjohn argued that if annexed, Gunbarrel would represent about seven per cent of Boulder's total population, but would represent more than 12 per cent in property tax revenues and more than 12 per cent of the city's assessed value. He told the committee that if a major shopping center were to locate in Gunbarrel, an estimated \$1 million in sales tax would be generated.

On April 24, John Lannon reported in the "Camera" that the Gunbarrel Committee was told it could opt for a number of intergovernmental service agreements and buy only those services needed. In this way the costs of incorporation could be kept low. When committee member Mike Kennedy asked if open space acquisitions were being made in an effort to provide Boulder and Gunbarrel with the common boundary necessary for annexation, Lou Dow said that one proposed route to develop the boundary was through open space lands. If that route were taken, he said it would take approximately one and a half to two years for the boundary to be established.

The Citizen's Advisory Committee decided to postpone further hearings and an opinion survey, and try to negotiate with the city about the status of services in the interim until annexation is possible. The largest concern is the continued payment of double inside-city utility rates. A spokesman for the committee said that residents had already paid more than \$1.5 million in excess sewer, water and tap fees.

On August 6, Ken Frizell wrote in the "Camera" that the Gunbarrel Area Citizen's Committee

... created by the Boulder City Council to study the feasibility of annexation, has spent the past several months examining annexation, incorporation, the cost of special service districts and maintenance of the status quo.

Lou Dow, last week termed the committee's incorporation estimates "unrealistic".

Monday, Tom Stroop, committee chairman, defended his group's work and accused Dow of giving the committee inconsistent cost estimates.

Dow said that the city's annexation estimates were never intended to represent actual costs. ... He said the figures provided to the committee represented only the "added increment of what it would take to annex ...". Finances are a factor, but there are also questions to be answered concerning control, growth, land use and political feelings."

"Camera" staff writer Frizell reported on August 12 that Mr. Stroop sent out a letter to Gunbarrel residents urging them to retain the status quo when they voted in the annexation opinion survey on this date. Mr. Stroop said that "a high elected Boulder official ..." told several of the committee that Gunbarrel has the power to get a better deal from Boulder. He also indicated there is considerable discontent with the present Boulder city administration ... and that the Boulder staff has not only been devious with our committee, but they have been devious with the Boulder City Council, too.

In a "Longmont Times-Call" article on August 13, Chuck Rankin reported that more than 40 per cent of the property owners in the Gunbarrel area voted overwhelmingly (86 per cent) to keep that unincorporated area exactly that -- unincorporated and unannexed to the city of Boulder.

Of the 2,000 to 2,500 residents eligible to vote, 1,052 cast ballots in the August 12 opinion poll with 915 voting against annexation. The vote represented a second rejection within a year of any drastic change in the political or economic status of a semi-developed area. Last year, more than 400 residents of Niwot also rejected incorporation.

In November, the Council unanimously passed one resolution declaring the intent to put Boulder in a position to annex the Gunbarrel area and another requesting the Planning Board to review and advise on possible routes of land annexation that would lead to an adjacent boundary. And in December, the board recommended that the route start north near Wonderland Hill, cross open space land, then turn east through Boulder Reservoir to 63rd Street.

The City Council, on a six to three vote, approved a resolution accepting the annexation petition filed by Flatiron Companies for a 400 acre site south of Table Mesa Drive and the Denver-Boulder Turnpike. ("Camera, May 7, Ken Frizell)

In a study session in October, the Council reviewed the proposal which had been through several public hearings, including four by the Planning Board, which recommended Council approval.

The plan calls for the city to annex 418 acres, permit Flatiron Companies to mine sand and gravel for 20 years, during which time they would reclaim the land. If given a favorable tax break from the Internal Revenue Service, they would then donate 379 acres to the city.

Open Space Board member Roger Cracraft requested that the city purchase a 1,000 foot buffer strip to preserve the portion of the site adjacent to the turnpike. Real Estate Director Don Walker recommended against that purchase. ("Camera", October 15, Ken Frizell)

In the October 29 edition of the "Colorado Daily", Jeff Morgan reported on the first hearing before the Council which was also the fifth open forum in the Flatiron Companies' year-long effort to get its proposal to mine gravel at the city's southern border approved. Ed McDowell, spokesman for Flatiron, emphasized the reclamation aspects of the mining operation

comparing it to a similar project in the White Rocks area. Consultants who had worked on the environmental impact study stated that the city had nothing to fear. City Planner Nolan Rosall summarized Planning Board and administration recommendations that annexation and the special use permit (to mine) should be approved. The annexation was necessary because the property was in the county and zoned as rural residential.

The Flatiron Companies proposed donating part of the land to the Colorado Open Land Foundation who would in turn give it to the city, thus giving Flatiron some tax benefit.

Because of the large number of questions raised during the public hearing, the Council instructed staff to write answers and have them ready for its work study session.

At a study session held in November, Councilman Bob White expressed concern that the 117 acres to be retained by Flatiron may eventually be developed into housing. Councilwoman Janet Roberts asked about the legality of the present Council giving first reading approval when second reading will come before a new Council in January. The delay in readings was arranged so that the Internal Revenue Service would have time to make its ruling. ("Camera", November 12, Ken Frizell)

Ordinance 4070 was approved on first reading at the Council meeting on December 9. Mayor Penfield Tate cast the tie-breaking vote in favor of the annexation. ("Camera", December 10, Bill Jordan)

In May, residents of Country Club Park and Wagoner Manor subdivisions east of Boulder voted nearly four to one in favor of annexing to the city. In June, the Council unanimously approved the annexation and zoning ordinance.

"Town & Country" reporter Barbara Gigone asked January 15th

Whatever happened to the "growth study" which took over one year, hours and hours of work by ... citizen committees and a number of paid consultants, and resulted in ten thick volumes of information?

According to Rich Lopez of the Planning Department the study is being used in the formulation of a growth management system. Because of the many steps necessary to implement the system, it will probably take most of 1975 before it is in operation. Many people have expressed concern over the long period between adoption of the interim growth policy and a final growth management system.

At an April study session of the Council, Planning Director Nolan Rosall explained that the heart of the proposed new Growth Management System (GMS) is that "we are not aiming at determining a particular rate of growth as much as we are aiming at obtaining the benefits of growth without the negative side effects." According to "Camera" writer Ken Frizell, whatever Council decides ... the new plan will likely be kicked around for several months before a final decision is reached.

In the "Camera" of March 4, Mr. Frizell reported that Clarke Watson, chairman of the Boulder Centennial-Bicentennial Commission announced his resignation, primarily because his commission activities were taking too much time from his business. He also said he didn't think any individual "should be more committed to a community project than a group of elected officials," an obvious reference to Council. The Council did not appropriate money requested by Mr. Watson for staff help.

"Camera" staff writer Barbralu Fried wrote on March 8 that the Boulder Commission had asked Mr. Watson to resign and had accepted his resignation. Pamela Stinson, chairman protem, said that Watson "works better on his own" and tended to take over decisions and things that could have been done by

others.

On March 11, "Camera" reporter Ken Frizell said that the Council had reviewed cuts to the 1975 budget which City Manager Archie Twitchell says must be made. Council had decided in February to forgo a fourth fire station, to increase recreation fees, and to reduce city staff by attrition. Also cut was money that would have gone for improvements in fire stations to accomodate women firefighters.

In June, the city was awarded a federal grant of \$245,000 for its Housing and Community Development Program. Of this, \$175,000 has been designated for downtown mall construction. In the second and third years another \$475,000 is earmarked for the mall and \$220,000 is proposed for a Senior Citizen's Center at Canyon Park.

On September 10, "Camera" writer Bill Jordan said that Boulder City Council and Lafayette City Council had been notified that "Camera" reporters will no longer sit in on closed-door sessions if they can't report on the happenings.

On September 30, City Attorney Walt Wagenhals gave the opinion that "executive sessions" of the Council are illegal. The Charter states that "All meetings of the Council or committees thereof shall be public", but Council has held closed sessions anyway, claiming the privilege to do so in matters related to real estate, personnel and attorney-client relationships. According to Ken Frizell:

Council has barred the public from such meetings and has allowed the press to sit in, but only with the understanding that the content of the meetings could not be reported.

The Daily Camera feels that council has not used executive sessions exclusively for such matters and has in the past discussed departmental audits, appointments to city boards and commissions, as well as appointments to council.

(Mayor) Tate and other members of council attempted to hold an

executive session as recently as last week to discuss City Manager Archie Twitchell's job performance.

After Attorney Wagenhal's opinion, Council decided to initiate a ballot proposal to authorize Council to hold closed sessions. The Charter change was worded on the ballot as follows:

Continues the requirement that the City Council have all its meetings open to the public, but would permit going into executive session for consideration of documents or testimony given in confidence. Only discussion would occur and no actions could be taken while in executive session.

One argument used before the election was that allowing for executive sessions was a move backwards towards a Watergate approach of governmental secrecy.

During October, the Council was attempting to evaluate City Manager Archie Twitchell. Mayor Penfield Tate sent letters to Council members asking for their evaluation by mail. Councilman Walter Slack openly dissented with this procedure. The performance review was postponed until after the election.

In a "Camera" article dated December 19, Ken Frizell reported that Mayor Tate and Councilman Buchanan had developed a range of salaries for the City Manager which began at \$32,000 and ran to \$36,000. The salaries carried performance designations ranging from "minimum" to "superior"

Rather than publicly discuss City Manager Twitchell's performance, council conducted a secret vote, each member writing down a salary figure.

The numbers, which were then averaged by Tate, resulted in the city manager's \$2000 raise and a rating somewhere between "satisfactory" and "excellent" . . . .

Councilman Buchanan, as he had in the past, said it is impossible to have a "meaningful evaluation" in public.

At the same session, Council gave raises to the municipal judge and the municipal attorney and other management personnel.

Ken Frizell reported in the December 17 "Camera" that the City Manager had requested an 8.99 per cent across the board increase for his management staff, a group which includes department heads and other supervisory personnel. The figure would have been consistent with a settlement negotiated earlier this year with members of the Boulder Municipal Employees Association.

But Council was reluctant to grant the increase and instead adopted a motion made by Buchanan to grant the increase with the provision that no management level employee receive more than a \$1,339 raise. Buchanan's plan was approved on a six to three vote.

In September, Council approved on a six to three vote an ordinance placing the question of a one per cent sales tax increase on the November ballot. They tied the proposal to a promise to reduce property taxes by about 6.7 mills. Council also unanimously decided to ask for a vote on dropping the age requirement for Council candidates from 25 to 18.

Members of the American Civil Liberties Union presented City Council with a proposed "sunshine" law on March 18. The draft called for financial disclosure on the part of elected officials, candidates and top administrators. Kenneth Wright asked the city attorney to work on an ordinance. David Markowitz, an ACLU member and former candidate for City Council called attention to another proposal which had been submitted last year. That bill, supported by both the CU student government and "Inform", a non-partisan voter registration group, would permit people to retain their right to vote in municipal elections when they move as long as they remain within the city limits.

In July, Walter Slack and Robert Trenka were the only Council members to vote against a "sunshine" ordinance for Council candidates. The ordinance was a watered-down version of the one originally proposed.

In a September 10 "Colorado Daily" article, Timothy Lange wrote:

For the past 20 years in Boulder, before each City Council election, some people make the charge that the city's business interests aren't being adequately represented on the nine-member council.

A look at the occupations of the 39 City Councilmembers elected and appointed from 1955 to the present, however, indicates that business interests, far above any other, have dominated Boulder's City Council for most of those 20 years.

Of the 39 councilmembers, 14 (36 per cent) have been business owners or managers, three (7 per cent) have been bankers and five (13 per cent) have been realtors or realty-associated professionals.

The article goes on to list the number and percentages of other occupational groups who served during those years.

In an article dated October 30, "Camera" writer Ken Frizell said:

Both the Boulder City Council and the city administration can always be expected to be fair game in a local election. This year has been no exception.

... criticisms have fallen on an administration which has faced a summer's worth of problems ranging from hostile employees to having to explain the expenditure of several thousand dollars for office furniture and remodeling, as well as outside studies -- these at a time when the administration also proposed cuts in library hours and closure of the city's recreation centers.

Incumbant candidate George Boland ... who recently refused to evaluate City Manager Archie Twitchell by a secret evaluation form, was most notably at odds with the administration over a plan to have city firemen perform non-fire related duties.

Boland has also expressed dismay over the City Manager's proposed administrative reorganization. ... "The city manager has the right to run the administration the way he feels he must," said Boland, but if he adopts a plan that is going to cause discontentment among the employees of the city to where it disrupts operations, it's time for council to take a look at that.

On November 26, "Camera" writer Ken Frizell reported:

Boulder City Manager Archie Twitchell's plan to reorganize the city administration suffered a setback Tuesday night when City Council declined to discuss the matter.

... Mayor Penfield Tate announced that the reorganization, which has led to a dispute between Twitchell and Library Director Marcelee Gralapp, was being pulled from the agenda.

Thomas D. Waugh, a former city councilman and chairman of the Planning Board wrote a guest editorial for the "Camera" dated November 9 on the role of the mayor in Boulder's city government.

... The mayor must be the political head of city government. The city manager must be the administrative head. Neither can do the other's job. When the mayor is not really functioning as political head of city government, a vacuum is created. That vacuum inevitably sucks the city manager into politics, where he cannot be effective. In return, the Council begins to meddle in details of administration. The result is chaos.

The mayor's chief function is as interlocutor. He or she needs to be the communicating link between manager and Council ... (and) ... must be in constant contact with the other members of Council. He must know their attitudes, their biases, and their loyalties to various constituent groups.

Finally, the mayor needs to be the spokesman for city government to the people.

#### CITY MANAGER

In April, Peat Marwick, Mitchell & Co. sent to Assistant City Manager Robert Westdyke its report on the feasibility of establishing an "in-house" computer processing capability. The city had been using service bureaus to process some financial and management information. In May, the Council unanimously authorized the City Manager to execute a contract for purchase of computer equipment. ("Camera", May 7)

During June the offices of the City Manager were remodeled to provide more space and a new office for the assistant City Manager.

In a "Camera" article dated June 2, Ken Frizell quoted City Manager Twitchell as saying that "because the city has indulged in deficit spending for the past three years and because of declining revenues," he is now faced with holding next year's budget within 1975 levels. ... "The city has three alternatives -- no salary increases for employees, eliminate all

capital outlays or cut expenses."

The same article indicated that city employees were supporting firemen who were upset over plans to assign them non-fire related duties as an economy measure. There were expressions of interest in a change to a mayor-council form of government or a vote of no-confidence in the City Manager.

Meetings on the 1976 budget continued throughout June. Each department was requested to lower its budget by 10 per cent. The City Council agreed with the City Manager's plan to allow attrition to cut the work force.

Assistant City Manager Westdyke said that unless the Council approved a vote for additional sales tax revenue, they would have to agree to cut programs.

City Manager Twitchell canceled plans to build an addition to the Municipal Building, and funds from the \$400,000 budgeted for that construction were used to make up the budget deficit.

In a "Camera" article dated October 23, reporter Ken Frizell said that opposition to the City Manager's reorganization plan had ebbed. Mr. Twitchell had proposed regrouping his staff into four "super departments" called Planning Services, Administrative Services, Property Services and Human Services. The objections stemmed from the insertion of a new layer of administration between the departments and the city manager. Library Director Marcellee Gralapp termed the reorganization a subordination of the library and said the library, by City Charter, has a "direct line function" to the City Manager. The dispute was resolved at a meeting of staff and Library Commission members with Miss Gralapp retaining her direct access to the manager.

In November, the City Manager requested a budget transfer and approval of a position entitled Coordinator for Property Services. He proposed that part of the salary be paid out of the Real Estate budget and the remainder from that part of the City Manager's budget allocated for the salaries of two assistants.

The Council, which previously had conducted evaluations of their city manager in executive session, were told this year that such closed meetings were not legal. They postponed a review until after the November election. However, the voters rejected a Charter amendment that would have allowed closed sessions for personnel and financial discussions.

In a December 19th article, "Camera" reporter Ken Frizell said that Mayor Tate and Councilman Buchanan had developed a range of salaries that included performance designations. Rather than publicly discussing Mr. Twitchell's performance, the Council members wrote down a salary figure giving a rating.

#### CITY ATTORNEY

In a "Camera" article dated November 26, Ken Frizell reported that City Attorney Wagenhals had ruled it illegal for the city to pick up the tab for City Council spouses to attend an upcoming conference in Miami.

In December, City Attorney Wagenhals was before the Colorado Supreme Court presenting arguments in two cases involving the downtown mall and the city's refusal in 1972 to extend utilities to a Gunbarrel area developer. In the latter case, the city had appealed a lower court decision to the high court. The mall court test was initiated by the city to clarify a question of the rights of downtown property owners. Defendants, a group of

local businessmen, appealed a District Court ruling in favor of the city to the State Supreme Court.

#### MUNICIPAL COURT

Municipal Court Administrator Terry Aragon said that the city's three meter maids gave out more than 61,000 parking tickets during 1974 and collected some \$86,000 in fines.

#### ELECTIONS

One city election was held in 1975. On November 4, five Council members were chosen from a slate of 12 candidates. There were also three ballot issues: One suggesting an increase in the sales tax from two to three percent with a corresponding decrease in the property tax; one allowing the Council to hold executive sessions; and one lowering the age to 18 for Council candidates. All three issues were defeated by a margin of two to one.

#### GOVERNMENT

#### CITY COUNCIL

At the first meeting of the new Council, Frank Buchanan had been elected mayor on a six to three vote. One of his first acts as mayor was to draft a rule that would discourage news releases by department heads, employees and/or employee organizations. Some employees interpreted the proposal as a "gag" rule and a threat to free speech. Mayor Buchanan very shortly withdrew his proposal.

"Camera" writer Ken Frizell reported that the Council voted eight to one at its first regular meeting to direct the City Manager "to follow a northeasterly annexation route (to Gunbarrel) through much of what is already city-owned open space.

Even if Gunbarrel residents never ask to be annexed, the city, because of state law, could prevent incorporation if Boulder's boundaries fall within one mile of Gunbarrel. In another Gunbarrel-related matter, council voted unanimously to direct (City Manager) Twitchell to explore the feasibility of creating a special district to provide fire protection to the area.

In March, the Council approved the first in a series of annexations designed to put the city in a position to annex the Gunbarrel area. The land, between Wonderland Lake and Lee Hill Road, was already owned by the city.

In an article dated November 17, Mr. Frizell said that:

Council ... had originally expected to follow a leisurely annexation policy that would have seen the city limits extended to ... (Gunbarrel) ... over a two year period. (However) ... increasing pressure from county officials who say they cannot provide the kinds of urban services that Gunbarrel residents want and who fear the impact of the recently adopted Danish growth plan ... led City Manager Bob Westdyke to recommend that council speed up the annexation process (by approving each parcel on an emergency basis).

In March, the Council voted unanimously to annex the Maxwell Reservoir and other land surrounding it.

The controversial Growth Management System which would create a permit system for new development has been "deferred until late this year," said Planning Director Nolan Rosall in January. In the meantime his staff will proceed with revisions to the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan.

In the March 16 issue of the "Colorado Daily", Timothy Lange reported that Colorado's Supreme Court had "upheld a district court judge who had ruled the City of Boulder cannot refuse to provide water and sewer service to property owners in the Gunbarrel area ... ."

The decision was good news for Lawrence Robinson, the developer who pursued the case through the courts for three years.

Robinson wanted to develop 79 acres north of Boulder. His development met the county's requirements for rural residential subdivisions, but county officials made his moving ahead with the development contingent on obtaining water and sewer service from a secure source.

Robinson applied to the Boulder Valley Water and Sanitation District (BVWSD) for those services, and his request was approved. But the City of Boulder, which created and ultimately controls the BVWSD, rejected his application on the ground that his subdivision proposal conflicted with the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan and the city's Interim Growth Policy.

Essentially city planners said that the proposed development was "out of phase" with the Comp Plan, which schedules Robinson's land for development "after 1990."

Robinson sued. In May 1974, Boulder District court Judge William Neighbors ruled in his favor, arguing that the city must extend water and sewer service equally to all customers because it "acts as a utility," that is, a monopoly. As such, ... Boulder can only refuse service for utility reasons, not for growth control or land-use reasons.

Nolan Rosall ... (said) ... "There's no question that this places the Comp Plan revision and implementation into a high priority position." ... A real need (he said) is to make the subdivision regulations and zoning consistent with the plan.

Within a few months of the court decision, the city had received six requests for utility service from individuals and developers outside the city.

In August, the Council, after years of debate and court fights, voted unanimously to extend water and sewer service to Larry Robinson; and to extend the same services to Don Unkefer, a second Gunbarrel area developer.

In October, the County Planning Commission voted three to two to approve conditionally the controversial 78-acre "The Willows" subdivision proposed by Lawrence Robinson. The conditions imposed are for adequate fire protection and a contribution towards the cost of four-laning area highways. These requests are on top of routine subdivision requirements such as interior road improvements and park land dedication.

The County Commissioners tabled a proposed addition to Heatherwood subdivision pending answers to questions about urban services in the Gunbarrel area. County Land Use Director Ed Tepe said the Comprehensive Plan will address those questions.

In November, Council voted five to four to approve the annexation of a proposed 108-unit development into the Boulder Valley Water and Sanitation District. The request for services came from William Carran ... on the heels of the controversial Robinson decision.

In April, Councilman Paul Danish presented his five-year growth regulation plan. Based on the Petaluma, California system which was recently upheld by the Supreme Court (by refusing to hear the case thus upholding an appellate court decision), it set a limit on the number of building permits issued each year and included a point system for rationing permits to developers.

In effect, the plan would fill in city lots, possibly stimulate more housing redevelopment in the older areas of the city and discourage sprawl.

In a "Denver Post" article dated May 23, Jane Cracraft asked Mr.

Danish if growth control would inflate the cost of housing in Boulder. Danish said it may do that, but growth seems to make Boulder a more expensive place to live, too. As more people come, more streets, sewer lines, parks, and fire stations have to be built.

In July, Planning Director Nolan Rosall said he will recommend that the Planning Board vote against adoption of the Danish growth plan. Ken Frizell, in a July 13 issue of the "Camera" wrote

Rosall faulted Danish plan, warning that it would force growth into the unincorporated portions of the valley and increase pressures (on nearby communities).

In August, the Council voted five to four against a referendum on the Danish plan. Most vocal of Danish's critics was Deputy Mayor Bob Trenka, who termed the plan "disruptive" to the city's efforts to update the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan. ("Camera", August 18, Ken Frizell) Danish said the Comp Plan revisions, which will see the division of the Boulder Valley into three zones, speaks "only to where growth will occur. My plan," he said, "says when it will occur and how fast."

In September, the City Council, on being presented with a petition bearing more than 5,000 signatures, formally placed the growth issue on the November ballot.

In a guest editorial in the "Camera" of October 23, Mayor Frank Buchanan urged a "no" vote on the Danish plan.

It is true we had an accelerated rate of growth the latter part of the 1960's, a period during which we assimilated accelerated growth of the University and a new IBM plant. In 1971, we all agreed in a referendum that we did not want Zero Population Growth, but we did want to cut the rate of growth from that of the late sixties to a manageable level. Since 1971, the State Legislature has advised the University that it will not grow, and all elected officials, City and County, and the Chamber of Commerce, agree we will not seek or welcome, and that we will not discourage any new large primary employers. Since 1972 our actual rate of growth in the City and County has been less than one-half of what it had been in the peak years ... . At the present time, the

City and the County are coordinating their efforts so that the city has effective control of the entire Boulder Valley, and, if we and other municipalities continue to agree with the County that all growth in the County will be forced within the municipal service areas, then the County has, under State law as a "region", the power to stop sprawl between Broomfield and Boulder and along the diagonal to Longmont, and to stop the urbanization of our mountains and farms.

It is patently clear, we have the revenues and we will continue to have sufficient revenues to provide necessary municipal services at a reasonable level, to all who live here and work here now, and who will live here and work here in the future. And without any increase in taxes. To assert otherwise is a deliberate choice to misinform. If this proposal is adopted, we will lose control of Boulder Valley and will have robbed the County of the power to stop urbanization.

Under the growth control ordinance passed on November 2, if Council did not implement it by next March 10, a moratorium on building permits would go into effect. Between election day and the publication of the December 19 "Camera", Steve Hoffman reported that more than 150 permits had been issued, roughly four times the number during the same period last year.

According to Nolan Rosall, the planning staff should have a draft resolution for putting the Danish plan into effect written within two weeks.

A "Denver Post" article dated January 18 was headlined "Boulder City Council, Twitchell Still Failing to Synchronize". It recapped some management problems and the method of evaluation of the City Manager after the banning of executive sessions. A former councilman was quoted as saying:

The 1971 council was tired of the strong manager-weak council form of government. They wanted a weak manager-strong council ... . What we wound up with was a weak manager-weak council and that's why the city is in a mess.

Councilwoman Janet Roberts said that it was unfair to blame the City Manager for all the problems, that he was trying to let the Council set the course of action, but "the council was divided and nothing really

happened."

In the "Camera" of April 7, Ken Frizell announced that Archie Twitchell, the City Manager for the last three years was fired on a five to four vote. Over 300 citizens attended the meeting and more than two dozen persons spoke to Council, the majority of those protesting the firing and the manner in which it was done.

... the news of the move to unseat Twitchell came only after the Daily Camera discovered that Buchanan and Trenka had last Thursday approached other members of the council to learn how they would react should Twitchell be asked to leave.

The Camera confronted Buchanan with the story on Friday, at which point he and Trenka scheduled a meeting with Twitchell to deliver the ultimatum (the choice of resigning by Saturday noon or being fired). Other members of the council did not learn of the meeting until later.

Council members voting against sacking the manager were Bob White, Janet Roberts, Ruth Correll and Paul Danish.

Later in April the Council indicated it would use a citizen's committee to screen applicants for a new City Manager, as recommended by Acting City Manager Robert Westdyke. City Attorney Wagenhals had advised the Council that the use of a consultant was the only way to keep the manager applications confidential. Since the committee would be an agent of the Council, its records would be open to the public.

Jane Cracraft reported in the "Denver Post" on April 13 that a formal complaint was filed in ... Municipal Court charging that Mayor Frank Buchanan and Deputy Mayor Bob Trenka violated the city charter by meeting secretly with other city council members on April 1. Buchanan had said that he "polled" the city council members before giving an ultimatum to City Manager Archie Twitchell on April 2 to quit or be fired.

Ken Frizell reported in the "Camera" of May 5 that:

Petitions bearing the signatures of 1,363 Boulderites demanding the resignations of Mayor Frank Buchanan and Deputy Mayor Bob Trenka were delivered to City Council Tuesday night.

They were presented by Sara-Jane Cohen, spokesman for a group which had formed three weeks ago in protest over the firing of ... Archie Twitchell.

Cohen ... told council that the (petition) drive was motivated by the

"gag" order abortively imposed on city employees by Mayor Buchanan last January, the lop-sided appointments (all male) to the city boards and commissions last February and the secret meetings and 16-hour ultimatum relating to the city manager firing.

In May, the City Council appointed a Denver attorney to act as special counsel to the Municipal Court, according to Ken Frizell in the "Camera" of May 12. John L. Kane, Jr. will determine whether or not grounds exist for the court to issue a summons to Buchanan and Trenka.

Mr. Frizell reported June 21 that John Kane said that because the Charter does not define a meeting, a defense attorney could argue that no formally prescribed meeting was held and therefore, no violation occurred. "I do not believe," wrote Kane, "that a criminal prosecution could be sustained against Buchanan and Trenka."

In a "Camera" article dated May 22, Ken Frizell reported that Ruth Correll is interested in Colorado University paying for its fire protection, and wants them to collect an admissions tax on events in Folsom Stadium. She said fire protection on campus cost Boulder \$200,000 last year, and that crowds attending stadium events have a heavy impact on city services.

#### CITY MANAGER

In a "Denver Post" article dated January 18, Jane Cracraft reported that:

This city, once a model of municipal government, is gripped by a management crisis that threatens the tenure of the city manager and every department head.

The administration suffers from a lack of teamwork and a tendency for department heads to pass their tough decisions on to Twitchell -- who has been working 70 hours per week -- or to make decisions without consulting heads of related departments.

The lack of communication between administrators trickled down and caused a lowering of morale in all departments. Many city projects started during the previous two years just bogged down.

Recognizing the serious management problems, Council members began talking about firing Archie Twitchell. Mr. Twitchell indicated he would appreciate more specific policy directions from the Council.

On Sunday, April 4, "Denver Post" correspondent Jane Cracraft reported that on the previous Friday night, City Manager Twitchell was given an ultimatum to resign by Saturday noon or face a vote by City Council.

"Camera" staffer Ken Frizell reported on April 5 that the reasons for the ultimatum by Mayor Buchanan and Deputy Mayor Bob Trenka were "cumulative". Among those given for dissatisfaction were the lack of coordination with the county over location of the sewage treatment plant and slow action on providing adequate downtown parking. "And," Trenka said, "there is little argument over the fact that Twitchell's administration had drifted from crisis to crisis."

In a five to four vote on April 6 the Council decided to fire the city manager. Assistant City Manager Bob Westdyke was appointed as acting manager.

The Council began advertising for manager applications and set up a citizen's committee to screen them. Over 650 were received and the committee gave a list of seven finalists to Council. Of the seven names, five had been requested to submit an application. One of these was Acting Manager Westdyke. In August, the Council unanimously voted to appoint him to serve as City Manager.

In October, City Manager Westdyke appointed a deputy city manager. Jim Piper had been administrator of the city's budget and grant division.

Added to the problems in management, were the feelings of alienation among employees in a time of lowered budgets and fewer workers. In March,

the City Council had requested a comparison of wages paid city employees and those paid by the local private sector. Some members of Council expressed the concern that the city generally pays more.

In August, representatives of all three city unions, the Boulder Police Benefit Association, the Boulder Municipal Employees Association (BMEA), and the local chapter of the International Association of Firefighters expressed anger over a new rule requiring a vote of the people in the event of an impasse in contract negotiations. Past procedure involved the selection of an outside arbitrator. According to Personnel Director Mike Patton, the new rule does not need to be agreed to by the unions in order to implement it.

According to a spokesman for the agency handling the city's insurance, more claims and costlier settlements in Boulder and across the country have created a situation similar to the malpractice dilemma facing doctors.

Council was told that Boulder's poor loss history resulting from general liability and false arrest claims has compounded the problem. ("Camera", March 10, Ken Frizell)

At the end of the year, the liability insurance for Boulder County was being canceled, and insurance for the city was in doubt. Insurance costs had more than doubled during the year.

The BMEA, which had been known as a "party group", began to expand its membership and became a major force negotiating for better wages and working conditions.

By September, the firefighters and the municipal employees had agreed to new contracts.

#### CITY ATTORNEY

In May, the City Attorney concluded he should not act as attorney for Mayor Buchanan and Deputy Mayor Trenka in a case stemming from complaints filed by three men who alleged the two had "initiated and participated in non-public meetings" of Council members.

Linda Cornett reported in the June 2 "Camera" that:

(The council) ... received a memorandum from city attorney Walt Wagenhals defining "meetings" as gatherings of authorities where members are notified and given a chance to attend and the gathering is intended for consideration of business before the authorities. The definition is a softening of Wagenhal's position in April that any gathering of city officials could constitute a meeting in violation of the city charter's open meetings regulation. A "meeting", Wagenhals now says, implies intent to conduct public business.

In an interview in June with "Denver Post" reporter Jane Cracraft, Mr. Wagenhals talked about lawsuits charging police brutality. He said they were virtually unheard of five years ago but had become common. Since the case filed by the family of Philip Thompson in 1972, at least 16 formal letters of complaint and half a dozen civil suits had been filed.

In September, Mr. Wagenhals urged Council to inform the County Planning Commission of its intention to stick to a strict interpretation of the court decision in the Robinson case and not use its utilities as a growth control. The Council unanimously voted to do so. Howard Klemme, a member of the commission, had urged that the policy be reviewed, saying that by amending its contracts with the Lefthand Water and Sanitation District and by announcing it is no longer the exclusive provider of utilities in the Boulder Valley, the city could reinstate the use of water and sewer services as growth controls. Wagenhals said the high court's decision was explicit. ("Camera", September 23, Ken Frizell)

#### ELECTIONS

After the Council declined to put the growth control ordinance on the November 2 ballot, a citizen's "Slow Growth" committee led by Councilman Paul Danish gathered 5,300 signatures on a petition that would allow Boulder citizens to vote at the general election. The proposal would limit growth in the city to one-and-a-half to two per cent per year for a five year period by limiting the number of dwelling units built in major projects during that time to an average of 450 per year. Opposition to the ordinance came from the mayor and a majority of the Council, the city administration, the Chamber of Commerce, and the Board of Realtors.

The final count from the County Elections Office was 18,783 in favor and 18,231 opposed. The Danish Plan was approved by a margin of 552 votes.

After the Council, in response to a request from the state, voted for a supplemental appropriation of \$600,000 in matching funds to extend 47th Street from Baseline Road to Arapahoe Avenue, the local chapter of the Sierra Club successfully circulated petitions referring the budget question to the voters. In September, the State Highway Commission decided to pay the entire cost of the project, so the ballot question as proposed became moot. However, according to a "Camera" article on September 22 by Ken Frizell, " ... under pressure from both the Sierra Club and the State Highway Department, ... (the council) decided to go to the voters anyway."

The ballot question simply asked whether the voter was in favor of the construction of 47th Street (Foothills Parkway) from Baseline Road to the Longmont Diagonal. The issue passed with a vote of 22,394 in favor and 14,780 against.

It took some time for the election to be certified by the Secretary

of State because of complaints about "problems" in the election, some resulting from computer breakdown and some from human errors.

1977

#### GOVERNMENT

#### CITY COUNCIL

In February, the Council indicated at a study session that the city was ready to acquire the non-residential parts of Gunbarrel. In March they restated their intention of requesting petitions of annexation from the owners of the commercial and industrial property in the area.

In the "Camera" Focus section of April 3, S. I. Nealon and Mark Schultz wrote a story called "Gunbarrel: Municipal No Man's Land". They described the "spokes of the wheel" concept of growth control, the formation of the Boulder Valley Water and Sanitation District (BVWSD) in 1963 and the contract between Gunbarrel landowners and the BVWSD which "stipulated that the area could be annexed whenever the city saw fit. Another provision, to be of great importance later, required that Gunbarrel homeowners receiving BVWSD services refrain from ... any move against annexation."

Quick annexation of Gunbarrel ... would have allowed Boulder to urbanize the area slowly according to set city policies. However ... when several abortive attempts by Gunbarrel residents to force a reluctant city to commit itself resulted in nothing more concrete than a flurry of reports and memos, an already cool relationship between the two groups turned gelid.

The delay in making a commitment came as a result of a slower than expected growth rate in the Valley.

The article speculates that during a period of liberalism the Council did not want annexation because Gunbarrel was full of conservatives. A

more recent trend has brought in more diversification in housing thus diluting the conservative influence. "Election Day, 1975, turned the tables ... . With the 5-4 ideological split finally in their favor, conservative members of Council ... wasted no time in preparing to annex Gunbarrel."

In the "Denver Post" of August 3, Jane Cracraft reported that:

On an 8-1 vote the City Council annexed nearly 400 acres of industrial and commercial land in the Gunbarrel area. The action took in another 167 acres of IBM land, plus the King Soopers shopping center, the Boulder National Bank, Head Ski Corp., the Leaning Tree Publishing Co. and other industrial land.

The city is committed to providing police and fire protection within the next few months. Other urban services, such as parks and libraries, will be added later. The area already is served with city water and sewer.

"Camera" staff writer Sarah Hoover reported on January 20 that the Chamber of Commerce had released the results of a survey conducted last year before the Danish Plan was approved by voters in November. Based on the results, the Chamber plans to make recommendations that local governments have positive economic and housing policies which might "run smack against" the growth limit.

In March, the Council unanimously adopted a merit system putting the growth controls of the Danish plan into effect.

The system sets up a review process for deciding which residential construction projects will be eligible to apply for building permits at the end of two six-month periods each year for the next five years. With certain exemptions, the Danish plan limits the number of new housing units in the city to an average of 450 a year.

In June, the Chamber of Commerce sent a proposal to repeal the Danish plan to the Council. The group expressed concern that middle income persons are being priced out of the Boulder housing market.

In the "Camera" of October 27, Steve Hoffman reported on a study by the Human Resources Department. The study is part of the department's annual social report which includes a history of the city's growth and an index of local business activity. Its conclusion is that the slow-growth plan cannot be singled out as the primary cause of soaring house prices or construction rates.

Mr. Hoffman reported on November 16 that:

The extension of municipal water lines to a north Boulder property was narrowly approved by the City Council Tuesday night, marking the first test of the underlying growth-control strategy of the revised Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan.

That strategy is based on the idea of gradually phasing a full range of city services into the county. By controlling timing and location, the city and county governments are jointly attempting through the plan to stop urban sprawl in the Boulder Valley.

...the controlling phasing ... will at times conflict with the court decision on the Robinson case ... .

On a 5 to 4 vote, the council in effect decided that the Robinson decision shall remain unchallenged and will be the controlling factor when there is a clash between its legalities and the principles of the comprehensive plan.

Councilman Paul Danish was outraged at the decision to extend a water line north of the city limits to the property of Karl A. Fettig on Lee Hill Road. The issue was not water service to a warehouse, but the opening of a new area for housing development. Mr. Danish said the action subverts the intention of the Comprehensive Plan and jeopardizes efforts to control growth adjacent to the city limits. He said the broad interpretation of the Robinson decision is "a catastrophe."

R. Alan Rice reported in the "Town & Country" of December 7 that the County Planning Commission had asked to review the utility extension.

In April, the county asked the City Council to endorse the concept of issuing economic development bonds to help Celestial Seasonings build a new plant on city acreage west of Hayden Lake. The City Charter prohibits the

city from issuing bonds without first holding a special election, but the county is not subject to such a restriction. The site, known as the Reynolds property, was zoned for development only after a court ruling that the land was totally unsuited for agricultural purposes.

The Council adopted a resolution in October urging the County Commissioners to proceed with the issuance of economic development bonds for a computer located at the National Center for Atmospheric Research.

On January 12, "Camera" writer Steve Hoffman reported that:

City Council earmarked almost all of the \$1.1 million in unallocated funds available for the 1977 budget year ... giving taxpayers a 1 mill reduction and the police department an extra \$300,000 to spend ... .

... Council also set aside over \$200,000 to remodel the Municipal Building and \$250,000 to buy land in the so-called "civic block" bounded by Broadway, Canyon, Arapahoe and 9th Street.

Those proposals met with little opposition, but a \$167,000 a year "savings sharing" plan outlined ... by firefighters and city administrator Andy Hollar was firmly turned down. Instead firemen got an \$81,000 one-time pay boost ... .

At the January 18 meeting, the Council approved a \$50,000 allocation to the Chamber of Commerce to set up a Conference Bureau in Boulder.

In March, the Council accepted a revised version of a motion by Councilman Danish that the City Manager work out a system of zero-based budgeting. The version proposed by the City Manager is termed "PMS" or Performance Management System.

In June, the Council decided against giving the University of Colorado \$1 million for its Events-Conference Center. The University had asked the city to use one of its federal grants and in return to start collecting an admissions tax on campus events.

In August it was revealed that the county had levied 13 mills instead of 13.74 mills against property in the city resulting in a loss of \$150,000 in taxes. The Council decided not to file a claim.

In a "Camera" article dated September 2, Steve Hoffman reported a sharp rise in sales and property tax revenues as the effects of the mid-1970s recession disappeared.

In November, the city adopted a 1978 budget of \$24.7 million. Mr. Hoffman reported that "about \$13 million will go for ... (operating) expenses. The rest, the bulk of which is restricted by law for specific purposes such as constructing new sewage treatment facilities, goes for capital costs."

The City Manager was told to work within the \$250,000 available from the federal government for renovation of the Municipal Building. This money will give the handicapped access to the Council chambers with an elevator and improve the chambers with better seating, heating and cooling, and a more effective sound system.

The Council did vote to spend \$112,000 in city money to build a fire station in Gunbarrel. The Economic Development Administration (EDA) had approved \$200,000 in federal money for the project.

Council also unanimously approved \$17,300 in city funds for public restrooms on the mall. The EDA had granted the city's original request for \$50,000.

Also given unanimous approval was an allocation of \$60,000 to the Police Department for overtime pay so that the mall can be more fully patrolled.

The budget continued a \$50,000 allocation to the Boulder Conference and Visitor's Bureau.

In a "Town & Country" article dated May 4, former councilman Tom Waugh gave advice to present Council members.

At all costs avoid these interminable studies. If we counted money spent on flood control studies, and had spent the money on flood control

itself, the possibility of deluge would be of less concern to us.

... Council study sessions are sterile and the public participation period is a farce, Waugh believes. Turn it around, he recommends. Invite the public in for study sessions where their views could be valuable ... and keep on schedule at regular council meetings where decisions are made.

#### CITY MANAGER

Jim Peltz wrote in the "Camera" dated March 27 that following a note in the March 1 "Wall Street Journal", Personnel Director Mike Patton had received dozens of inquiries about the city's layoff plan. It originated during the 1975 recession and went into effect on January 1, 1976. It was designed to prevent keeping substandard employees strictly because of their seniority.

The Council gave informal approval for City Manager Westdyke and Planning Director Nolan Rosall to develop more sophisticated budgeting techniques. Rosall will attempt to tie the city's Capital Improvements Program to the revised Comprehensive Plan. Westdyke and Budget Director Joe Romano will set up a "Performance Management System". It could take two years before detailed performance measurements are in place. ("Camera", April 14, Steve Hoffman)

Andy Hollar, acting director of Public Works since Sam Hobbs resigned in February, was named director in May by City Manager Westdyke. In February, Transportation and Building Inspection were added to the existing seven divisions of the Public Works Department. Mr. Hollar will continue to oversee the construction of the Downtown Mall. ("Camera", May 20, Steve Hoffman)

In August, the Council held its very first public review of any city manager's track record. The procedures set up by Council members Janet

Roberts, Wayne Hutchens, and Greg Lefferdink outline a number of general categories for judging performance, including budget preparation, follow-through on Council policies and response to citizen complaints. The Council gave City Manager Westdyke a strong vote of confidence. ("Camera", August 10, Steve Hoffman)

Mr. Hoffman reported in the November 18 "Camera" that:

The people who run Boulder's city government want to know if citizens think they are doing a good job, and have sent out a 39-question survey to each of 2000 households to get some answers.

A cover letter sent out with the survey by (the) City Manager ... notes that the results will be used in the development of the new ... performance management system.

Personnel Director Mike Patton announced his resignation in November. He was hired by the city in early 1973 to serve as its chief spokesman in labor negotiations

#### ELECTIONS

On November 8, 1977 five Council members were elected from a slate of 17 candidates.

1978

#### GOVERNMENT

#### CITY COUNCIL

At the first meeting of the new Council, Ruth Correll was unanimously elected the first woman mayor of Boulder. Outgoing Mayor Frank Buchanan and Councilwoman Janet Roberts were honored. Neither ran for re-election.

At a meeting in December, the Council honored former Council members including the late Robert V. Potter and former Councilwoman Karen Paget.

The Council section of the chambers was designated in honor of former Councilwoman Janet Roberts.

In July, after five months of debate, residents of Moore's subdivision in north Boulder voted for annexation of 130 acres to the city.

On October 25, prior to the Gunbarrel annexation election, Linda Cornett wrote in the "Camera" that:

The city and seven Gunbarrel developers cemented an agreement on growth control for that area ... .

The agreement recognizes a commitment from the county to five ... developments and exempts them from the constraints of the city's slow growth ordinance.

In return, the developers have agreed to phase their development over the next seven years and to include 15 percent low and moderate income housing.

Two developments (Red Fox Hills and 63rd Street Investors) ... will hold off until Feb. 1 before requesting building permits while the city decides if it will buy the land for parks and open space.

The two tracts are designated as parks and open space on the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan.

The agreement became moot when voters turned down the annexation proposal on November 1.

Linda Cornett reported in the "Camera" dated November 2 that:

Gunbarrel and Heatherwood residents said "No thanks" to annexation to Boulder at the polls Wednesday ... .

In heavy voting, 890 residents opposed annexation and 788 favored joining the city. The 1,678 voters who turned out for the election represented 55 percent of those qualified ... .

(City Manager Bob Westdyke) said the next annexation move will have to be initiated by the Gunbarrel area residents.

Tom Stroop, who opposed an earlier annexation attempt, indicated the residents would never be offered as good a deal again.

In return for an increase in property taxes, the homeowners would have received police and fire protection, street maintenance, human resources programs, parks, and a recreation building and a branch library. The city would have waived the park fee and paid off the remaining indebtedness of the Boulder Valley Water and Sanitation District. Also, the water and

sewer rates, which were one-and-a-half to two times those of in-city homeowners would have dropped to in-city levels.

Some of the pre-election rhetoric included the worries that the developers in the area expressed over being subjected to the city codes, especially the Danish growth limitation ordinance. Councilman Danish threatened a referendum so that the taxpayers already in the city could say whether they wanted to add 2,000 people to their ranks. The political ramifications of annexation probably would have meant it would be necessary to change the City Charter from the present at-large system of electing Council members to a districting system in which they would be elected from wards. Also the area is rated "conservative" which might have meant a shift in Council policy.

The county was disappointed in the defeat of annexation because, under the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan, areas in the county like Gunbarrel which require urban services that the county isn't set up to provide, should be annexed to a city. Gunbarrel land, zoned and approved for development in the county, could be occupied by 18,000 people by 1990.

Jane Cracraft reported in the "Denver Post" on January 4 that Councilman Paul Danish introduced a resolution that led to Council's five to four vote agreeing to set up a committee to look for "outside, expert advice" on growth control. Danish had clashed with City Attorney Wagenhals over interpretation of the Colorado Supreme Court ruling on the Robinson case. Council member George Boland said the action would "disembowel" the city attorney's office.

The Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan is intended to phase development from the edge of the city out into the county. A challenge to that concept

was posed by a request for the extension of a city water line to an area not scheduled for development. The Boulder County Long Range Planning Commission returned the request to the city, strongly urging the city to finalize portions of the Comprehensive Plan including the Utility Master Plan. According to an article by Steve Hoffman in the March 9 "Camera", the missing portions of the Comp Plan included specifications on how it was to work.

The lack of detail has been an increasing source of friction between the city and county legal and planning staffs, and was partly responsible for the City Council's recent decision to hire Boulder attorney Paul Snyder to give it independent advice on tough growth-control issues.

In September, the City Council approved supplemental appropriations of \$1.9 million to provide additional funding for the Senior Citizen's Center, the Gunbarrel fire station, increased overlay of streets and the completion of improvements to the 75th Street wastewater treatment plant.

In November they approved a \$30.5 million city budget for 1979. Major elements include the addition of seven patrol officers to the Police Department, support of the arts including improvements to the Boulder Arts Center gallery, and the addition of a zoning administrator and an environmental control officer.

In January, Council members decided not to amend the city code to permit meetings out of town, but to make do with temporary quarters at the Public Library during the remodeling of the Council chamber.

The City Council decided in March to use surplus city funds to remodel the west and north wings of the Municipal Building. Last year, all available funds were used for remodeling the Council chambers and for the installation of an elevator. The first Council meeting in the "new" chamber was on July 11.

In March, they voted unanimously for a resolution to forbid city expenditures for travel to conventions in states that have not ratified the Equal Rights Amendment.

Linda Cornett wrote in the "Camera" dated May 3 that the Council had squashed plans to drain Coot Lake.

The council's unanimous vote included a provision that the city administration take immediate steps to remedy a problem with parking and traffic on 63rd Street.

Residents of the area ... also asked the city to provide screening for the lake. ... skinny dippers are drawing a crowd of motorized "Looky-Loos" who are in turn creating hazardous driving conditions.

The much-criticized decision (to drain the lake) was based on concerns that the city would be liable for swimming mishaps at the unsupervised area.

And in August there was a drowning and a report from the Health Department which indicated a possible health hazard at the site. Mayor Correll urged the Council to reconsider the Coot Lake question.

In November the city named eight Boulder residents to an Energy Task Force to guide a Community Energy Management Program. Boulder was chosen for one of the federal energy management program grants.

#### CITY MANAGER

In September, a frustrated "Hill" resident smashed an empty wine bottle on the floor of the City Manager's office. Dr. Martin Spector said he had been unable to get the city to act on University Hill's problems. Westdyke and his assistant, Jim Piper held the man until police officers arrived. Dr. Spector filed charges of assault against them, but the District Attorney said those charges were unwarranted.

In October, Mr. Westdyke requested funds to hire a deputy City Manager to supervise administrative services.

The search continued in November for people to fill six vacant positions of city department directors. Nolan Rosall resigned as planning director. Leonard Ehrler left the Parks and Recreation Department in August. Real Estate Director Don Walker left in September and Police Chief David Voorhis' resignation will be effective December 1. Assistant City Manager Piper is looking for a budget director. City Clerk Carl Chapel retired after 23 years with the city. He was clerk for 19 years. David Manzanares, sales tax supervisor, was named to Chapel's position.

#### CITY ATTORNEY

In a "Camera" article dated January 4, Steve Hoffman reported that:

At their first meeting in 1978, the new council voted to select an outside attorney to give them advice on growth control and comprehensive planning. City Attorney Wagenhals objected saying, "It will be next to impossible to work with this system."

Throughout the past year Wagenhals had been responsible for giving advice on the complex legal implications of revisions to the 1970 Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan.

Councilman Danish continually challenged Wagenhal's interpretation of the Robinson decision. The City Attorney held to the idea that the city in some cases must follow the Colorado Supreme Court decision and extend its water and sewer lines even when they will serve areas in the county not scheduled for development under the Comprehensive Plan.

In February, the Council selected Paul Snyder to serve as its special land use advisor.

Jane Cracraft reported in the July 26 "Denver Post" that Walter Wagenhals had resigned after 10 years as City Attorney:

He said difficulties between the Boulder Police Department and the police union weren't a factor in his decision, but the Council's decision to hire an outside land-use attorney had some bearing.

He said he takes particular pride in the mall legislation which he drafted that eventually was adopted by the Colorado legislature.

He said he also is proud of the careful drafting of ordinances. During his 10 years in office, not one was overturned by a court for

vagueness.

"Colorado Daily" writer Stephen Foehr reported on September 6 that Acting City Attorney Steven Barnett had requested that the Council fire land-use attorney Paul Snyder. The Council instead "broadened" Snyder's position to include matters of general legislation.

In December, three finalists for the City Attorney position were interviewed by the City Council. No one was chosen.

#### ELECTIONS

In a May 9 special election, city voters joined those in the county to determine the issue of a county-wide one-half cent sales tax to finance open space acquisitions. The issue was defeated by 23,419 votes against to 15,761 for.

Some charges of irregularities at campus polling places were filed as well as a complaint against the Chamber of Commerce by Councilman Paul Danish relating to campaign expense statements mandated by state election laws. The Chamber "under protest", finally did file a financial statement of its expenditures and the District Attorney decided not to prosecute.

1979

#### GOVERNMENT

#### CITY COUNCIL

After the vote by Gunbarrel residents against annexation to the city, the money earmarked for improvements in that area had been spent on other things. However, in an article in the July 3 "Camera" Sue Deans reported that:

Renewed interest in annexation has surfaced recently because residents fear a subdivision will be built on a proposed park site if the city doesn't buy the land. (Red Fox Hills).

State law prohibits another election involving the same area until a year has passed, (City Manager) Westdyke said. An area may be annexed by petition, without an election, if the consent of holders of 51 percent of the property in the area is obtained.

Sue Deans continued the subject on August 31:

After going through the proposed 1980 budget ... Boulder City Council found the city would not have enough money to pay for increased services until property tax revenues began coming in from Gunbarrel in 1981.

As outlined by City Manager Bob Westdyke, the council's position includes the following points: They are willing to see a vote on annexation. If residents vote to annex, Red Fox Hills will be preserved as park and open space land. Residents must pay a park fee of \$145 per household to pay for half of the park land. City services will not be provided in 1980. The area being considered for annexation should include Heatherwood.

The price of the (park) land has doubled in (the past) year to \$1 million ... .

In a "Camera" article dated September 6, Sue Deans said that "the restrictions the city has placed on the possible annexation of Gunbarrel are making some residents wonder if they really want to annex."

In the "Camera" of September 11, Sharon Massingill reported that the

County Commissioners had unanimously approved initial plans for the controversial Red Fox Hills subdivision ... .

However, the commissioners still hope an annexation election will keep them from being asked to give the development their final OK.

The developer, Bill Carran, had agreed to dedicate 15 acres, or 29 percent of the parcel to open space, 4 percent more than county subdivision regulations require.

"Camera" staff writer Sue Deans wrote on September 29:

Gunbarrel-Heatherwood and Boulder seem to have reached a standoff on the question of annexation.

City officials say residents have to make the first move ... . Residents, however, contend the city should come to them.

City Planner Chris Cares said (the) Heatherwood survey results showed less than half (46 percent) of the residents favored annexation, even if the city improved Heatherwood Park.

Westdyke said it would cost the city \$300,000 a year for services in Gunbarrel.

Mayor Ruth Correll said, "It's not a dead issue — it's just a matter of the city not having enough money to pay for services."

However, in a budget session in October, Council moved the \$75,000

earmarked for the purchase of Red Fox Hills to the City Manager's contingency fund.

The City of Boulder annexed the largest amount of land in Boulder County from 1970 to 1977 -- seven square miles -- according to the United States Bureau of the Census.

The Growth Control ordinance limits growth in the city to two percent a year. Each year the Council must decide whether prior commitments, made before the ordinance was enacted, should be added to that number. In 1979, 151 units based on prior commitments were added to the year's total. Even with them added, the city's growth rate is within the two percent target rate, according to Assistant Planning Director Ed Gawf.

The plan was adopted on November 2, 1976 and went into effect March 10, 1977. In the summer of 1979 it is still a controversial issue. Gloria Bucco wrote in the "Camera" of July 29:

Mayor Ruth Correll said she is not yet ready to assess the plan, but she did admit that some concerns have come to her attention.

"The need for housing, the cost of housing and building materials, implementation of the comprehensive plan, rehabilitation of areas of the city, redevelopment of the commercial core, effects of University decisions, and inflation are all parts of an ongoing assessment."

Sue Deans reported in the May 18 "Camera" that the City Council's late night action ordering its closing had resulted in the Friends of Coot Lake taking the city to court to prevent its draining.

Acting City Attorney Steve Barnett and a private attorney representing the "Friends" reached a temporary agreement. Barnett said the city agreed to stop digging a drainage ditch because "the Division of Wildlife requested ... a delay to get the fish out."

At the Council meeting of July 17, policies were established that, hopefully, would satisfy both the neighbors and the bathers, nude or otherwise, of Coot Lake.

In January, the Boulder County Consortium of Cities voted to study the

impacts of withdrawal from the Denver Regional Council of Governments (DRCOG). Federal regulations require that some agency act as the "designated clearing house" for federal money. If Boulder County withdrew, it would have to join another agency, form its own, or contract with DRCOG in order to receive the grants. Boulder, after hearing a report from the firm of Briscoe, Maphis, Murray and Lamont, Inc. on the alternatives, decided to stay with DRCOG for the present.

In January, the Council and city staff met for two days to establish goals for the city and to determine how to allocate a \$750,000 budget surplus. The money was originally intended to pay for improvements in the Gunbarrel area. In the "Camera" dated July 2, Sue Deans reported that:

According to Deputy City Manager Dave Knapp, about \$400,000 of that money is unspent and is in the city's reserve fund. The remainder established a day care training center, built four soccer fields, (and) was used as a loan to finance the Boulder Urban Renewal Authority (which will repay the money).

In June, Boulder's City Council decided not to put a proposed \$15 million bond issue on the November ballot. Included were performing arts facilities, recreation facilities and a forest management program to curb the pine beetle.

The arts proposal included a new building housing three small theaters (\$4.5 million), a \$1.6 million renovation of the Boulder Theater, and a \$300,000 renovation of the Boulder Arts Center. The consensus seemed to be that money would be allocated for initial planning, but the vote would have to wait. ("Camera", June 27, Sue Deans)

The 1980 proposed budget was released at the end of July and called for revenues and expenditures of \$34 million.

In August, Councilman Greg Lefferdink urged that the city eliminate its two percent tax on food. The sales tax on food is expected to bring in

\$1.8 million in 1980. The 1980 budget allocates \$360,000 of that to the open space fund, \$540,000 to transportation improvements, and \$900,000 to the general fund. ("Camera", August 7, Sue Deans)

In October, the Council decided not to eliminate the food tax.

The Council recommended that the county approve more industrial revenue or economic development bonds in 1979. Included were those for expansion of NBI Corporation and for M-M Boulder Associates, developer of La Belles store.

Early in the year, Governor Lamm had suggested phasing out the School of Education at the University of Colorado. In a report prepared for the Colorado Commission on Higher Education, it was stated that such a move would cost the city at least \$9 million in economic activity. This figure is based on a 1974 study which, adjusted for inflation, showed the average student spends \$3,453.75 a year in Boulder while the average faculty member spends \$18,721. ("Camera", February 23, Sue Deans)

#### CITY MANAGER

Linda Cornett reported in the January 3 "Camera" that:

Boulder's City Council gave City Manager Bob Westdyke an "A+" in most areas of his job and a \$3000 a year raise Tuesday night.

Westdyke won high marks from the council members in the areas of responsiveness to the public and to the council, of imagination and creativity, honesty and not "playing games" with his nine bosses.

There was criticism.

Council members said they are tired of vacancies in key administrative posts ... . Westdyke wasted too much time on lengthy Police Department hearings, council members said, and allowed himself to be overworked.

David W. Knapp, assistant director of financial management for San Diego, became Boulder's deputy City Manager in February.

Jane Cracraft reported in the "Denver Post" dated May 16 that:

It took three years to do it, but City Manager Bob Westdyke now has a full staff of department heads -- and eight out of 10 of them are persons he hired.

The only carryovers ... are Marcelee Gralapp, director of libraries, and Melba Shepard, director of Human Resources.

In July, Boulder sponsored a civic awards program for steps being taken in the Boulder Valley to conserve energy. Those programs, projects or lifestyles which are judged by the Energy Task Force (a citizen advisory group to the Comprehensive Community Energy Management Program) to be significant in their energy savings or which take an innovative approach to energy conservation will be recognized in an awards presentation at the Boulder Community Appropriate Technology Fair on August 18. ("Camera, July 7)

In August, the Boulder Municipal Employees Association voted to accept a new two-year contract with the city.

Marc Lerner reported in the "Camera" of December 17 that:

The City of Boulder got into the data processing business five years ago.

The city has invested more than \$500,000 in computers and has upgraded its equipment since the initial purchase of two Burroughs models.
