Charges for ... (data processing) ... services ... (are made to each department). The Fire Department is one example of how a city agency puts data processing to work.

Last April, the department switched from a filing system to a computer to record, analyze and update fire inspections of more than 2,300 Boulder addresses. ... More than 10,000 inspections are carried out each year. (Cliff Harvey, chief fire marshall said): "It's a tough system to keep track of ... but the computer won't let you forget."

"We handle things that might otherwise take thousands of hours if they were handled by staff," said Marty Grisham, lead systems analyst for the city's data processing division.

#### CITY ATTORNEY

Linda Cornett reported in the "Camera" of January 3 that the Council "was evenly split on whether to select Acting City Attorney Steve Barnett

as attorney or to search for more experienced candidates." They later decided to continue the search.

The "Camera" reported on August 22 that Joseph de Raismes, a member of the Colorado Attorney General's staff was offered the city attorney's post at a Council meeting July 17 and had now accepted the position. His delay in the decision was evidently due to the five to four vote in his favor. Mr. de Raismes wanted to clarify why four Council members had voted "no". The new appointee said he would be unable to begin the new job until November 1 because of pending court cases.

In October, Acting City Attorney Steve Barnett announced his resignation.

#### ELECTIONS

Two elections were held in the city in 1979. The first, on June 12, was on a Charter amendment to provide financing for the Boulder Urban Renewal Authority to develop the Crossroads Shopping Center area.

According to a "Camera" article by Sue Deans on May 17, "an unlikely amalgamation of about 10 local groups that have usually been on opposite sides in political issues ... " formed the Coalition for Boulder's Future "to urge a yes vote in the ... municipal election."

On June 11, Judge Richard Dana dismissed a court action filed by five Boulder citizens who attempted to cancel or delay the June 12 shopping center vote.

On election day, an error was found in a precinct map that may have affected as many as 1,000 voters.

The Charter amendment was approved by a vote of 6,442 to 5,754, a margin of 688 votes.

On July 6, a suit was filed in Federal District Court asking that the

June 12th election be declared null and void. The city asked dismissal of the suit in August.

On November 6, 1979, voters chose five Council members from a slate of ten candidates, and gave their approval to a Charter amendment exception to the "Blue Line" which allowed city water service to a resident of the Canon Park subdivision at the mouth of Boulder Canyon.





## CHAPTER II

### HEALTH AND SANITATION

1975

### WATER SUPPLY AND SEWAGE DISPOSAL

City Council voted unanimously to approve a revocable water permit for the State Highway Department garage on Lee Hill Road. They also authorized the City Manager to provide emergency water service to residents of the Wagoner Water District, an area east of the city that has been plagued by water shortages and by recent breakdowns in pumping equipment. ("Camera", January 8, Ken Frizell)

Mr. Frizell reported in the "Camera" of February 19 that the Council authorized the mayor to sign a contract between the city and the Northern Colorado Water Conservancy District which would allow an increase the storage capacity of Boulder Reservoir. The city is proposing to construct a hardened spillway which would increase the water storage capacity by 7,000 acre feet. The storage space will be needed in the future for Big Thompson and possible Six Cities water.

The water treatment plant on Betasso Hill currently treats about 18 million gallons of water daily, though during the summer that figure can run as high as 35 million gallons. It is being remodeled to boost its capacity to 50 million gallons daily. ("Camera", April 8)

The City Council voted to authorize the mayor to execute a petition for 80 units of the Six Cities Project. Boulder's share of the costs is expected to be about \$4 million. Kenneth Wright was the only member to vote

against the motion saying that the city does not have accurate estimates of the cost and that the plan should not be approved before completion of an environmental impact statement. ("Camera", May 21, Ken Frizell)

Six northern Colorado municipalities were awarded a total of 480 units of water to be brought from the western slope by the Windy Gap project in action taken by directors of the Municipal Subdistrict of the Northern Colorado Water Conservancy District. Each unit is the equivalent of 100 acre-feet of water at delivery. ("Camera", July 13)

The Platte River Power Authority (PRPA) now owns one-third of the Six Cities water. Fort Collins transferred its one-sixth share to PRPA and Loveland and Estes Park transferred half their shares.

Don Douglas, Boulder's water utility director, said that the city's 1975 assessment as a member of Six Cities was \$40,000 and the 1976 payment would be more than \$98,000. ("Camera", September 8, Ken Frizell)

The Fred C. Sproul Land Company owns a water diversion project that could bring enough water from the Western Slope to serve an additional 250,000 persons in Boulder County ... .

The Four Counties Water Diversion Project includes conditional water rights ... and the necessary ditches, reservoirs and tunnel to bring the water across the Continental Divide into Boulder County.

The project calls for the water to be brought to the Eastern Slope through the ... Adams Tunnel, or the proposed Arapahoe Ditch and Tunnel system at the Fourth of July mine south of Arapahoe Peak ... .

When the Boulder City Council was considering purchase of the project in 1951 and 1952, the estimated development cost was \$25 million.

Any development of the Four Counties project would ... face opposition ... (from various groups and agencies) ... and possible difficulties in obtaining permission for the various ditches, reservoirs, pipelines and tunnels required for the project. ("Camera", October 19, John Leach.)

Canyon Associates are continuing their quest for approval of a sewage treatment plant on their lot at Third and Canyon so they may construct an office building.

The County Health Board approved a package sewer system for the

property, but final approval has to come from the State Water Pollution Control Commission since the plant would be on the banks of Boulder Creek. ("Town & Country", January 22)

The County Planning Board voted unanimously to recommend denial of a special use permit at the Third and Canyon site.

Boulder will not serve utilities unless it is annexed. Canyon Associates are reluctant to annex since there is no guarantee the land will be zoned for the use they want. ("Camera", April 17, Tony Stroh)

The city's sewage treatment plant operation is running at a deficit, but is meeting current state water pollution standards.

Ernie Hamilton, wastewater treatment director, said that secondary treatment has been moved from the old East Pearl plant to the one at 75th Street, and that a surcharge on the large dumpers -- such as Arapahoe Chemicals and Watts Hardy Dairy -- has brought a sharp decrease in the amount of their effluent. He reported that an experimental farming project near the sludge dump at Marshall will be used as a pilot project. Hamilton added that a new contract with Urban Waste Resources should alleviate the complaints about sludge disposal at the Marshall landfill. ("Town & Country", May 28)

The City Council, in a morning session, passed a resolution showing its intent to improve the quality of Boulder Creek water so that it could support fish. The meeting was held at the urging of Ruth Wright, a member of the Water Quality Control Commission. If Council had not acted and if the commission had not adjusted a proposed rule change to allow cities intending to improve stream quality to keep their present rank, Boulder could have lost its share of federal sewage treatment money. ("Camera",

June 18, Bill Jordan)

Timothy Lange wrote in the "Colorado Daily" dated July 9 about the experimental farm next to the East Pearl sewage treatment plant.

"Injecting the sludge as fertilizer into the land is a joint project of the city, Colorado State University and the National Science Foundation and has been going on for two years." Sam Maphis, of Briscoe-Maphis, Inc., a Boulder consulting and engineering firm, said it is the environmental hazards associated with dumping sludge in the ocean, the rising fuel costs associated with drying and burning it, and the landfill costs of burying it that have increased interest in sludge application.

During the summer, the City Council was struggling with the alternatives suggested by two engineering consulting firms on the best way to meet federal water pollution control criteria by 1978 and again in 1985. The two studies of future sewage plant needs cost the city \$250,000. ("Town & Country", July 16)

Bill Jordan reported in the "Camera" dated September 21:

Two respected consulting firms in the field of wastewater treatment were asked in 1973 to come up with their ideas for more than just a new treatment plant. The city said it wanted to be a leader in cleaning up its wastes.

The two firms reported nine suggested alternatives.

Boulder is about to move on upgrading its (waste) water treatment. But it isn't following any of the nine recommendations.

In one way, the move to upgrade wastewater treatment had its roots in federal regulations. Stricter water ... (standards) ... for 1985 mean the city has to do some upgrading.

But there was another form of pressure ... . ... environmentalists ... were interested in land application of wastewater ... (and wanted) ... to hire ... a consulting firm that was already building up a reputation in the field of land application.

Bob Westdyke, now assistant city manager and then public works director, recommended that the city hire not one but two consultants ...

When the reports came back in the spring of 1974 ... Doug Smith (sewer utility director) ... said (they) had a sort of value in that they let City Council know instantly that most of the pace-setting treatment plans were out of Boulder's financial ballpark.

In November the Boulder County Commissioners urged the city to withdraw or delay a federal funding application for the wastewater

treatment plant expansion. The county did not know the application had been submitted until notified by the Denver Regional Council of Governments. They indicated that since the county would have to issue a Special Use Permit, they felt it would be "beneficial for the administration of the City to hold some type of public hearings to allow the citizens to comment on the final document that has been prepared." ("Town & Country", November 12)

Ken Frizell reported in the "Camera" dated November 19:

The city administration's multimillion dollar proposal for upgrading Boulder's sewage treatment facility was dealt a setback Tuesday night when the Boulder City Council moved to delay further consideration of the plan.

The plan, the result of more than two years of work, proposed that the city meet water quality standards for Boulder Creek by either installing \$5.2 million in filtering and polishing ponds to supplement the existing 75th Street treatment plant or by constructing an \$11 million system of infiltration-percolation basins.

Both alternatives included an additional plan for a \$2.3 million system of sludge injection.

Criticism came from property owners in the area of the 75th Street plant, and from representatives of PLAN-Boulder, the League of Women Voters and the Heatherwood Homeowners Association who cited what were termed inconsistencies in the plan and who urged Council to delay further consideration.

Despite reassurances by City Manager Archie Twitchell and members of the city's Public Facilities Department, council voted 5 to 3 to reject a motion by Councilman Robert Trenka to approve the plan.

#### REFUSE DISPOSAL

The County Commissioners approved a rate increase for Urban Waste Resources, operator of the county landfill. ("Camera", April 29, Tony Stroh)

The City Council voted to approve a motion to spend \$6,700 for a study on resource recovery from solid wastes. ("Camera", May 21, Ken Frizell)

#### AIR POLLUTION

A new federal air pollution regulation went into effect on January 1. It aimed at indirect sources of air pollution such as large shopping centers, parking lots, airports and new highways. The new rules provided that construction plans and air quality impacts of projects of a certain size and location must be reviewed by the United States Environmental Protection Agency or an authorized state or local agency.

In March, the City Council unanimously approved the ordinance prohibiting smoking on any public transit, in elevators, theaters, retail food establishments and elsewhere. It encourages restaurateurs to segregate smokers from non-smokers and provides for segregation in hospitals and in the main public library.

1976

### WATER SUPPLY AND SEWAGE DISPOSAL

The target date of 1980 was set by engineers for the proposed completion of the Windy Gap Project. A dam no more than 25 feet high, but 3,375 feet long, on the Fraser River would produce the storage area. A huge pumping station, mostly underground, would transfer the water eight miles to Lake Granby through buried pipelines. It would then be transferred through the Colorado-Big Thompson Project facilities to the Eastern Slope.

Ken Frizell wrote in the April 25th "Camera" that:

If Boulder follows the advice of its water consultant, Black and Veatch, it could spend more than \$56.6 million over the next 20 or more years to supply water for a population of up to 205,000.

years to supply water for a population of up to 205,000.

That kind of money and that many people, when viewed in the context of the recent Colorado Supreme Court decision taking away the city's control of water and sewer service outside the city limits, is making a few Boulderites more than a little nervous.

The city has received complaints about odors emanating from the 75th Street sewage plant. Not much can be done until the plant is upgraded. Money to pay for the improvements may be another problem since federal funding is down and Boulder will have to improve its standing on a state priority list before it receives an allocation.

Doug Smith, sewer utility director, said the city will ask the State Water Quality Control Commission to upgrade the rating the state has given Boulder Creek. It is state policy to funnel money for sewage treatment improvement improvements to those areas which already have relatively high quality streams.

The state's philosophy is that the better streams should be maintained rather than spending money to upgrade already severely polluted streams. Hence the need for Boulder to improve its rating. ("Camera", March 30, Ken Frizell)

The Sombrero Ranch development located east of Cherryvale Road between Arapahoe Avenue and Baseline is on county land and its site plan is subject to county approval. In order to put 138 houses on the 62-acre parcel, the developer had to have city water and sewer service. The Planning Board went against the advice of Nolan Rosall last August and recommended against the project. The City Council denied the request for utilities. ("Camera", March 3, Ken Frizell)

At the August 3 Council meeting, Sombrero Ranch, Larry Robinson and William Unkefer all received permission to hook up to city utilities. These changes in policy were dictated by the Supreme Court's decision in

the Robinson case.

Jane Cracraft reported in the "Denver Post" dated December 19:

Environmentalists Ruth and Ken Wright of Boulder have discovered that it isn't easy to combine professional interests and public service.

Both have been quick to volunteer their time in public office, but last month they were accused of a conflict of interest in water-quality matters.

They were cleared a week ago, though, in a seven-page report by Kay McEver, attorney to the Larimer-Weld Regional Council of Governments.

#### REFUSE DISPOSAL

A study on solid wastes was being prepared for the Denver Regional Council of Governments. It was not available at the time a bill was introduced to the state legislature proposing a metro solid waste recovery district. The district, which would be subject to taxes to pay for a recycling project, was suggested as an environmentally sound way in which to cope with the metro area's solid waste problem. ("Camera", February 18)

The study found that a regional solid waste recovery system was economically unfeasible, largely because of the costs involved in hauling garbage around the metro area.

The plan recommends recycling programs and suggests city and county encouragement for those activities. The statement appears to recognize the Boulder Eco-Cycle Project which began earlier this year. ("Town & Country", October 20)

In December, City Manager Bob Westdyke recommended that funding requested by Eco-Cycle be turned down because of a lack of city control plus the possibility of city liability. On a five to four vote, Council took his recommendation. ("Town & Country" January 19, 1977)

Eco-Cycle began operating on a six-month, temporary basis July 1. It is a non-profit, volunteer-run recycling business. The county donated a

lot at 26th and Spruce streets for the center, and the University Environmental Board donated a phone. ("Town & Country", July 7)

#### AIR POLLUTION

A "Camera" article on February 22 by Caryl Buckstein said that Tom Le Mire of the Boulder County Health Department was not happy about the plan to put a parking lot for the Justice Center at Sixth Street and Canyon Boulevard. The land around the area is radioactive in spots because it was the site of processing factories for some of the old mines above Boulder. However, the State Health Department determined the radiation levels were low and not dangerous.

1977

### WATER SUPPLY AND SEWAGE DISPOSAL

Steven Hoffman reported in the "Camera" of February 27:

The City of Boulder's policy of maintaining a minimum flow of water through Boulder Creek, put into effect in 1975 at the urging of the local chapter of Trout Unlimited, has been suspended by City Manager Bob Westdyke.

According to Water Utility Director Don Douglas, the flow has been supplemented so far this winter under drought conditions through exchange with private ditch companies.

"With drought conditions existing, it is my recommendation that no water be released until the reservoirs are back to normal," Douglas said.

The past president of the Boulder chapter of Trout Unlimited ... and some 200 members ... would like to see ... a channel in the middle of Boulder Creek so when the flow drops, fish will have pools of water deep enough to survive.

The \$2.4 million Betasso Plant expansion to 50 million gallons per day capacity was listed at 98% complete in the "Town & Country" dated June 29.

In November, the Council approved an ordinance granting a water permit to the Fettig property in north Boulder.

Steve Hoffman reported in the "Camera" dated August 31 that after months of feuding, Boulder will "go along with the county on a method for improving sewage treatment" at the 75th Street plant.

The method -- although it is costly and may result in higher sewer rates -- will virtually eliminate the stench that has raised hordes of protest from the Gunbarrel area in recent years.

It can also be built within the boundaries of the present 75th Street site.

In the "Camera" of September 21, Mr. Hoffman reported:

Sewage will be decomposed inside covered tanks, so the smell will not escape.

The sludge from the tanks will be injected into city-owned farmland near the plant.

Paul Danish recommended that the city keep the present method of disposing of sludge by drying, then burying it. His was the only vote against the injection method.

At a hearing on the controversial plan promoted by the United States Environmental Protection Agency to expand the 75th Street Wastewater Treatment Plant, citizens raised objections to the sludge injection method because of possible groundwater contamination, especially since the site is in the floodplain. Some said it is not energy efficient, others said its \$7 million cost is too high even though the federal government will pay 75 per cent for construction. ("Camera", October 4, Jane Hoback)

#### REFUSE DISPOSAL

Steve Hoffman reported in the "Camera" on January 19:

Boulder City Council approved a revised funding request from Eco-Cycle and passed a policy statement ... reaffirming its commitment to recycling.

Council's actions will give Eco-Cycle more than enough in-kind services to qualify for a \$59,000 Environmental Protection Agency grant if the recycling group gets funding commitments from the state and county governments.

Eco-Cycle was awarded a \$55,000 federal grant for their recycling

program. The EPA set a goal of 50 per cent participation, and Eco-Cycle said they would do 15 per cent better than that according to co-director Pete Grogan. ("Town & Country", August 24)

#### AIR POLLUTION

Comput-A-Ride is Boulder's computerized CarPool system which could answer the community's need for reduced costs in automobile operation and parking, for reduced rush-hour congestion, smog and air pollution. ("Town & Country, August 24)

Automobile trips now number 475,000 trips per day in the Boulder Valley, or about two million miles driven every day. That has increased by 50,000 trips in the past several months and will increase by another 16,000 trips per day this year if the city's growth rate is held at two percent. ("Colorado Daily", October 27)

Jim Adams of Boulder's Environmental Protection Office attributes 90 per cent of all air pollution to the internal combustion engine. Boulder is one of five Colorado cities which is endangering public health by excess auto pollution according to an Environmental Protection Agency report released in June.

Susan Eastman reported in the September 14 "Town & Country" that Tom Le Mire of the Boulder County Health Department stated that 121,000 cars and trucks were tallied in the last count for Boulder. The pollution generated by these cars is measured by a monitor on top of the County Courthouse and it periodically exceeds the safe levels set by the federal government.

The city reported no court cases involving the "no smoking" ordinance since it was passed.

### WATER SUPPLY AND SEWAGE DISPOSAL

Steve Hoffman reported in the "Camera" of February 2:

A request for municipal water service by the owners of a piece of property just north of the city limits has been dropped, ending six months of wrangling over the growth control policies of the revised Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan.

... Karl A. Fettig said he no longer wishes to pursue his plan to extend city water lines to his warehouse ... northwest of the intersection of Broadway and Lee Hill Road.

His property lies in the area where the city acts as a water utility, but is "out of phase" with the (Comprehensive) plan. ... uses on the Fettig property remain under the control of county building regulations. (County land Use Director Ed) Tepe said that Fettig's plans do not include uses that would require a sprinkling system and thus a tap on to city water lines, as was thought to be the case earlier.

Linda Cornett gave an account of the sewage treatment plant operations in the May 14th "Camera". She said that "when construction began on the 75th Street plant in 1968, a smaller plant on East Pearl Street was on the edge of town." The new plant was five miles farther east, which perhaps was not far enough.

Each day an average of 10 million gallons of waste water pours from the drains of Boulder Valley into a 15-acre treatment plant ... . And each day about 10 million gallons of almost drinkable water is funneled out of the plant into Boulder Creek.

A secondary treatment system with trickling filters was built into the 75th Street plant. These filters prompted complaints about odors from nearby residents.

In an effort to meet tougher water quality requirements, the city is planning a third level of treatment ... .

The activated sludge treatment ... would probably add another \$2,500 a month to the plant's \$5,000 energy bill.

A directive from the Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) ordered another look at land treatment for sludge disposal for the Boulder plant, further delaying a decision. The land treatment system would cost \$9.9

million and the mechanical system \$10.3 million. However, the EPA will pay 85 percent of the former but only 75 percent of the latter.

Linda Cornett reported in the "Camera" on December 28 that the EPA has agreed that "the mechanical treatment system favored by the city and county more than a year ago is the best compromise for Boulder" in an environmental impact statement now at city hall.

The impact statement is a required step before the city can apply for federal money to design and construct the \$12 million to \$14.7 million activated sludge system. The project was estimated to cost \$10 million in 1977 but inflation has pushed the total up by almost \$100,000 a month.

#### AIR POLLUTION

The Environmental Protection Agency released a survey in February that showed the air over Boulder was dirtier than the law permits for photochemical oxidants and carbon monoxide.

A report in June to the Colorado Air Pollution Control Commission listed actions implemented by the City and County of Boulder to take the lead in air pollution control planning. Among these are a carpool location service provided by the city, 12.98 miles of bikeways and bikepaths built, more streets and alleys paved, efforts undertaken to upgrade and synchronize traffic signals, and implementation of the Comprehensive Plan which has set a precedent in pollution control measures by limiting the outward growth of Boulder.

In October, Public Works Director Andy Hollar sent a memo to the city manager outlining a plan to clean up Boulder's air by 1982 and thus avoid a loss of federal funds. The federal government has requested each state to submit a compliance plan to the Environmental Protection Agency by the end of this year detailing how it will meet the new standards. Boulder's plan recommends a voluntary no-drive day, city support of RTD's goal of doubling

ridership, and providing incentives for city employees to carpool.  
("Colorado Daily", October 18, Allen Houston)

In December, the City Council requested that Boulder be considered separately from the rest of the metro area when the federal government decides which local government is trying to meet air pollution control standards. Some local projects which may be endangered by a cut in federal funds if standards are not met are 47th Street, expansions to the sewage treatment facility, and even some flood control projects. ("Camera", December 6, Linda Cornett and Todd Malmsbury)

1979

### WATER SUPPLY AND SEWAGE DISPOSAL

Linda Cornett reported in the "Camera" of February 5:

The Six Cities project was first proposed nine years ago but a series of roadblocks -- opposition from Western Slope residents, questions about the (growth effects) of the extra water -- have slowed progress to a crawl.

Environmentalists, concerned about the effects of the project, have argued for years that the (environmental impact) statement should have come first, before any commitments were made ... .

(Kenneth) Wright was the only Boulder City Council member to vote against ... the Windy Gap project back in 1975. (One of his concerns is) ... the increase in salts in the (downslope) water ... .

In the "Camera" of June 21, John Leach reported that the Six Cities water project has overcome one more obstacle. The Bureau of Reclamation had stalled the delivery of an environmental impact statement to the Environmental Protection Agency. Deputy Mayor Robert White had to lobby in Washington "and scream a lot" before the report got unstuck.

Thad Keyes reported on September 18 in the "Camera" that the Colorado Supreme Court has ruled "that a lower court erred last year in awarding water rights to Windy Gap ... ."

In the works for more than a decade, the \$35 million project is considered a key building block in Boulder's and Longmont's future water plans. Boulder plans on using its share in 1995, and Longmont looks toward the late 1980s or early 1990s.

Experts hope that by supplying extra testimony, the project will be put back on track. However, retracing court steps will cause further delay.

The City Council approved utility rate increases in March.

The increase in the sewer bill, almost doubling the cost for most homeowners, will provide 40 percent more revenues for the sewer utility to help catch up with operating expenses ... . A portion of the money will also be used to pay the city's share of improvements at the 75th Street plant ... . The city ... has determined not to go into debt to pay off the project.

Charges in water rates won't bring that utility any more money, but will level out uneven rates which have been charged to homeowners and industrial customers. For years, (Utility Director Doug) Smith said, homeowners "have been getting a break" on their water.

The out-of-city rate was lowered, Smith said, because it appeared unlikely the city could defend the double rates if a county resident took the issue to court. ("Camera", March 31, Linda Cornett)

Doug Smith resigned as utilities director in June. Roger Hartman who has been acting director, was chosen director in September over 50 other candidates. ("Camera", September 8)

In December, the Council granted an out-of-city water permit to Fred Krafts of Canon Park. In November, voters approved an exception to the Blue Line allowing the Krafts access to the water. Eight of the nine houses in Canon Park have city water under the "grandfather" clause in the "Blue Line" ordinance. The Kraft house was built after the others so was not included. Wells on the property had proved inadequate. ("Camera", September 19, December 5, Sue Deans)

Andy Hollar, city public works director, sent a memorandum to City Manager Bob Westdyke and the City Council, saying that the EPA approval of

the sewage treatment plan the city was ready to adopt more than a year ago was received August 14. "The method that was approved," wrote Hollar, "is the same system that was recommended in the Draft Environmental Impact Statement in October of 1977." ("Camera", August 20, Ken Frizell)

#### REFUSE DISPOSAL

Boulder's faltering Eco-Cycle got a hefty shot of capital ... in the form of a \$140,000 appropriation from City Council.

Eco-Cycle director Pete Grogan told the council that Coors had donated a baling machine worth \$48,000 ... that would replace the "machine now being used."

That machine would "dramatically improve" the organization's ability to recycle cardboard ... .

The money from the city would be used to build a concrete slab for the baler, enclose it as much as possible and wire it. ("Camera", August 31, Sue Deans)

#### AIR POLLUTION

In the "Camera" of February 9, Todd Malmsbury wrote:

In a move that surprised the Colorado Air Pollution Control Commission, Boulder city officials said ... that they don't want to be separated from the Denver air quality region after all.

(City Manager Bob) Westdyke said that instead of being separated from Denver, "we ask only for separate recognition" for the steps the city has already taken to improve air quality.





## CHAPTER III

### TRAFFIC ENGINEERING AND TRANSPORTATION

1975

### HIGHWAYS AND BYWAYS

Although every transportation problem is a concern to the Boulder Transportation Department, the emphasis in 1975 will be on solving today's problems and the needs of the immediate future.

Barbara Morrison, Director of Transportation said, "People want good roads, but they want something else."

They want a good mass transit system, a safe place to walk or ride their bikes. They want good land use and the chance to reach their destination with minimum traffic.

Morrison said that areas of focus in 1975 would be on pedestrians, bicyclists, major street programs (Pearl Street and Broadway), the computer system and street maintenance. ("Camera", January 26, Ruth Duncan)

In January the City Council approved scrapping a one-way couplet plan for Pearl and Spruce streets in favor of widening Pearl Street. The widening was the only realistic plan because it did not involve the relocation of people. ("Town & Country", January 22)

Barbara Gigone reported in the "Town & Country" dated February 12:

The old bugaboo, Folsom Avenue through the University campus, returned to haunt the City Council ... .

The University planning staff said a survey showed that a total of 16,000 pedestrian crossings were made on Folsom during the space of ... 10 hours.

(City Transportation Director Barbara) Morrison said there were two possibilities for immediate action on Folsom ... .

(One) ... would be to immediately close Folsom and move that traffic to Regent Drive ... .

(Councilwoman Janet Roberts) ... recalled that former city manager E. Robert Turner proposed that seemingly simple solution about 15 years ago.

Ms. Roberts ... said she had "never received as many citizen complaints" as she did after that suggestion.

The City Council gave the transportation department permission to design a median strip with pedestrian "refuge" islands and crosswalks on Folsom between Regent Drive and Colorado Avenue.

The median strip is only a three-to-five-year "interim" solution, and most observers expect the final solution to be a \$5 million, four-lane, depressed street exiting on Broadway. ("Colorado Daily", April 2)

A student demonstration closed Folsom Street to traffic for four days. The students protested an announcement that the city could not construct safety medians until December because of lack of funds. By Friday night Boulder officials had agreed to post a new 20 mph speed limit, to paint zebra striping on the street and place stop signs at pedestrian crossings. ("Camera", April 6, John Leach)

The university owns about one-third of the two-block section closest to Regent Drive, a hold-over from the 1950s when Folsom Street (then 24th) was outside city limits and the county commissioners gave the street section to the university.

For that reason, the university could actually balk any action which it didn't favor for the city street, but the regent committee members agreed they prefer to avoid confrontation with the city. ("Camera", April 12, Linda Cornett)

Another controversial local traffic engineering project is the Foothills Parkway, better known as the 47th Street Project.

The last time the parkway was in the news, an environmental impact statement had just been sent to the Department of Transportation (DOT)

\*\*\*

The DOT approved the statement on Jan. 7 and sent it to the Council on Environmental Quality (CEQ).

CEQ had 30 days in which to comment on the statement.

The 30 days expired on Feb. 7 with no word from the CEQ.

That apparently gave the highway department the green light to proceed with the design phase.

However, the state has no funds to cover the design work, and if federal money does come in, it will probably be spent on interstate highway projects which need only 10 per cent matching funds as opposed to the 30 per cent needed for state projects such as 47th Street.

The design phase would take from six to nine months and then the plan would be subject to a public hearing. The state would next need money to purchase right-of-way. It could be tied up for months in condemnation proceedings. The district engineer said it could be several years before the parkway is completed.

"The parkway became an issue when area residents voiced fears it would eventually become part the the I-470 loop around Denver." ("Camera", February 19, Ken Frizell)

Both the Federal Highway Commission and the Council on Environmental Quality approved the impact statement. However, the Environmental Protection Agency requested that new studies be conducted into noise and air pollution levels which might be expected on completion of the highway.

At a Council meeting in March, Barbara Morrison suggested allocating city funds to assist in building the road. ("Camera", March 19, Ken Frizell)

The Boulder City Council voted 6 to 3 ... to commit \$100,000 in city funds to the construction of the bitterly controversial Foothills Parkway (47th Street).

... the money could be used to help design the five-mile parkway, only one mile of which has been completed. ("Camera", April 16, Ken Frizell)

Mr. Frizell reported in the "Camera" of April 26 that Mayor Penfield Tate had responded to Governor Lamm's criticism that the city's offer was an "unhealthy" influence on the State Highway Department. The mayor said that the completion of 47th Street was in the "best interests of the citizens of Boulder" and the offer of additional local funding was not considered an improper action.

Boulder County requested top priority for state funds for design, right-of-way acquisition and construction of Foothills Parkway in their State Highway recommendations for 1976-1977. ("Camera", September 21, John

Leach)

The controversial 47th Street link between the Denver-Boulder Turnpike and Colo. 93, deleted two years ago from the ... 47th Street plan because of citizen protests, has popped up again.

The link, which was dropped for fear that it would create a through route for regional automobile traffic, has been discussed again by members of the city's Transportation Department ... .

The road has also been a subject at recent discussions of changes in the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan.

Although the city's latest version of the comprehensive plan does not provide for the link, John Aldridge, a city transportation planner, would like to see the right-of-way protected, even if it means only drawing a dotted line on the maps. ("Camera", September 29, Ken Frizell)

The 28th and 30th streets one-way couplet, planned for construction in 1975, has been delayed by a lawsuit filed by the city against Sherrelwood. The suit is to clear up the number of units which would be allowed on the property if it were developed, thus determining the price of the property. Also under scrutiny is the desired street alignment which is within the floodway on the recent flood maps of Boulder Creek. ("Town & Country", May 21)

In June, the City Council voted five to four to approve a resolution supporting the Denver Regional Council of Government's position that the \$200 million in federal funds allocated to I-470 should not be lost if the road is not constructed. Governor Richard Lamm recently vetoed the project and suggested the money could be better spent on transportation. ("Camera", June 4)

A study, performed for the Transportation Department indicated that the computerized traffic control system can be made to work. The traffic lights on Broadway and 28th Street are at present manually set. ("Camera", June 26, Ken Frizell)

Two one-way streets, 13th and 14th, had their north-south directions reversed in November. Transportation Director Barbara Morrison said that 30 additional parking spaces for the mall will be gained through the action. ("Town & Country", November 29)

### PEDESTRIANS AND BIKEWAYS

Some of the needs of the immediate future relate to bikepaths according to Transportation Director Morrison. She hopes to establish major north-south and east-west arteries for bikes and to implement an intense bike regulation education program. ("Camera", January 26, Ruth Duncan)

The Red Zinger Bicycle Classic, a series of bike races expected to draw some of the nation's top amateur bicycling competitors, will be held in Boulder June 21 and 22.

A rally and a parade open to anyone with a bike is scheduled.

There will also be a clean-up event for children after the criterium around North Boulder park. American Youth Hostels is putting up a prize for the team which gathers the most trash after the races. ("Camera", June 12)

Jerry Ruhl wrote in the "Colorado Daily" dated July 16:

Over a year ago the city began work on a bike code that was to spell out rules and regulations regarding two-wheel travel in Boulder. In the ensuing months, the machinery involved in drawing up the code was first stalled and then brought to a standstill. City planners are now aiming for December for the completion of a bikeways master plan.

In December, South Broadway was closed so a pedestrian and bicycle underpass could be built at the Viele Lake channel. ("Town & Country", November 29)

#### PARKING

In March, the Council approved a settlement in the condemnation lawsuit involving Der Wienerschnitzel, a hot dog establishment at 1415 Canyon Boulevard. The property is owned by the Denver-Boulder Bus Company. The land will be used by the Central Area General Improvement District (CAGID) for additional downtown parking facilities. ("Town & Country", March 26)

Barry Koltnow reported in the "Camera" of May 4 that the parking control function has been moved from the Police Department to the Transportation Department. In 1974, \$133,597 in revenues were collected from the city's parking meters.

In July, a member of the Parking District Board said they plan to begin construction soon on a 50 space parking lot at 1415 Canyon. The district is also taking over the 14th Street parking lot from the city for employee parking. Other possible parking sites are being studied. ("Camera", July 1, Beverly Butman)

Acting as the Board of Directors of CAGID, the Council approved the acquisition of six lots on the south side of Walnut between 9th and 10th Streets. ("Camera", November 5, Bill Jordan)

#### TRANSIT

The Regional Transportation District (RTD) Board of Directors voted unanimously to purchase the Denver-Boulder Bus Company. The \$1.7 million purchase price gives RTD the 20 buses owned by the firm as well as 14 buses owned by Colorado Motorway, Inc. ("Town & Country", March 5)

Service for the elderly and the handicapped began for Boulder County on March 10 using the RTD special needs bus equipment. ("Louisville Times", March 20)

The RTD staff has recommended that the PRT or "demand responsive" transit system, be dropped from consideration by the board of directors. John Simpson, RTD director said the district is still considering other alternatives including an all bus system or a light rail system. ("Camera", April 18, Ken Frizell)

The RTD is reportedly prepared to purchase 4.25 acres at the former site of Arnold Brothers Ford at Ninth and Canyon. The purchase price is \$950,000. They then want to build a \$750,000 garage at that location and finance the plan with the money the city of Boulder will receive for the next two years from the federal Mass Transportation Act of 1974. The city has objected to the use of those funds.

Transportation Director Barbara Morrison urged Council to pass a resolution requesting the RTD to delay action on the designation of funds for the project pending the completion of the Boulder Transportation Study. ("Camera, June 18, Ken Frizell)

James Bowers, Boulder's representative to the RTD said that in view of the city's problems with the district, it is "appropriate for an analysis over the next few months as to whether the county should get out of RTD."

Ken Frizell reported in the "Camera" dated June 19:

When the state legislature drafted the RTD enabling act in 1969, language was included which defines any system the RTD can operate as being on "prescribed routes".

According to Howard Beck, RTD attorney, that precludes the district's operating a dial-a-bus, which does not follow a prescribed route.

... the Boulder RTD Citizen Action Committee recommended in March that dial-a-bus be part of a multi-level transportation system.

On July 1, Mr. Frizell wrote:

Boulder's relations with the Regional Transportation District continued to chill ... .

The district has already put \$50,000 down on the deal (to acquire the garage site at Ninth and Canyon).

As for waiting until after completion of the Boulder Transportation Study, Simpson said that failure to proceed now would foreclose the possibility of a Boulder garage.

But there appears to be some question as to whether or not the district can spend the money without the city's approval.

Barbara Morrison ... claims that it cannot ... .

Barbara Gigone wrote in the "Town & Country" on July 2:

The Regional Transportation District bus garage will be tolerated in downtown Boulder only if the ... RTD insures its short-time use by concurrently purchasing other land and preparing to construct a permanent facility.

The RTD board of directors met in Boulder and voted to purchase the land at Ninth and Canyon. They plan to remodel the existing garage and use the building temporarily as a bus facility. John Simpson, RTD executive director, told the board that RTD has been advertising for a developer of the downtown site and for land for a permanent garage.

Service at the old bus depot at 1740 14th Street will be discontinued beginning December 2. The RTD will begin operating on Saturday, November 29 from its newly remodeled offices at Ninth and Canyon. ("Town & Country", November 26)

In an article in the "Camera" of September 26, Bill Jordan reported:

The Regional Transportation District ... adopted an \$84 million 1976 budget that reflects a need for only one mill ... .

Although the budget reflects a sharp increase from this year, it would be brought into balance by using funds that have been set aside for several years for a possible refund of sales tax paid on food and drugs.

A settlement was reached in the year-long dispute between the city and the Public Service Company (PSC) which had operated the bus system under a franchise agreement with the city. It calls for the company to pay the city \$911,000 in cash along with the 10 buses in operation. In exchange, PSC will be relieved of its franchise agreement to run the system. The RTD will then purchase the system from the city by January.

For its part of the agreement, RTD will initiate a new circulator bus service, will order 20 new buses for the local routes and will initiate a bus shelter improvement program.

A downtown transfer facility will be built. RTD will use the temporary site at Ninth and Canyon on a rent-free basis for three years, then transfer the deed to three-fourths of the land to the city. The remaining land will be sold to a private developer. ("Camera", November 21, Barry Koltnow.)

A contract for use of almost \$19,000 worth of federal Health, Education and Welfare funds for a senior citizens "dial-a-bus" program was approved by the Boulder County Commissioners.

County Human Resources Director John Higgins said the program will utilize five Head Start vans and part-time employees. The county will contribute "in-kind" services for its 10 per cent matching funds portion of the project. ("Longmont Times-Call", July 11)

The airport land owned by the city consists of 160 acres which includes 75 acres for runways and taxiways, 11 acres for a tie-down area, 26 acres for hangars, 13 acres for a public area, and 35 acres presently not suited for development. ("Camera", February 20)

1976

### HIGHWAYS AND BYWAYS

In January, the City Council voted unanimously to proceed with the downtown mall and voted seven to one to authorize the reconstruction of a number of streets around the central business district. The money for improvements will come from the federal Urban Systems funds and local assessments, and will include curbs, gutters and sidewalks. ("Camera", January 21, Ken Frizell)

These decisions created a double assessment for some residents who live just off the downtown area and are in the mall assessment district and the Whittier street assessment district. ("Camera", March 25, Ken Frizell)

When the Whittier district was approved in April, the Council endorsed plans to close portions of one or two streets for mini-parks and to expand and landscape the corners at several intersections. ("Camera", April 21, Ken Frizell)

Pearl Street was closed to traffic between 11th and 15th Streets in June. Some modifications were made in the Mall Loop circulation system in July, but the counterclockwise flow on the streets around the mall was maintained.

In July, the City Council began studying a DeLeuw Cather and Company report on traffic flow. They recommended an improved at-grade intersection at Broadway and Baseline, design studies for a 27th Street by-pass south of that intersection connecting Broadway and Baseline with a "major rebuild of the U.S.36/Baseline interchange ramps". The study recommended against a Folsom Street connection with Broadway unless the city reconstructed Broadway and Baseline as an underpass-overpass arrangement. ("Town & Country", July 28)

In September, the City Council "reluctantly authorized the immediate closure of Folsom Street through the University of Colorado campus."

Council, which has been negotiating to close the street in exchange for certain university owned rights-of-way, expressed concern ... that CU might not live up to its part of the bargain.

Council eventually solved the dilemma by passing a motion directing (City Manager) Westdyke to erect temporary barricades on Folsom at Colorado and at Regent Drive for a period not to exceed 30 days. During that time, Westdyke is to secure written commitments. ("Camera", September 15, Ken Frizell)

A very complicated trade agreement was negotiated. Among other things, the University took over Folsom Street from Regent Drive to

Colorado, Colorado Avenue and 18th Street through the campus, and Green Mountain Avenue.

In return, the city made Regent Drive a dedicated city street, took over land on the corner of 28th Street and Baseline Road for ramps, and took over rights to extend Colorado Avenue through to 47th Street. ("Camera", August 1, 1977, Sue Deans)

After three years of discussion and negotiation Folsom Park has been born ... just 18 hours after the City Council voted to close (Folsom Street) for a trial period. ("Camera", September 19, Linda Cornett)

At a study session in January, the Council urged City Manager Archie Twitchell to press ahead with the much delayed 28th and 30th Street couplet. ("Camera", January 27)

The Colorado Department of Highways announced ... it will spend \$3 million on ... Foothills Parkway (47th Street).

... Boulder may contribute \$600,000 in matching funds. ("Camera", June 8, Ken Frizell)

Council agreed on the allocation of matching funds in August. After their vote, the Sierra Club successfully circulated petitions demanding a referendum on the expenditure. In September, Council unanimously agreed to refer the question to the voters. ("Camera", September 15, Ken Frizell)

The next mile of the ... Foothills Parkway ... will be built without ... city funds.

City Manager Bob Westdyke said ... that the State Highway Commission voted ... to pay the total \$600,000 toward construction ... .

Charges were made against City Manager Westdyke and Governor Lamm's environmental advisor by a spokesman of the Sierra Club claiming interference with the referendum on the Foothills Parkway. ("Camera", September 20)

The ballot question was changed to a simple pro or con vote on the continuation of 47th Street from Baseline Road to the Longmont Diagonal.

What sits in the dark, doesn't work and was supposed to solve many of Boulder's traffic light synchronization problems? It's the city's traffic computer.

The Transportation Department is analyzing the system and may decide Boulder doesn't need such a sophisticated machine. ("Camera", February 28, Debi Bregman Moss)

#### PEDESTRIANS AND BIKEWAYS

The city of Boulder is preparing a bicycle roadway plan to meet bicycling demands to the year 2000 and accept bicycling as a "legitimate, recognized means of transportation," says Barbara Morrison, director of transportation.

City Clerk Carl Chapel says about 17,000 bicycles are registered with the city although he estimates about twice that many use city roads. A municipal ordinance requires all bicycles using public roads be registered.

The City Council granted the Department of Transportation \$10,000 last August to conduct a study on integrating bicycles into the transportation system.

The transportation director says the city presently has 13.5 miles of bike roads.

The Boulder Police Department reported 211 injuries, including two fatalities, during the past three years in bicycle-related accidents. Sgt. Jeff Libby is calling for a "paraprofessional" team whose only job would be enforcement of the bicycle laws. ("Camera", March 28, Brett C. Skakun)

#### PARKING

Ken Frizell wrote in the March 10 "Camera":

Keeping its promise to Boulder merchants to increase parking downtown, the Boulder City Council ... gave informal approval to a plan which would eventually provide several hundred new parking spaces in the core area.

Within the next few months, the city is expected to begin increasing the supply of metered parking and converting several downtown streets from parallel to diagonal parking.

The administration has recommended that the three city-owned parking lots be converted to attendant lots with a 20 cents an hour parking fee.

Larry Caldwell reported in the "Camera" of March 17 that parking at the Justice Center is filled to capacity every working day.

Hobart Wagener, the architect who designed the building, said, "Deficiencies in the parking were detected during the early stages of the planning." He said many more spaces could have been built in front of the ... (center), ... (but) it would look like a shopping center.

Several officials said there was great enthusiasm during the planning stages ... that mass transit would solve the parking problem. ... The hopes have not panned out.

Tom Evans, administrator of the courts, said that during jury selection, as many as 125 prospective jurors must be at the justice center. "As long as there are cars and gasoline, we'll need parking." The only solution ... is to buy more land ... .

With the construction of the mall due to begin, and the subsequent loss of 77 parking spaces on Pearl Street, some merchants were uneasy about losses to their businesses.

The design for a new employee parking area at Ninth and Walnut has been completed.

The Parking District Advisory Board has approved a lease of the railroad property at Ninth and Canyon.

Stan Friedman of the Planning Department said, "There would be 113 spaces freed for the customers of downtown merchants ... (after) ... employee parking (is) transferred to the new ... lots." ("Town & Country", April 28)

In May, the County Commissioners decided to withhold the county's share of the Whittier improvement project until the city decides whether it will contribute funds toward a new parking lot across the street from the Justice Center. The county is planning to purchase a 2-acre parcel at the corner of Sixth and Canyon for public and employee parking. ("Camera", May 14)

The Planning Board favors a project to build a parking structure at Broadway and Walnut. It would be on the site presently occupied by a series of counter-culture shops fronting on Broadway.

The structure would be 54 feet high and contain retail and office space as well as 260 parking spaces. ("Town & Country", July 7)

#### TRANSIT

The Boulder city bus system was officially acquired by the Regional Transportation District at midnight Wednesday, December 31. RTD paid \$214,000 for the system. ("Longmont Times-Call", January 8)

The RTD board of directors approved a \$39 million project providing Boulder with a \$5 million bus storage, maintenance and administrative facility by mid-1978. The facility, to be located at 33rd Street and Arapahoe Avenue, will provide all major functions for maintaining buses serving the northern part of the district. ("Camera", February 27, Art Boden)

The Urban Mass Transportation Administration turned down an RTD request for a \$733 million rail transit system for Denver. A second application for \$31 million, critical to Boulder because it includes funds for construction of the proposed maintenance facility, is probably going to be granted. ("Camera", June 30, Ken Frizell)

Patrons of the local RTD buses are riding in vehicles that have an average age of 12 years. The 27 new buses promised by RTD will probably not be available until mid-1977.

Drivers of the buses claimed poor management and inadequate supervision of bus mechanics were the major problem. ("Camera", October 10, Ken Frizell and John Leach)

Nan Pirnack wrote in the "Town & Country" dated November 17:

Barbara Morrison, when approached about the possibility of building turnouts for buses, replied that "we're really angry with RTD. The building of turnouts is RTD's responsibility, but there have been so many managers on the Boulder scene since RTD took over the Boulder bus that the problem has still not been resolved despite repeated promises ... ."

Boulder County has received a sizable portion of \$20.4 million in federal funds as its share of the largest transportation grant awarded in the Rocky Mountain region.

Work will begin on the bus storage and maintenance facility to be constructed on a 6.9 acre site in the Eastpark Industrial area and the city's over-aged bus fleet will be replaced. ("Camera", December 30)

1977

### HIGHWAYS AND BYWAYS

In the "Camera" dated March 18, Barbara Morrison announced her resignation to take effect April 15, citing personal reasons, particularly the wish to spend more time with her family.

John Aldrich was acting director until the city named Robert Whitson to the position starting October 17.

The Whittier Street Improvement District was still a topic of intense discussion in that neighborhood. Construction had been delayed last year until fall because of a lawsuit. The city wanted to start work at an inclement time of year in order to get 1976 prices. ("Camera", April 21, Steve Hoffman)

The city's effort to ease traffic congestion at the intersection of Broadway and Baseline by constructing a new 26th Street was finished in

December. The street is included in the \$2.5 million South Link project.  
("Camera, December 21)

In March, the southern link of 47th Street connecting Colorado 93 with the turnpike was reinstated by the County Planning Commission. Commission member Howard Klemme argued it was needed to relieve traffic congestion on Broadway. In April, the County Commissioners voted to remove it again from the County Transportation Plan. ("Camera", April 12, John Leach)

In the April 24th edition of the "Camera" it was reported that the Chamber of Commerce sent a recommendation to the City Council and the County Commissioners requesting reinstatement of the 47th Street extension. They also requested cost-benefit studies of a link-up between Canyon Boulevard and Arapahoe Avenue and of the widening of Arapahoe Avenue from 30th Street to Broadway.

A new synchronized traffic signal system on the downtown loop, Broadway, and Canyon Boulevard should eliminate much of the stop-and-go traffic and idling cars along these routes. Along with the smoother flow of traffic should come some alleviation in automotive air pollution.  
("Colorado Daily", April 27, Allen Houston)

The traffic signalization projects, which will cost more than \$300,000 ... (are) ... already partly underway, ... (and) ... will put Boulder back into the computerized traffic control game, but at a much lower level of sophistication than before.

Two other major plans should also get off the ground this summer, Aldridge (Acting Transportation Director) said. They are the four-laning of Folsom from Boulder Creek to Colorado and the four-laning of Baseline from 47th to 55th.

#### PEDESTRIANS AND BIKEWAYS

The Boulder Bikeway Plan, recently released by the Transportation Department includes recommendations for bicycle education, strong enforcement of bicycle laws and regulations, the revision of the existing bicycle ordinance, and bikeway maintenance and design standards. A network of 77 miles of bike paths, bike lanes, and bike routes within 58 square miles of the city and county would link residential areas with centers of employment, shopping, education, and recreation.

The bicycle population of Boulder is estimated at 20,000 and some 3,000 bicycles are sold annually by Boulder bicycle dealers. ("Town & Country", January 5, Leonard Thompson)

In April, the City Council approved an ordinance amending the Vehicle Code to clarify the responsibilities of motor vehicles in relation to bicycles.

In May, a new bicycle ordinance was discussed by Council, but was sent back to staff for further study.

In June, after a second public hearing, five members of Council remained worried that it might create more confusion than it is intended to remedy. ("Camera", June 22, Steve Hoffman)

#### PARKING

Members of Boulder's First United Methodist Church approved a plan that includes removing five buildings on 14th and Pine Streets to make room for an enlarged parking lot.

The plan includes the possibility of negotiating a contract for the city to build and lease the lot during hours when it is not needed for church functions. ("Camera" February 1, Linda Cornett)

Sarah Hoover reported in the "Camera" dated February 6:

A group of local businessmen, organized into the Broadway-Walnut Company, is proposing ... to build ... and lease ... decked parking (on Walnut Street between 11th Street and Broadway) to the parking district.

... petitions are being circulated to anyone who pays property taxes in the Downtown Boulder Parking District and any registered qualified elector living within the parking district.

The reason for the petition ... is to amend a city ordinance to increase the debt limit to allow payment of a \$98,000 annual lease; to increase the debt limit by five years (to 25 years); to allow for the mill rate to be increased if necessary and to expand lease authorizations the district already has.

Financing would be handled from revenues from the entire parking district.

Steve Hoffman wrote in the "Camera" of February 25 that at its February 15 meeting, the Council voted unanimously to condemn the land surrounding, but not including, the Green Mountain Grainery. The short term use of the land would be to replace employee parking at the location selected for the senior citizens center.

A week later, a majority of the Planning Board voted against the use of condemnation to acquire land for parking.

The Council stuck to its unanimous decision despite the opposition of property owners. One of the owners, Pinon Oil Company, had already spent \$150,000 preparing for construction of their office building. Peter Dietze, the lawyer representing Pinon Oil, said his objections "centered on the fact that the land was not formally included in either the regular 1977 budget hearings last fall or the capital improvements plan devised each summer by the Planning Board."

In response, City Manager Westdyke said the "council has indicated in past votes its intention to buy up land (in the so-called civic block) as it becomes available." ("Camera", March 16, Steve Hoffman)

In April, the Council was pondering parking problems again, and concluded something has to be done for the Hill merchants, and that the old

Stammler Porsche-Audi site at 15th and Pearl should be acquired for the site of a downtown parking structure.

According to memos supplied by staff, the present parking demand on the Hill was estimated at about 225 spaces, although only 127 are available.

The University Hill General Improvement District has urged that 100 spaces be added as soon as possible.

The most controversial proposal for increasing Hill parking has been acquisition of one to three residential properties on the east side of 12th Street. The idea has drawn heated opposition on grounds that it would eliminate units of low-income housing which conflicts with the city's commitment to encourage such housing.

Acquisition of the downtown site is being delayed because of the reluctance of Mountain Bell, owner of the site, to annex to the Central Area General Improvement District.

Mountain Bell has some \$8 million in equipment in the area, which would be subject to the district's mill levy. The parking district cannot be expanded by forced annexation. Businesses outside the district can derive benefits from district parking facilities without paying the costs of providing them.

#### TRANSIT

Four Free-B buses, especially designed and decorated for a free lift service were introduced in April. Coordinated by the Chamber of Commerce, the project is paid for through local businesses and agencies. The shuttle will serve downtown, Crossroads, the Hill, and the University. ("Camera", April 19)

In a progress report submitted to the Council, James Bowers gave an account of various RTD projects. Bus pullouts planned for Table Mesa, University Hill School, and Community Hospital should be completed by October. The city and the RTD will cooperate in deciding on a new downtown Boulder transfer point. And delivery is expected early next year on a fleet of new buses. ("Camera", July 20, Sue Deans)

A lawsuit has been filed in federal court in Denver which would delay the delivery of 231 new buses to the RTD until the new equipment includes special wheelchair lift devices.

Lift devices would be present initially in only 18 of the new coaches. To place wheelchair lifts on all the new buses would cost nearly \$1.5 million in addition to the approximately \$16 million already committed for purchase.

To compound the problem, the Yellow Cab Company and the independent driver's union sued RTD in mid-July to force the transit operator to halt all special service to the elderly and the handicapped. The two suits could have diametrically opposed results. ("Town & Country", August 3)

A recent ridership survey, as part of a year-long test program begun July 1, disclosed no burden on the RTD system or equipment with free service offered to persons over 65.

Senior citizens can now ride public buses every day, anywhere in the six-county region except during "peak-hours", without paying fares. ("Camera", November 2)

Boulder Municipal Airport has slipped into disrepair and needs over \$135,000 of improvements. The airport brings in around \$8,000 a year from leases with fixed base operators. It has fixed expenses including insurance, utilities and maintenance which cost around \$21,000.

Council is reluctant to provide any more money when the facility is used by so few persons, and when some local flying generates a large number of citizen complaints. ("Camera", July 12, Bill Jordan)

1978

### HIGHWAYS AND BYWAYS

Last fall, the city received grants from the federal government to tear down and reconstruct both the Sixth and the Ninth Street bridges so they would not present as much of an obstacle to flood water.

Several neighborhood residents have requested that the Sixth Street bridge (demolished last month) either not be rebuilt or be replaced with a "one-lane bridge that could handle emergency traffic as well as pedestrians and bicyclists." ("Camera", January 22, Steve Hoffman)

The City Council voted to rebuild the bridge with two lanes for cars, a special lane for bicycles and a sidewalk for pedestrians. ("Denver Post", February 1, Jane Cracraft)

Sixth Street opened again in August, and Ninth Street between Canyon and Arapahoe was closed so its 50-year-old bridge can be replaced with a span almost twice as long. ("Camera", August 25)

The much-delayed Foothills Parkway is the city's number one nominee for state and federal construction funds again in 1979. It has been a top priority for two years. The state has already appropriated money to buy the right-of-way from Arapahoe to Pearl Street plus another \$1.5 million for construction of that section. ("Camera", September 20, Linda Cornett)

#### PEDESTRIANS AND BIKEWAYS

A bicycle ordinance was passed by Council giving Boulder's cyclists the right to ride in the right-hand lane instead of being required to hug the curb. They are still required to use bike lanes which are part of the roadway, but are freed from the legal stipulation that they must use bike paths. ("Camera" March 22, Steve Hoffman)

In September, Mo Siegel, president of Celestial Seasonings Herb Teas, announced that there would be no more Red Zinger bicycle races in Boulder until the city offers some firm commitment to improving its accommodations for bicyclists. Siegel wants the roads safe for bicyclists. He says that separate bike paths discourage bicycling by routing riders blocks out of their way and they cost too much.

The director of the city's bikeway program, Doug Hartshorn, says he is beginning to question the safety and cost effectiveness of bikeways, particularly those that mingle bicyclists and automobile traffic. He insists that the 10-year bikeway construction plan is on schedule with 13 miles completed. Some of his budget is spent on correcting flaws in existing bikeways which "quite often are poorly designed". ("Camera", September 13, Linda Cornett)

#### PARKING

The resolution of the battle between the city and the property owners near Ninth and Arapahoe came when the site of the senior center was moved to that location and the employee parking lot remained at 11th and Arapahoe. The purchases were made without condemnation being necessary. Green Mountain Grainery was also acquired, but has a two year lease with the city. The contract requires that the city provide parking lots for the store during that time. ("Camera", September 10, Linda Cornett)

Hill business people passed a \$375,000 bond issue to pay for additional parking. Only the merchants voted because it is their money that will pay the bond if parking revenues can not.

Several University Hill residents complained that their views were not represented in the choice of suggested sites, which they say would require tearing down existing homes. ("Camera", September 20, Linda Cornett)

The Hill merchants expressed frustration with city hall, which, they claimed, has been ignoring them for years. The city had delayed consideration of a report on possible parking lot sites after a protest by residents of the area. Instead they had authorized the City Manager to order a study of traffic circulation, pedestrian movement, future uses and future zoning boundaries as well as parking needs. ("Camera", October 22, Sarah Hoover)

In an article by Chip Howard in the "Camera" of September 30, he reported:

The new four-story downtown parking facility leased by the City of Boulder for \$8,166 per month is bringing in about \$800 a month in revenue.

Clint Fullen, director of parking districts and enforcement, said the garage at 1100 Walnut St., which opened Aug. 11, "certainly has alleviated a lot of the need for business (long-term) parking and short-term parking in the west end of downtown."

There are 261 spaces in the garage. Until (it) can pay for itself, money from other parking programs, such as meters and monthly permit lots, will be used to help pay the bills ... .

#### TRANSIT

Despite repeated charges that Boulder County taxpayers are subsidizing the bus service in Denver, the county appears to be getting its share of the RTD budget.

RTD spent \$4.7 million in Boulder County operations, and it received \$1.3 million in fares and other revenues and about \$3.2 million in sales

taxes.

RTD officials proudly point to the district's recent efforts:

Twenty-nine new buses ... were placed in the Boulder fleet last month, replacing 22 ... obsolete buses dating back two decades. Another six are used for the Free-B.

A major shift in bus routes, including two new routes and the first service to Gunbarrel and Boulder Valley Village, was inaugurated today ...

Construction began last year on a \$9 million solar-heated ... facility at 33rd Street and Arapahoe.

A \$1.1 million downtown Transportation Center, a terminal for local and regional buses, was proposed for the corner of 14th Street and Canyon Boulevard. ("Camera", March 5, John Leach)

A portion of the RTD parking lot on the northeast corner of Ninth and Canyon will be available for commuter parking on weekdays. The lot will be fenced for bus storage since the former storage area west of the terminal had to be vacated for construction of the Canyon Pointe housing project. ("Camera", October 26)

Last August, Trans Mountain Airlines suggested a helicopter service between Boulder hotels and the Denver airport. Proposed helicopter pad locations would have caused zoning problems and were near residential areas which would be disturbed by the noise. An interim airplane shuttle was suggested between Boulder and Denver airports until a suitable helicopter landing site could be discovered.

The city, the county, state and federal officials became involved and, as a result of the controversy, plans were dropped and have not been heard of since. ("Town & Country, October 11, Susan Eastman)

### HIGHWAYS AND BYWAYS

A proposal for the widening of North Broadway, first discussed in 1975, was presented to the Council in May. After listening to neighborhood objections, they returned it to the Transportation Department for further study. ("Camera", May 16, Sue Deans)

The staff came up with a four-lane plan using narrower right-of-way with a meandering sidewalk on one side and a bike path on the other. The Council unanimously approved the "new" North Broadway project. ("Camera", June 20, Sue Deans)

During this summer, a large number of street projects were under way including 47th Street connecting Baseline and Arapahoe, the first phase of the Goss-Grove project, Ninth Street Bridge, the widenings of Broadway between Regent and College, Folsom between Boulder Creek and Colorado Avenue, Baseline between 47th and 55th, and Colorado Avenue between 30th and 47th, and flood-control improvements with a culvert and bridge at 34th and Iris with street improvements scheduled later. ("Camera", August 3, Sue Deans)

On Friday, September 14, the Ninth Street Bridge was reopened. Original predictions for replacement were four or five months, but the construction took over a year. Transportation Director Bob Whitson said the harsh winter delayed completion as did heavy summer schedules of the Public Service Company and Flatiron Paving. ("Camera", September 16, Sue Deans)

In the process of approving the 1980 budget, the Council deleted \$460,000 proposed to be spent on a pedestrian underpass at Broadway and College. ("Colorado Daily", October 4)

The city's bill for snow removal from last week's storm is more than \$100,000 putting the city at least \$20,000 over the 1979 snow removal budget. City Manager Westdyke said the city coped as well as it could with unusual conditions that had cold weather follow the 31.2 inch snowfall. ("Camera", Sharon Massingill and Sue Deans)

In December, Council approved final assessments for street and sidewalk improvements in the Whittier neighborhood. Lynn Hollar of the city manager's office said it was the largest residential improvement project in the city's history, involving 30 city blocks and 280 property owners.

#### PEDESTRIANS AND BIKEWAYS

Signs have been installed marking the completion of a six-mile, \$89,000 bikeway system constructed jointly by the University and the City of Boulder.

The system features 10 routes to the center of campus. The city constructed the Baseline/Broadway segment from 27th Street and Baseline to Regent Drive and Broadway. ("Colorado Daily", August 1)

Some of the old sandstone sidewalk along the west edge of the campus had to be moved when the new bikeway was constructed. ("Camera", February 23, Laurence T. Paddock)

#### PARKING

Linda Cornett wrote in the "Camera" of January 31:

A plan to increase parking for the business area of University Hill got a green light from Boulder's City Council Tuesday.

The city will begin negotiating for property to build a new lot at the north end of the Hill.

In addition, they will begin on plans to extend the existing lot behind Jones Drug onto a portion of 14th Street.

The changes would also include expanding evening hours for the (existing) lot and improving its lighting and landscaping.

The two projects, plus added on-street parking, would increase Hill business parking spaces from 117 to about 210.

Apparently in response to complaints from residents (including members of the Chi Psi Fraternity and Dr. Martin Spector), expansion plans for the Jones Drug parking lot have been modified. Both the Chi Psis and Spector claim that the lot is a gathering place for delinquents and the problem will worsen if the lot is expanded.

Currently the University Hill Parking District is providing security at the lot on the weekends. ("Colorado Daily", May 16, Melinda Green)

In August, the Council approved a motion to pursue acquiring a site at 12th and Pleasant Streets for Hill parking, instead of beginning condemnation procedures against the Exxon station at 13th and Pleasant.

Two pieces of property are involved at the 12th and Pleasant site. One is part of a larger parcel owned by a trust. The city is negotiating to lease the land and purchase the air rights. Despite Council's reluctance to condemn property, they have agreed to that action if negotiation does not succeed. ("Camera", December 5, Sue Deans)

The city of Boulder's downtown parking garage brought in only half of what it cost during the first 10 months of operation.

The 260-space garage, part of the Randolph Center at Broadway and Walnut Street, costs the city \$11,000 a month in lease payments and

operational expenses.

It was built by the Broadway-Walnut Company, which operates the retail and office complex in the east half of the building. The garage, which was constructed on parking district land, is leased to the city for 25 years, and then it automatically becomes district property. ("Camera", June 22, John Leach)

The garage, municipal parking lots and downtown parking meters are operated by the Central Area General Improvement District, which was formed by the city and downtown businessmen to finance parking improvements.

The deficit from the garage operation is being made up by income from employee parking lots. The attended short-term ... lots break even, and parking meter revenues and property taxes are paying off the bonds that financed the purchase of the parking lots.

To provide an estimated 1,000 parking spaces needed downtown in the next 10 years, the Central Area General Improvement District has recommended eventually building parking structures on two downtown sites.

The sites are Mountain Bell property on the north half of the block bounded by Pearl, Walnut, 15th and 16th streets and the site above a city-owned parking lot at 14th and Canyon which is to become a bus terminal for the Regional Transportation District.

Initial plans call for a surface parking lot on the Mountain Bell site, with decks to be added later as needed.

The RTD bus station will occupy the ground level at 14th and Canyon, with parking decks added above. ("Camera", December 11, Sue Deans)

City Council decided to shelve a proposed parking permit program which would control student parking in the Hill neighborhood.
