The proposed program would have enabled residents to purchase \$12 annual permits which would allow them to park on neighborhood streets any time of the day. Cars without permits would be allowed to park only two consecutive hours between 8 a.m. and 5 p.m.

No city administrator recommended putting the plan into action. Disadvantages they cited included spillover of University parking to areas outside that neighborhood, inability of visitors to park (near the places they were visiting), and the high cost of enforcing the program. ("Camera", May 9, Sue Deans)

Estimates for implementation costs of the system are about \$54,000 the first year. ("Colorado Daily", May 9, Michael Ross)

City officials have found 14 more parking spaces for the Senior Citizens Center, bringing the total number there to 50.

Seniors have pleaded with the City Council over the last few months to increase parking at the center, noting that many of the building's elderly and handicapped users are unable to use the bus or walk long distances. ("Camera", October 31)

#### TRANSIT

In 1975, RTD's first full year of operation, budgeted expenditures totaled \$62 million. By this year the budget had escalated 88 percent, representing an annual growth rate of approximately 17 percent.

At RTD's inception, two statutory funding mechanisms were provided to subsidize any deficit between passenger fares and total operating costs. Portions of the one-half cent sales tax and two mills ad valorem property tax were to be used for this purpose. Congressional approval of federal operating grants in 1974 now allows RTD to draw upon a third source for meeting any deficits. Prior to this legislation, federal grants could only be used for capital construction. ("Louisville Times, January 4)

Bus fares will return to the area during non-rush hour times marking the end of a one-year free fare experiment that has raised transit ridership to a 20-year high. Bus ridership in Boulder County jumped by 5,000 passengers to 14,817 on an average weekday. ("Camera", January 29, John Leach)

The FreeB, two years old last week, was based on in-kind promotion and advertising support pledged by Boulder businessmen and CU. Lack of support

from the business community has caused the need to charge fares, according to RTD Executive Director John Simpson. "The University is probably the one element of Boulder that did its share in the time the FreeB has been in effect," he said. ("Camera", April 27, Thad Keyes)

Renewed support from the University of Colorado has succeeded in gaining at least a temporary delay in the imposition of fares for the FreeB shuttle bus. ("Camera", May 30, Sharon Massingwell)

Council members unanimously approved a Park'n'Ride facility at 27th and Broadway. It will be built by the Regional Transportation District on city-owned land, and leased to RTD for 20 years. ("Camera", June 6, Sue Deans)

The Table Mesa Park'n'Ride facility will be expanded to provide 255 spaces with room for bicycle and motorcycle parking and special spaces for the handicapped. There also will be a covered shelter. ("Denver Post", June 27)

The RTD chose a site at 14th and Walnut streets for its new transportation center. The main political barrier is a dispute over its displacing a 94-space city parking lot. The project would be funded at 80 percent by a federal Department of Transportation grant and 20 percent by the RTD. ("Camera", June 29, Thad Keyes)

The RTD bus storage and maintenance facility at 1707 Exposition Drive was completed in February. It is the county's largest solar heated building with more than 30,000 square feet of solar collectors and a 60,000 gallon water reservoir. The active solar system is operated by computer. ("Camera", December 22, Donna Marek)

The RTD is working to create a light-rail system in the Denver metro area by the early 1980s. Finding construction money and determining the public's need and desire for light-rail are the major chores RTD faces before the project can begin, according to RTD Acting Director Howard Beck. A rail link between downtown Boulder and Crossroads Shopping Center could be among the first light-rail routes. ("Camera", October 13, Thad Keyes)

To save energy and set a good example for other citizens, city employees may request RTD tokens for their trips to and from work. The city used to give out bus passes, but mis-use or non-use of that system was too expensive and too difficult to administer. The subsidy costs the city approximately \$23,000 a year. ("Camera", December 18, Steven Wilhelm)

Complaints by a citizen living near the Municipal Airport have caused the city to take a closer look at the noise problem.

Solutions are difficult. For the future, the city could impose tighter control over land use around the airport. Closing the facility would be expensive since the city would have to reimburse the fixed base operators who have lease agreements, and would have to pay back the federal government its portion of a grant received in 1977 for purchase of noise-impacted land. Conditions attached to that grant stipulated that the airport operate for another 20 years. Changing the flight pattern is not recommended by the Federal Aviation Administration since the airport does not have a control tower. ("Camera", October 16, Chip Coward)

A civil suit was filed in District Court in December by Neil McKenzie seeking to halt operations at the airport. Mr. McKenzie claims the city has not lived up to an agreement made with him in 1974 and has violated the conditions attached to a special use permit granted by the county in 1975. ("Longmont Times-Call" December 8-9, Steve Krizman)





## CHAPTER IV

### LAND USE

1975

### PLANNING AND ZONING

The Citizen's Committee on Housing and Community Development was briefed by the City Manager on January 6 about the downtown revitalization program. He emphasized the need to prevent further deterioration of the core area in order to retain the tax base and market for local businessmen.

Some of the objectives prepared by the staff for discussion included the mall project, the senior citizens center, utilization of solar energy, and the revitalization of the Goss/Grove area.

The expenditure of \$650,000 in federal funds for the city's part in the downtown mall project was approved by the committee for the next two years. They also voted \$35,000 for physical improvements of existing low-income housing projects with priority for children's playgrounds to enable day-care programs.

The committee agreed that the proposed senior center would take priority in first year funding if the mall project falls through. ("Town & Country, January 15)

The Goss-Grove neighborhood was proposed for federal Neighborhood Development Project funds two years ago. Then federal funding was cut off and the proposal fell through. ("Town & Country", January 15)

Residents of the area have been meeting for several months as part of an effort to draw up a comprehensive plan to control the development of their neighborhood.

The group is the first in the city to test the idea of neighborhood planning control. The area's location close to the downtown mall makes it ideal for developers planning high density apartment complexes, and it was to avoid this that the association was formed.

This conflict between homeowners and developers could lead to controversy in the future. It has been said that some city officials see the area as a prime site for high density low- and middle-income housing. ("Colorado Daily", May 9, Lynne Domash)

Boulder Pow Wow Inc. brought suit against the city in September because of its refusal to rezone the Pow Wow rodeo grounds between 28th and 30th streets north of Pearl. The city considers the public-established zoning correct for the land. Pow Wow wants it re-zoned to a business category so they may sell it and buy a larger lot farther from the city. ("Camera", September 14, Bill Conti)

Boulder's sign code, authored by planning staff member Mary Jones, was aimed at solving the problem of too many signs in too little space. It limits the size, style and number of signs allowed for each business. The only notable opposition when the code went into effect was from the billboard industry. The ordinance prohibits permanent billboards not located on the premises of the business being advertised.

The sign code in 1971 gave businesses with illegal signs a limit of three years to conform to the new regulation. Special exceptions made a seven-year limit possible. Last year, Council extended the three-year limit to April 1, 1975. There have been virtually no requests for extensions. ("Town & Country", March 19, Barbara White and Michael Kinsley)

A recent ruling by the Colorado Supreme Court said that prohibiting

billboards goes beyond the zoning power to regulate and restrict. Although the case dealt with the Denver Sign Code, Boulder's code is similar and will probably have to be changed. ("Camera", November 20)

The Council was presented with a proposed "Growth Management Plan" (GMS) by Planning Director Nolan Rosall. He said one major aspect to consider is that new development outside the current full city service area will cost the city more than if that development takes place in vacant or decaying areas within the full service area.

For those who support the idea, growth management is a solution to urban sprawl. For those who oppose it, it is an infringement on the rights of the private property owner. Both stances are represented on Council. ("Colorado Daily", April 9)

In the proposed GMS, it's okay for Boulder to get larger, as long as it's done in the "right" way. It changes the city's development rules so that much of the population will be added downtown, instead of in the suburbs. It will help make public transportation work and reduce dependence on cars. ("Denver Post", April 14, Jane Cracraft)

The present administration, when asked if the core of experts who worked on the Growth Study Commission in 1972-73 had been asked to review the GMS, were not sure. ("Denver Post", July 22, Jane Cracraft)

The Boulder Board of Realtors and the Chamber of Commerce went on record as opposing the proposed Growth Management System. The realtors said the GMS "reverses the entire concept of individual property ownership and right of use ... ." The Chamber says it involves "excessive government control".

Nolan Rosall, who developed the system, said "A developer shouldn't have the right to abuse or misuse the land or to build in areas where

services aren't available and pass on massive problems to the city and the people."

The Chamber has already suggested that a "staging system" for development should be included in the Comprehensive Plan, that incentives should be provided for developers and that the city should streamline its development review process.

The Planning Board held public hearings on the plan by the Flatiron Companies to mine seven million tons of sand and gravel on land located south of Table Mesa Drive and the Denver-Boulder Turnpike.

Flatiron wants the city to annex 418 acres of the land and grant a special review to permit the mining. In return, the company, depending upon a favorable ruling from the Internal Revenue Service, says it will reclaim the land and donate a portion of it to the city for a regional park. Flatiron plans to develop housing on the remainder of the parcel.

A member of PLAN-Boulder questioned the city's involvement in the preparation of an environmental impact assessment done in conjunction with the project. The city co-authored the report and spent more than \$15,000 on the plan.

Ruth Wright, a Boulder attorney, ... pointed out that the proposed site is within a mile of the Harlow C. Platts Park, ... (that) ... the park would be at the very southern fringe of the city when all indications are that growth will occur to the north and east ... and that fears that the land would be mined even without city approval are groundless because of state law.

Whatever the board's decision, the plan must be approved by the Council. ("Camera", May 30, Ken Frizell)

City Planning Director Nolan Rosall said that he is "leaning" toward an agricultural zoning for the entire 418 acre proposed Flatiron annexation. That designation would allow the gravel operation but deny the 85 acres of medium density and 33 acres of low density residential use. One impact of residential development would add to the number of students at the already overcrowded schools in the area. ("Town & Country", June 4)

A special administration task force decided in favor of the Flatiron proposal. Don Walker, the city's real estate director said the agreement to be presented to Planning Board and Council cuts the length of the mining operation from 30 to 20 years and that the entire parcel would be zoned agricultural. ("Camera", September 9, Ken Frizell)

The Planning Board voted 5 to 1 to annex the 418 acres and then voted 4 to 2 to grant Flatiron permission to mine the land. Several protesters suggested there should be an election on the proposal. ("Camera", September 26, Ken Frizell)

The City Council expressed its support of a land use bill (HB 1092) before the legislature which would require municipalities to chart the boundaries of those areas to which they would provide urban services. ("Camera", February 9)

Under Colorado land use law, House Bill 1041, the County Commissioners designated "new communities" as areas of state interest.

Under the new system, a city must draw up a comprehensive plan for its future development, and submit that plan to the county. After it is adopted by both the city and county, -- as the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan has been -- then the city can proceed with annexations in that area.

But if a city wants to make substantial changes, it must return to the commissioners for an amendment to the plan or it must apply for a special permit. The commissioners will then review the application to see if there is a need for the development, if adequate public facilities such as water, sewer and streets are being provided, and if no significant geologic problems or natural hazards exist. ("Denver Post", June 10, Jane Cracraft)

Some of the procedures in revising the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan are being discussed by the city and county planning staffs. The city

wants to revise the plan in segments while the county wants to amend the entire plan at one time.

Also questioned was the Urban Systems boundary for highway funding. Commissioner Maggie Markey asked Council why the boundary did not include Flagstaff Drive in city greenbelt land. ("Camera", October 3, John Leach)

The residents of North Boulder claim that the city is effecting a de facto rezoning of their neighborhood by extending utilities to the area and, therefore, making higher densities possible. Under county zoning the area could be developed at 2.9 dwelling units per acre when central water and sewer are provided. The residents asked the County Commissioners for rezoning to limit density to one dwelling unit per acre. ("Town & Country", March 19)

Despite strong objections by some neighborhood residents, the Planning Board approved a request by Gemini Inc. to open a home for runaway youths at 815 Alpine. Gemini Inc. is a private, non-profit organization with ties to the Boulder County Social Services Department. ("Camera", June 6, Ken Frizell)

Planning Director Nolan Rosall said administrative changes have been made in two departments. The zoning function has been transferred from the Building and Housing Codes Division to the Planning Division. ("Town & Country", June 11)

The Council rejected the advice of both the Planning Board and the planning director and turned down a request by Boulder Memorial Hospital to rezone several parcels of property so they could construct medical offices near 4th Street and Maxwell Avenue. ("Camera", August 6, Ken Frizell)

The City Council appointed a Codes Review Committee to review and redraft the city's structural and fire and safety codes, "to permit a flexibility of materials and construction techniques where desirable without sacrificing quality." ("Camera", August 7)

The Planning Board voted 3 to 2 against extending water and sewer service to the Sombrero Ranch development east of Cherryvale Road. Objections of the majority of area residents were high density, the right-of-way reserved on Cherryvale which might allow the city to make that road an alternative by-pass, and the distribution of units which in effect would allow the new homes to be clustered against existing properties leaving open space in the middle for the benefit of the new homeowners only. ("Camera", Environmental Bulletins, August 10)

#### SHOPPING CENTERS

General Growth and Development Corp., the firm which has in the past promoted the idea of incorporation in the Niwot-Gunbarrel area so they may build a shopping center, has offered to pay part of the cost of an economic feasibility study of Gunbarrel's incorporation vs. annexation to Boulder. ("Camera", January 3, Ken Frizell)

Mr. Frizell wrote in the February 26 "Camera":

It's no simple trick to build a \$16 million shopping center.

That was the lesson learned ... by the City Council as it met to review a proposal to develop such a regional facility in the Arapahoe Village area.

The Arapahoe Village site is being proposed by Ralph Biernbaum, ... and Jim Kean ... .

Biernbaum currently holds an option on about 12 acres of undeveloped land in the vicinity of Arapahoe Village North. The land is owned by Western Industries, which also controls another seven acres that is already devoted to several smaller businesses.

Council discussed the 12-acre plan ... in late January, but voiced its preference for a larger site, one incorporating as much as 24 acres.

May D&F (Department Store) officials have said the smaller site is unacceptable.

Biernbaum has said he is willing to go after the 24 acres, but only with the city's help. ... council decided ... that the development should take place, that the city would be willing to use its industrial bonding capability to assist the development and might even be willing to go beyond that by using other means, including either a special parking or other district to help out.

Preliminary site plans for the Biernbaum shopping center propose a two story structure with a central mall on each floor. Sears Roebuck and May D&F have both expressed interest in the center.

Parking would be provided for 2,200 cars, but half these would be on an upper deck.

One reason city officials are agreeable to helping fund land acquisition is their opposition to any regional shopping center outside the city which would drain off sales tax revenue. Another is that the area is easily served by public transit from downtown and the University.

Nolan Rosall, city planning director said, "if it turns out that the package submitted by Biernbaum does not meet community standards, it does not deserve community support," presumably a reference to both the city's bonding capacity and its ability to assemble the site through condemnation. ("Town & Country", March 5)

Westcor came back to town two years after the city had refused to annex their shopping center site at 33rd and Arapahoe. Bypassing city officials, they talked with the Core Area Revitalization Committee about a study of the use of the former Arnold Brothers Ford Company property at Ninth and Canyon as a feasible site for a downtown shopping center. They said they could not develop that site without city support.

Nolan Rosall said a large shopping center downtown may not be beneficial considering traffic and parking problems. ("Town & Country", April 16)

Jane Cracraft reported in the "Denver Post" of April 8:

At the request of Boulder City Manager Archie Twitchell, the Boulder County Commissioners agreed ... to issue up to \$7 million in economic development revenue bonds to help finance a proposed new shopping center within the city limits.

The bonds won't be issued unless the ... developer can meet city and county standards.

C. W. Carpenter, secretary of the Better Government League and a citizen watchdog, complained that Twitchell circumvented the city charter and went "by the backdoor" to the commissioners.

Under Boulder's 1917 City Charter, no bonds can be issued without an election. Twitchell said Boulder may be the only city in the state with such a restriction.

The county commissioners ... can sell the bonds without a public vote.

City officials would prefer to see multi-use development at Ninth and Canyon because of its proximity to a redeveloping residential area. They said a commercial-office-residential complex would have less impact on the streets. ("Town & Country", April 9)

Ken Frizell reported in the "Camera" dated April 14:

... Tom Rogers, chairman of the mayor's Core Area Revitalization Committee has resigned his position.

Rogers, who is also president of the Downtown Business Association (DBA) ... sent a letter to council on behalf of the DBA's executive committee "to communicate to you our deep concern regarding a City Administration decision to locate a major shopping center in the Boulder City limits outside the downtown area, without an opportunity to fully evaluate and study a downtown location."

Certain members of the planning board seem to share Roger's sentiments.

Jay Tracy, a member of all three groups, said ... that, "I cannot understand why we have a planning board and why the core committee was set up, when the decision was made to locate a shopping center to the east of the downtown and neither body was even invited to share in the decision making process."

In the "Denver Post" of April 15, Jane Cracraft wrote:

The city council's aggressive campaign to get a major shopping center located in the city has developed into a three-way competition.

... Ralph Biernbaum ... wants to build on a 14-acre site west of the Crossroads Shopping Center at 28th St. and Arapahoe Ave.

The development firm of Westcor Inc. ... is looking at a downtown site.

A Boulder investor, George Wilson, has quietly assembled a 24-acre site north of Crossroads near 28th and Pearl Sts.

City Manager Archie Twitchell will report to the city council ... that the Wilson project fits the city's criteria most closely.

"Camera" writer Ken Frizell reported on April 20:

The administration, namely City Manager Archie Twitchell and Planning Director Nolan Rosall, both agree that the best place for the center is on land just north of the Crossroads Shopping Center ... .

Their choice, however, was made much to the chagrin of downtown businessmen who favor a center downtown, and Ralph Biernbaum, who has spent the last several months trying to put together a center in the Arapahoe Village North area.

Council's solution was to go on record as not favoring any site.

In a "Camera" article dated May 13, Mr. Frizell wrote:

George Wilson ... who is attempting to locate a regional shopping center on land north of the Crossroads area, has selected Westcor, Inc. to develop that site.

... Wilson, Westcor and downtown interests will attempt to work together to develop both areas.

A dispute between city officials and that of the Crossroads Shopping Center over improved landscaping and traffic circulation was resolved.

Crossroads threatened to sue the city, but agreement was reached leading to issuance of a building permit so the Denver Dry Goods Co. could open a store at Crossroads. ("Camera", June 14)

In June, and again in July, the Planning Board met with Mr. Biernbaum and his staff. After accusations both from Nolan Rosall about the developer's lack of cooperation and from James Bowers, an associate of the developer, about planning staff lies and delays, the board rejected the Arapahoe Village North site. Objections were primarily because of the traffic impact and the lack of innovation in design. ("Camera", June 20, July 11, Ken Frizell)

Mr. Frizell wrote in the November 21 "Camera":

The (planning) board voted 5 to 2 last week to reject a plan by George Wilson ... and Westcor Inc. ... to build the center in the block bounded by Pearl, 28th, 30th and Walnut Sts.

Because of criticism by both Wilson and his attorney ..., and because in rejecting the plan, the board went against the advice of Planning Director Nolan Rosall, the council voted to review the matter ... .

Bill Jordan wrote in the December 12 "Camera":

Westcor Inc., ... the developer turned down recently on its application to build a major shopping center under the city's planned unit development rules, has notified the city it will build its center anyway.

Boulder Planning Director Nolan Rosall ... said that under the city's land use regulations, Westcor and local developer George Wilson have the right to proceed with the development under a "use by right" approach.

The decision means Westcor will lose some of the flexibility it might have gained in center design. Normal 35-foot height restrictions must be complied with as well as set-back and parking requirements.

Rosall said Westcor will probably miss out on funds from economic development bonds the city may have endorsed for the developer.

#### DOWNTOWN

Rod Benson took over as manager of the Chamber of Commerce on January

1. In the "Camera" of January 5, Linda Cornett quoted him:

If a mall is well-done ... , it can do more in terms of downtown revitalization than any other single factor.

Although a mall can improve the environment of a financially troubled downtown area, that doesn't guarantee customers, he said.

Parking is a number-one priority in making a downtown attractive to customers ... .

Ken Frizell reported in the January 15 "Camera" that the City Council "indicated by voice vote its willingness to commit to a \$467,000 package of Pearl Street improvements" which included drainage work and widening from 15th to 21st Street, and improvement from Folsom to 30th. Council unanimously supported improved signing, striping and signalization of the one-way loop which includes Walnut, 15th, Spruce and 11th Streets. They also were unanimous in agreeing to the concept of downtown alley improvements and to a program of providing adequate parking to serve the mall.

Next, council unanimously agreed to commit \$650,000 to the construction of the mall itself, which will run along Pearl from 11th to 15th Street.

Theoretically, that money will come from the recently enacted federal Housing and Community Development Act.

Later in the month, the council approved an ordinance authorizing the

establishment of the mall.

In March, the city hired an economic consultant to prepare an assessment plan which would be equitable and legally defensible. the Downtown Boulder Association voted to approve the concept.

In June the work had been completed on the assessment package. The assessment district included nine subdistricts in an area generally bounded by Canyon Boulevard, 17th Street, Pine Street and Ninth Street. Of the \$1.85 million cost, \$1.2 million will come from the assessment district.

Larry Knudsen reported in the June 2 "Camera":

Boulder's \$1.85 million pedestrian mall has survived a test in District Court where Judge Richard Dana agreed with the city that no damage claims should be paid to four downtown businesses.

(The judge) ruled that both Boulder's mall ordinance ... and the state Public Mall Act of 1970 are constitutional.

The court test was initiated by the City of Boulder which wanted to clear up the question of how the mall affected the property rights of downtown.

Defendants in the suit were four downtown businesses which alleged the creation of the mall constituted a taking of their property without just compensation.

In December, the Colorado Supreme Court affirmed Judge Dana's ruling.

• On August 6, the "Camera's" Ken Frizell wrote:

Barring new legal challenges, Boulder's proposed Pearl Street mall has cleared its last major obstacle.

... the Boulder City Council approved, 8 to 0 ... the creation of a special assessment district to provide the ... (money) ... to build the four-block mall.

Opposition to the assessment came from residents and church members who claimed they would not benefit from the mall.

A member of the National Organization for Women objected to the city's use of federal Community Development funds to pay the city's share of the mall.

#### HISTORIC PRESERVATION

Timothy Lange reported in the "Colorado Daily" of June 6:

When the city's historical landmarks ordinance was approved last September, it was considered a major victory for many citizens, especially those associated with Historic Boulder.

In the months since then, however, the five-member Landmarks Board and its official and unofficial advisers have been unable to take the next step -- that of establishing specific criteria by which landmark structures and districts will be determined.

1976

### PLANNING AND ZONING

Four 28th Street motels are accused of violating a Boulder sign code because they did not remove or remodel their oversized signs by April 1, 1975 as required. Attorneys for the motels argued that the city's sign regulations were unconstitutional. ("Camera", March 19)

Nolan Rosall said that he has decided to put the Growth Management System "on the back burner" until an updated Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan (BVCP) is revised. The Planning Board agreed that an updated comprehensive plan should have first priority.

He said that while the core area "has considerable capacity to absorb growth in the Boulder Valley, obviously it will not support the total demand." He advocated that the Council review its passive annexation policy and the issue of revocable water and sewer permits. ("Town & Country, March 31)

The Council considered proposed revisions to the BVCP at a study session. The revised plan called for a three-area classification: Area I, the City of Boulder; Area II, the area anticipated to be served by the city within 15 years; and Area III, that area within the sphere of influence of

the city, but not anticipated to be ready for urban services or facilities for at least 15 years. ("Town & Country", July 14)

City planner, Ed Gawf, hit a hornet's nest of angry residents when he proposed zoning the area from 9th St. to the mountains along Pearl St. as "high density residential".

Part of the neighborhood now is zoned industrial, part is commercial, and part is residential. ("Denver Post", January 22, Jane Cracraft)

The rezoning of the West Pearl area was before the Planning Board in May and they approved all the changes except one which requested that the area along Pearl Street from 3rd to near 7th be given a high density residential-developing classification rather than its present high density residential-established. ("Camera", May 28, Ken Frizell)

Councilman Robert White has been criticized for his involvement in developing the West Pearl area. He pointed out that most of his property was purchased before he was elected to the City Council and that his ownership was listed for all to see.

He said the rezoning was undertaken as a part of the comprehensive plan and that he had nothing to do with the rezoning. ("Town & Country", July 14)

The City Council rejected the administrative plan to rezone a residential area along West Pearl Street. The Council voted to approve the changes relating to commercial and industrial zoning. ("Camera", September 8, Ken Frizell)

In May, the residents of the Goss-Grove area and the city Planning Department published the "Goss-Grove Neighborhood Plan". The Goss-Grove Neighborhood Association was formed to help preserve the area's social and physical characteristics.

Dena Wild, the planning department's neighborhood coordinator,

provided the impetus for the association. She used funds from the Department of Housing and Urban Development to write a pamphlet entitled "Guide to Neighborhood Planning" and the association used it as a survival kit.

As a result of cooperation between the city and the neighborhood, the planning staff and Planning Board approved the comprehensive plan.

Dena Wild is particularly proud of the success of the Goss-Grove Neighborhood Association because it has established a mechanism for cooperation. ("Camera", December 20)

On February 5, the Planning Board approved a special use application requesting that the Boulder Psychiatric Institute (BPI) be allowed to remodel a nursing home at 4390 Baseline. A suit was filed in District Court charging that the city acted improperly in granting the request.

Controversy has surrounded BPI since it sought to open a 50-bed psychiatric care facility late last year.

The Institute filed for its certificate of need after Memorial Hospital said it would not replace its psychiatric unit, Medical B. Medical B is threatened with closure by the Fire Department and is being permitted to operate only until another facility can be opened. ("Town & Country", May 19)

Lawsuits filed against the city and the BPI were dismissed on legal technicalities.

In October, the Boulder Board of Zoning Adjustment was requested to review the zoning regulations in the neighborhood where BPI is located. The property is in the Frasier Meadows subdivision which is zoned high density residential. If the BPI is operated as a "clinic" its use is allowed. BPI was recently granted a hospital operating license by the

State Health Department. It is illegal to operate a hospital in a residential zone, say the opponents. ("Camera", October 28)

The fate of the Flatiron Companies ... plan to mine sand and gravel ... south of Boulder remains uncertain today after an unfavorable ruling ... by the Internal Revenue Service.

The council had approved the project on first reading last December. Second reading approval was delayed pending the IRS ruling. ("Camera", August 5, Ken Frizell)

The Boulder Citizens' Committee on Housing and Community Development recommended that the city allocate \$35,000 for day-care, \$45,000 for Goss-Grove right-of-way acquisition, \$15,000 for a noise control officer, \$47,000 for housing maintenance and \$20,000 for housing rehabilitation administration. Money for the projects is from federal funds. Boulder's share for 1977 is expected to be \$920,000. Of that sum, \$720,000 has already been earmarked for a new senior citizens center. ("Camera", August 6)

#### SHOPPING CENTERS

One result of the city's rejection of shopping centers in the past five years has been to give downtown an edge in completing the mall before facing their competition.

Developers Ralph Biernbaum and Western Industries now have a site plan ... which drew enthusiastic response ... from ... four planning board members.

William Heinzman, the architect for the proposed Arapahoe Village Center ... showed how the land on both sides of Arapahoe between Folsom and 28th Streets with its existing buildings had been assembled and recycled into the regional shopping center.

Heinzman emphasized pedestrian, bicycle and bus access to the center although there are also 2700 parking spaces on the site. (There is) ... even a pedestrian arrival route to the second level of the center crossing the creek and (using) the existing stone terracing on Folsom Hill.

Developers plan to move the center out of the floodway by reconstructing Folsom bridge to pull the floodline back south of the Harvest House. The center still faces the problem of traffic ... ("Colorado Daily", February 27, Jan Tuchman)

The Planning Board approved the Arapahoe Village concept on a five to zero vote. Very few objections were raised, but among the concerns were traffic congestion and air quality impact. ("Camera", March 12, Ken Frizell)

The City Council reaffirmed the past councils' desire to restrict the development of any regional shopping center to within the city west of 30th Street. The vote was necessary because Councilman George Boland charged that the City Manager was setting Council policy when he set the eastern boundaries for shopping center developers. ("Camera", March 3)

Ignoring a planning staff recommendation, the Planning Board extended their approval of the proposed Arapahoe Plaza shopping center until the first of the year.

In March, the board had given Mr. Biernbaum until August to secure commitments from at least two department stores. He failed to do this and requested an extension. ("Camera", September 3, Ken Frizell)

Mr. Frizell reported in the "Camera" of October 6:

What the city of Boulder giveth, the City of Boulder taketh away.

Council voted 8 to 0 ... to void a recent planning board decision and to set Nov. 2 as the date by which Biernbaum ... must show that the (shopping center) project will go on his site.

George Wilson ... announced that he is now dealing with a new development firm, the Taubman Co., ... (and) ... has enlisted the aid of two more local attorneys, George Hollenbeck and Neil King.

Boulder's shopping center sweepstakes lost one of its entrants today with word that Connecticut developer Ralph Biernbaum is pulling out of the race.

" ... Mr. Biernbaum has been unable to obtain a second major department store for the Arapahoe Shopping Center site.

Should the city decide to take an urban renewal approach to assist in obtaining a regional shopping center, Mr. Biernbaum would hope to have the opportunity to be one of the bidders." ("Camera", October 21, Ken Frizell)

In November, the Perl-Mack Companies of Denver proposed a regional shopping center on 85 acres east of the IBM plant. The County

Commissioners unofficially rejected rezoning the land. Boulder Mayor Buchanan said the land in question is just outside the boundary of the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan. County Planning Director Ed Tepe said the "goals and objectives" of the proposed Boulder County Comprehensive Plan are that major commercial activities will be located in urban areas.

"If there's going to be a regional shopping center, it should serve the City of Boulder region, and not act as a catalyst for drawing from both places. Longmont should have its own." ("Longmont Times-Call", November 11, Charles Beal)

Ken Frizell wrote in the "Camera" dated December 14:

George Wilson, ... said ... that a representative for the Taubman Co., the (shopping) center developer, has secured a commitment from May D&F for the 28th and Pearl site. Sears Roebuck & Co. decided on the Wilson location several months ago.

(Nolan) Rosall, who has previously gone on record as supporting the Wilson site ... is expected to urge direct council involvement ... including a possible parking district, economic development bonds and tax increment financing -- an urban renewal concept.

The Crossroads Merchants Association has indicated that they, too, favor Wilson's location for the center because of its potential direct ties to Crossroads.

In the "Camera" of December 15, Steve Hoffman reported:

According to Rosall, the site could be termed a "blighted area" under Colorado law, which means the city, by forming an urban renewal agency, could freeze the existing level of assessed valuation.

The increase in the assessed valuation of the property once the shopping center is completed would generate the necessary revenues to retire the bonds ... .

Rosall, by a "conservative estimate", guessed the city's sales tax revenues would jump about \$1 million a year.

#### DOWNTOWN

At a Council meeting in January, Ruth Correll urged the city's mall design team to include public restrooms in their plan. Her motion failed on a 4 to 4 vote.

The problem of the residents who are caught in the double assessment area of the Downtown Boulder Mall and the street improvement district around Whittier School is being studied by staff.

City Manager Archie Twitchell said the city looked into the possibility of deferring assessments for hardship cases last year, but the firm handling the mall bonds recommended against any deferrals because such a postponement might jeopardize the sale of the bonds.

He said the mall assessments will not be billed until after completion of the mall. ("Camera", March 25, Ken Frizell)

In the "Camera" dated May 19, Ken Frizell wrote:

After months of uncertainty, the Boulder City Council ... approved a deferral program for low income owners of single-family, owner-occupied residences within the Downtown Boulder Mall assessment district.

The program will delay the city's collection of the assessments until such time as the property is sold, transferred or used as other than a residence.

Council also voted 7 to 2 ... to waive the interest on the deferred assessment as well.

Pearl Street from 11th to 15th Street was permanently closed to automobile traffic on June 12, designated as Pearl Street Mall Day.

#### HISTORIC PRESERVATION

The City Council voted unanimously to approve the city's first historic landmark designations. Named as landmarks were the Squires house, the First Congregational Church, the Armory Building, the Boettcher Building, and the Grill Mansion. ("Camera", March 17, Ken Frizell)

In January, Historic Boulder filed an application to have the Allen-Faus house saved from the wrecker's ball. The congregation of the First United Methodist Church had voted to demolish the building. The application provides for delay until a decision on the historic significance of the 1874 house can be made. ("Camera", January 14, Ken Frizell)

The congregation of the church reversed their earlier decision and

said the building should be preserved. In April the Council adopted the historic landmark ordinance for the Allen-Faus house. However, in May, they reversed that decision on second reading because the church wanted to retain the option to move the house from the church property. Without the protection of being a Boulder Landmark, the house could be torn down. ("Camera", May 19, Ken Frizell)

The railroad depot, once threatened with destruction to make way for completion of 14th Street to Canyon, was being refurbished to its former splendor by the Jaycees. The JayCees had moved the building to its present location near the corner of 30th and Pearl Streets. Its exterior is being restored to the way it appeared when it was dedicated on September 26, 1890. Its interior "will be used by civic organizations and will be home to the JayCees." ("Camera", June 13, Barbralu Fried)

In April, the City Council designated Highland School, which is owned by Historic Boulder, as a historic landmark. ("Camera", April 28)

1977

#### PLANNING AND ZONING

In January, the County Commissioners approved a 90-day delay in consideration of subdivisions in order to give the city and county land use planners time to complete the revised Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan. That time was extended another 90 days in April when it became apparent that the plan, then bogged down in legal questions over its key policies, would not be completed during the first moratorium. ("Camera", July 15, John Leach)

During the drafting of the policy section of the plan, County Planning

Director Ed Tepe said the county is confronting intense development pressure, especially in the Gunbarrel area, as a result of the Danish plan.

For Service Area III, the fringe areas of the Boulder Valley, Council adopted City Attorney Wagenhals conclusion that municipal water and sewer services could be denied, but only under the constraints of the Robinson decision.

Wagenhals suggested the city set a policy stating that it expects capital funds available for line extensions will be consumed for projects either within the city limits, Service Area I, or in Service Area II, and that no funds will be available to push the lines farther out into the county. ("Camera", January 5, Steve Hoffman)

As recommended by Ed Tepe, Boulder Planning Board adopted a policy stating that the city will not extend service lines or grant service from existing lines to new urban development outside the city. The 4 to 3 vote came after strong objections from City Attorney Wagenhals who based his opinions of the ruling in the Robinson case. ("Camera", June 3, Steve Hoffman)

The Council approved a compromise worked out by Deputy Mayor Bob Trenka which recognized that though the city may be legally forced to provide water and sewer services, it is Council's intention not to do so.

Subdivision requests submitted to the county prior to June 13 may be "grandfathered in" to the Comprehensive Plan and could result in construction of about 950 residential units outside the city not subject to the Danish plan. ("Camera", June 15, Steve Hoffman)

The County Commissioners ended their 180-day delay in consideration of subdivisions proposed for Boulder Valley a day after the revised comprehensive plan was adopted by the County Long-Range Planning

Commission.

The Boulder Planning Board approved the plan in May and the City Council, in June. The plan is designed to take the county out of the land development business by setting stringent standards for urban services to new subdivisions.

The required services could only be provided by Boulder which would require the developer to annex his property in order to obtain the services. ("Camera", July 15, John Leach)

The County Commissioners adopted the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan unanimously. The year-long process of review by city and county agencies was praised by the League of Women Voters and criticized by PLAN-Boulder County. ("Denver Post", August 16, Jane Cracraft)

The Council reaffirmed an earlier decision to seek petitions of annexation from IBM and owners of about 500 acres of other industrial and commercial property in the Gunbarrel area. ("Camera", March 2, Steve Hoffman)

IBM agreed to annex to the city if they were given industrial zoning on 167 acres southeast of Colorado 119. County officials fear that the zoning will make it more difficult for them to downzone a large property north of Colorado 52 that has been coveted by shopping center developers. ("Camera", June 24, John Leach)

Karl Fettig requested extension of a city water line to his property on North Broadway. His attorney said that Fettig would pay for a looped water line and would be reimbursed by anyone who tapped onto that line. Council was concerned over the possibility that the extension would open up acres of land to development.

At the same meeting, land on Table Mesa Drive was rezoned to allow the

expansion of a King Soopers store, and Council passed a motion that the planned unit development of Sherrelwood existed before the growth limitation ordinance was passed last November and thus was exempt from restrictions on building permits under the Danish plan. ("Camera", July 13, Sue Deans)

The Council canceled a second reading on the extension of water lines to the Fettig property on the advice of the County Planning Department who sent them a letter requesting that the County Commissioners give approval for construction of the line under the authority of a Colorado law. The Fettig request is considered a potential test of the revised Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan. ("Town & Country", December 14)

Were the Fettig request granted, it would violate the timetable set up in the plan for a gradual phasing of urban services into the county.

Residents of the North 26th Street neighborhood asked that the comprehensive plan be amended to lower projected densities for development in the area between 26th and 28th streets. ("Camera", December 14, Steve Hoffman)

The Department of Housing and Urban Development adopted new guidelines for the disbursement of federal funds by the Citizens Committee on Housing and Community Development (CCHCD). The money was to be used more directly to assist those in a low-income category. ("Camera", May 3, Paul I. Johnston)

Better communication was the goal this year for the CCHCD, after most of their recommendations for last year's funds were thrown out by last year's Council. ("Town & Country", May 18, Nan Pirnack)

Although the citizens' committee allocated \$150,000 for the purchase

of Highland School for a community center, the Council turned it down on a 5 to 4 vote. City Manager Westdyke had recommended against buying the school at a time when money for services to Gunbarrel took priority.

Other modifications to the CD proposals were \$100,000 in "extra" money, after the denial of Highland School, was put into the San Juan del Centro neighborhood center and into improvements at Eben Fine Park. ("Camera", July 27, Steve Hoffman)

The CCHCD is a 30-member citizens' group which "has led a voluntary existence nurtured by sheer enthusiasm and strong interest" since its inception in 1974. A new Housing and Community Development Act made this kind of group an integral part of the city's receiving funds. The city wants to restructure the group, reducing its members to 15 with several of those from city departments. The present committee resents the proposal, but will discuss it with city staff. ("Town & Country", October 26, Susan Eastman)

Federal funds from the Economic Development Administration (EDA) were awarded to the city. The Council chambers will get a facelift, there will be new bridges over Boulder Creek at Sixth and Ninth streets, and Gunbarrel will have its own fire station. Funds for Chautauqua improvements, downtown mall restrooms, and street repairs were also approved by the EDA. ("Camera", October 25, Jane Hoback)

The City Council recommended and the County Commissioners approved the issuance of industrial development bonds so Celestial Seasoning Herb Teas can build a new plant. ("Colorado Daily", April 19, Charlotte Koontz)

The Boulder Planning Department is working on a proposal for a design review committee. A 3-member Public Design Review Committee was formed by

Council on March 18, 1971. Their term of office was three years. They have not been called on to review any public project since 1973. ("Town & Country, April 6, Nan Pirnack)

Steve Hoffman reported in the "Camera" of June 14 that District Court Judge Richard Dana ruled against Boulder in its effort to keep the Pow Wow rodeo grounds in a zoning category that would not permit industrial or commercial uses. Pow Wow has been trying to sell the property because they need more space to continue the rodeo operation.

In an article in the "Camera" dated December 13, Steve Hoffman reported:

"The best use for the area is for public recreation, and it has been shown that way on the comprehensive plan since 1957," said (Planning Director Nolan) Rosall.

... Judge Richard Dana ... ruled that the public use zoning denied a "reasonable use" of the land.

Reacting to that decision, the Boulder City Council gave the property a commercial designation in mid-November.

La Belles, a major catalogue store ... signed an agreement with Pow-Wow Inc., that could result in construction of a ... retail outlet on the eight-acre rodeo grounds.

The Planning Board approved rezoning of a church on North Broadway so it may be used by the People's Clinic. ("Colorado Daily", December 2, Stephen Foehr)

The zoning designation given was "public-established" but the People's Clinic is not a government agency. When the Council voted to rezone so the clinic could purchase the church at Broadway and Hawthorne, it had to promise to amend the zoning ordinance so "public-established" can contain quasi-public agencies. ("Denver Post", December 21, Jane Cracraft)

The Boulder Neighborhood League was formed to be a bi-partisan group to back Council candidates and push for a platform of continued growth control, environmental issues and concern for neighborhoods. ("Denver

Post", July 19, Jane Cracraft)

#### SHOPPING CENTERS

Steve Hoffman wrote in the "Camera" dated January 19:

Boulder City Council unanimously approved a new shopping center policy Tuesday night that puts a strong emphasis on keeping major retail development west of 30th Street by holding out the possibility of direct financial aid to private developers.

If the promise of ... assistance ... within that boundary proves an insufficient attraction, however, then council is now prepared to consider sites east of 30th Street "consistent with good land use planning."

Then, by an 8 to 1 vote, Council rejected the proposal by Taubman Co., Sears, and May D&F to assemble a 40 acre site north of Crossroads with the financial assistance of the city. They dismissed the use of tax increment financing because it is based on a new state law as yet untested in the Colorado courts and because the amount of the subsidy was too high.

A new plan for the Arapahoe Village North site owned by the Cohagen family was submitted to the planning director. It involves an expansion of the existing facilities into a "town and country" center without any major retail chain.

In March, the Village Shopping Center, designed by William Heinzman, won unanimous approval from the Planning Board.

In June, it was announced that a retail and commercial complex would be developed on land across 28th Street from the Crossroads Center. Buffalo Village was designed by Junge & Associates of Boulder.

"Camera" staff writer John Leach reported on July 18:

A major developer, Ernest W. Hahn, Inc. and two large department chains, Sears and May D&F, have joined forces to try to get Boulder's elusive regional shopping center off the ground.

The group ... is looking at ... University of Colorado property and two other east Boulder sites ... .

The move comes as the George Wilson shopping center site ... , once considered the ideal west-of-30th Street location, is slowly being dismantled. Part of the property has been purchased by the Dayton-

Hudson Corp. parent company of the Target Stores. Another parcel was acquired from Wilson by National State Bank.

Another investment company announced that it was buying a 114-acre site near the IBM plant for a regional center.

Steve Hoffman wrote in the "Camera" dated September 27:

Over strong objections by its four liberal members, the Boulder City Council voted Tuesday morning to consider proposals for shopping centers on sites east of 30th Street.

The 30th Street boundary had represented a basic land use decision, now a part of the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan, that major new retail development take place as close to the existing core area as possible.

Planning Director Nolan Rosall and City Manager Bob Westdyke told council that it appears highly unlikely that companies such as Sears and May D&F will put money into a shopping center site west of 30th Street or that they would participate in an expansion of the existing Crossroads Shopping Center.

Councilmembers Bob White, Janet Roberts, Ruth Correll and Paul Danish voted against the east of 30th Street policy.

Danish was the most vocal in his objections, claiming that the new policy urged by Rosall and Westdyke should take place only after an amendment is made to the comprehensive plan.

Jane Cracraft wrote in the "Denver Post" of September 28:

On a 5-4 vote, the council voted to "consider" applications for a shopping center in the 600-acre area bounded by 30th St., Valmont Road, 47th St. and Colorado Ave.

A majority of council members agreed they would like to consider some kind of subsidy for (a) major department store to locate in the center of town.

Councilman Wayne Hutchens said he thought "the best of all worlds" would be to locate May D&F and Sears at Crossroads, adding decked parking.

(City Manager) Westdyke disclosed that he and Planning Director Nolan Rosall have gone to representatives of the Crossroads center and urged the owners to expand and upgrade their stores.

"They've not moved, and they have leases for their major stores (The Denver, Penney's and Wards) which give existing leaseholders veto power over new major stores coming on the site," Westdyke said.

In the "Camera" dated September 28, Steve Hoffman wrote that the new proposal for a shopping center on University of Colorado land is located at 47th Street and Arapahoe Road. Any leasing contracts between the University and the developers would very probably be subject to approval by the Board of Regents. The County Commissioners had written to the Council

earlier this month requesting cooperation in amending the comprehensive plan before "any serious consideration of change in location of the regional shopping center."

City Planning Director Rosall said that "changing the (comprehensive) plan now would be premature."

John Leach wrote in the "Camera" dated October 4 that the County Commissioners were concerned over the impacts of building a "Cinderella City" on land at 47th Street. They were "also concerned about the integrity of the comprehensive plan in the eyes of local residents when the city is willing to change it so freely."

Steve Hoffman wrote on October 5 in the "Camera":

Stung by criticisms from the County Commissioners, downtown merchants, Planning Board members and later from angry citizens ... the Boulder City Council Tuesday night backed away from changing its shopping center policies.

The council voted 6 to 3 to postpone until March any further consideration of relaxing a basic land use decision it reaffirmed last January -- that major new retail development take place west of 30th Street, preferably as part of an expansion of the existing Crossroads center.

Then it overturned Mayor Frank Buchanan's ruling that a public hearing on the matter was no longer necessary, and listened to citizen comments for two hours.

(Councilmen) Lefferdink and Boland shifted their votes toward postponement.

#### DOWNTOWN

The grand opening of the downtown mall was on Saturday August 6.

"Camera" writer Thad Keyes reported:

The 1300 block was almost completely filled as local officials dedicated the mall. ... Mayor Frank Buchanan lauded the undertaking as a joint venture between local business, municipal government and citizens.

... (Lane Earnest) ... read a letter from Congressman Tim Wirth ... congratulating Boulder for revitalizing its downtown ... .

The Boulder Centennial Bicentennial Time Capsule ... will be buried near the courthouse and covered with a boulder and plaque. James Carter, a Rocky Flats technician ..., is the man behind the stainless steel capsule which was constructed at the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration.

Included in the capsule (to be opened July 4, 2075) are 1976 election mementos, local newspapers and church and school rosters.

In September, the Council approved an ordinance designed to protect pedestrians on the malls from dogs, frisbees, bicycles and skateboards.

The Mall Review Committee, made up of merchants, businessmen and city officials, gave their approval to an architect's plan for spending \$50,000 in federal funds for a two-stall public restroom. ("Camera", September 9, Steve Hoffman)

Bill Conti reported in the "Camera" of September 17 that a suit against the City of Boulder was filed in District Court by a downtown Boulder restaurant claiming \$2 million in damages. "Altered traffic patterns and the elimination of parking on Pearl Street because of the mall has resulted in a loss of business and revenue by the restaurant, the suit says."

Steve Hoffman wrote in the "Camera" of November 2:

Those who own and occupy a single-family home in the assessment district surrounding the Downtown Boulder Mall got more of a break from the Boulder City Council ... in the form of an expanded payment deferral program.

But churches, the council decided on an equally narrow 5 to 4 vote, will have to pay their share on time.

The council's actions ... expanded a deferral program already in effect for low-income homeowners to include all owner-occupants of such dwellings regardless of financial status.

#### HISTORIC PRESERVATION

In January the Council passed a series of ordinances on second reading designating as historic landmarks the Earl-Greene house, St. John's Episcopal Church, the Temple-Bowron house, the McKenzie house, the Carnegie Library, and the Chautauqua Auditorium. ("Camera", January 19, Steve Hoffman)

In April, these were joined by the Boulderado Hotel, and in November by the McAllister house and a historic district bounded by 15th and 16th streets, Mariposa and Bluebell.

In the "Camera" of February 1, Linda Cornett reported that the members of the First United Methodist Church voted to sell and move the Allen-Faus house to make room for a small park for church activities.

Prissy Bowron, president of Historic Boulder, has announced that Highland School has been sold to Economics Research Inc. which will convert the building to office space. The building had been offered to the city but the Council decided on a 5 to 4 vote that it would be too expensive to remodel and maintain. "The school was declared surplus by the Boulder Valley Schools about five years ago because of declining enrollment and a need for renovation." ("Denver Post", August 1, Jane Cracraft)

1978

### PLANNING AND ZONING

The Council adopted on second reading the ordinance rezoning the Broadway Baptist Church to permit its use by the People's Clinic.

They adopted a motion permitting the Alaya Pre-School to locate in a neighborhood zoned for low-density residential use.

Council will consider putting a moratorium on construction projects in the Mapleton Hill area. ("Camera", February 8)

A Mapleton Hill Neighborhood Association has been trying to downzone high-density designations in their area for five years. They wanted them as medium-density on the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan (BVCP) last

summer but were told to wait until the plan was passed and then minor zoning changes would be permitted.

Planning Board member Wally Palmer, who is planning to construct a 13-unit townhouse at Ninth and Maxwell, was requested to postpone his building in a spirit of fairness. Mr. Palmer already has his building permit and has no legal problem with starting construction. ("Camera", February 19, Steve Hoffman)

The Council unanimously adopted the downzoning ordinance, and Planning Board Chairman Bill Ganter requested the resignation of board member Palmer. However, only the Council can remove one of its own appointees. ("Camera", March 17, Steve Hoffman)

The moratorium ordinance was rejected by the Council on a 5 to 4 vote.

The townhouse development got started before any action was taken on the moratorium. ("Camera", April 12, Steve Hoffman)

Two weeks after granting the Alaya Pre-School request, the Council rescinded its vote under pressure from the neighborhood. On a third vote, they again granted a special use permit. ("Colorado Daily", March 1, Stephen Foehr)

Dissatisfaction of North 26th Street residents with the Winding Trails Village development has lessened. The proposal asks for annexation and rezoning for an area just south of the Elks Club. The developer reduced the total number of units, agreed to primary access focused on 28th Street, and will buffer the new development with landscaping and fences.

Wells are the only problem left. Residents fear the construction of the 269 units will have a tendency to deplete the natural groundwater levels. ("Town & Country", May 17)

One frustrated University Hill resident smashed a wine bottle on the floor of the City Manager's office in order to get some attention for his neighborhood.

A member of the University Hill League said the Hill is suffering from serious problems. He said the biggest concern is the city's "lack of zoning enforcement" -- an inability or unwillingness to control the number of unrelated persons living in rental housing. ("Camera", September 18, Ken Frizell)

The Boulder Neighborhood League wants the city to keep its current occupancy ordinance and enforce it by penalizing the landlords.

Recognizing that the rental situation in Boulder forces high occupancy, League members are considering applying pressure on the University to build additional on-campus housing units.

Currently only 26 percent of single students and 31 percent of married students can find room in University housing. ("Camera", September 19, Marilyn Webb)

Linda Cornett reported in the "Camera" on December 8:

Boulder's Planning Board rejected a unique zoning proposal for the Goss-Grove neighborhood, urging the neighborhood and the planning staff to try again.

The Goss/Grove rezoning has been developed over the past four years ... . A first proposal, called Pyramid Density, would have tied the size of new development to the density of surrounding neighbors. The Planning Board approved that concept last year but the trade-off which would have allowed densities up to 40 units per acre in some areas "freaked" residents and the plan was abandoned.

The proposal designed in its place encourages single lot development to guard against massive apartment buildings, decreases the number of units which can be built on some lots from four to three and requires that each project over three units face special review.

The proposal is the first zoning classification designed specifically for one neighborhood.

Acting Planning Director Ed Gawf said his staff could not support the new proposal because an existing zoning classification contains most of its

elements and some sections need further study.

Members of the committee which drew up the zoning ordinance (expressed) dismay at the denial ....

A less controversial proposal to rezone a portion of the Whittier neighborhood from high to medium density was passed on to the council on a 3-3 vote.

Three 28th Street hotels were cited for violation of the sign code and were convicted in Municipal Court. They appealed to County Court where there was a new trial.

County Judge Marsha Yeager ruled that the city has a right to restrict signs. She said the city's sign code does not infringe on the rights of free speech. ("Denver Post", January 25)

A group of neighborhood residents filed suit in Boulder District Court charging that the Council exceeded its authority in changing the zoning of the Broadway Baptist Church so it could be used by the People's Clinic. ("Camera", March 7)

The Planning Board voted 4 to 3 to allow the People's Clinic to move into the former church. It also permitted the Clinic to rent space in a building just north of the church to four other non-profit agencies. ("Camera", March 17, Steve Hoffman)

District Court Judge Murray Richtel upheld the zoning change saying it does comply with the Comprehensive Plan and is not an abuse of the city's power. Judge Richtel indicated that the city was promoting the general welfare of the community. ("Camera", June 16, Sharon Massingill)

The Boulder County Commissioners unanimously approved and adopted the revised 1977 Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan.

The revisions include definitions of "urban development", methods of implementation, and a Water Utility Master Plan. The agreement also states

that both county and city must modify their planning, zoning, subdivision and related functions "in a manner consistent" with the plan. ("Longmont Times-Call", June 13, Tim Alger)

The Pow Wow grounds have been purchased by La Belle's catalog store which hopes to build a small shopping center on the 8.3 acre site. ("Camera", June 7, Linda Cornett)

Susan Eastman reported in the "Town & Country" on November 8:

The Boulder Pow-Wow Inc. request for a new home went before the Boulder City Council ... for approval as a special use review ... .

Desiring 52 acres of land adjacent to the Longmont Diagonal and 55th Street, the request was denied by the Open Space Board of Trustees Oct. 11 when they decided it would not be compatible with the land's open space designation on the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan.

The City Real Estate Services Office recommended denial of the special use review because it lies in Area III of the Comp Plan ... .

The Planning Department sent a memo to city council pointing out that Pow-Wow's out-of-city water and sewer service request can not be approved under current ... policies and ordinances.

After the Pow Wow said it would reduce the amount of building on the site, move structures farther from the Diagonal, and include an open space dedication to the city, the Council went with a motion to postpone action. ("Camera", November 8, Linda Cornett)

In the "Camera" dated November 30, Chip Howard wrote:

In a case of give-and-take, Boulder Pow Wow board members and the city's Open Space Board of Trustees compromised their divergent positions on use of 52 acres of open land along the Longmont Diagonal.

... the Pow Wow representatives agreed to let Boulder acquire the development rights to about 40 of the acres.

Acquisition of development rights ... would allow the city to limit use of the parcel to temporary parking, grazing and horseback riding, thus bringing it into compliance with its designation as open space on the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan.

The plan leaves the Pow Wow board with about 12 acres on which to build its ... outdoor and indoor arenas and a barn.

The Planning Board defended the principle of open space and, despite recommendations for approval by the planning staff, again voted to deny Pow Wow's request for the land. ("Colorado Daily", December 1)

Linda Cornett reported in the "Camera" of December 6:

Boulder's City Council surprised a lot of folks ... when it approved plans for the Boulder Pow Wow to move onto a 52-acre site ... .

The matter now goes to the County Planning Board and the County Commissioners for their approval.

The Wonderland Hill Development ... has been planned under a set of city land use regulations that allow community residents a strong voice about what kind of non-residential uses are permitted in the midst of their homes.

The rules governing such a project, called a Planned Unit Development (PUD), permit some commercial uses ... , but only for the convenience of the people living there. The concept is to reduce unnecessary auto traffic.

Under the original plans ... (the developers) had in mind a small convenience store and a day care center ... .

But, primarily because those uses were felt not to be economically viable, they asked Planning Board to permit the construction of a 48-seat pub and two racquet ball courts instead.

... concerns (of the residents) led ... to the rejection by the city Planning Board. ("Camera", January 13, Steve Hoffman)

John Leach wrote in the January 15th "Camera":

City and county officials are exploring the possible withdrawal of Boulder County from the Denver Regional Council of Governments.

The county and its cities have been in DRCOG since its formation in 1968, but recent disagreements over wastewater and housing plans have caused them to reconsider their membership.

Sherry Hodges of the "Longmont Times-Call" reviewed the development of regional planning agencies on February 6.

As early as 1939, when the metro population hit the 445,000 mark, Denver and its surrounding counties joined together in the Upper Platte Regional Planning Commission.

The group gave way to the Tri-County Planning Commission in 1944, after several counties dropped out ... , leaving an association of Adams, Arapahoe and Jefferson Counties.

As the area's population soared past the 750,000 mark in 1955, the tri-county group was joined by ... Denver in the Inter-County Regional Planning Commission (ICRPC). Boulder County joined that group in 1966.

Composed of citizens, rather than elected officials, from each of its member units, the ICRPC became DRCOG in 1968. DRCOG replaced the ICRPC's lay representatives with a governing group of locally elected officials from member jurisdictions.

It was ... primarily a creation of the federal government, responsible for conducting regional studies and for reviewing almost all local applications for federal funds.

The Citizens' Committee on Housing and Community Development allocated funds in 1978 to Goss/Grove street improvements, land purchase for low-income housing, downtown parks and other projects. The People's Clinic was awarded \$14,800 for renovations on the North Broadway Baptist Church. A grant of \$15,000 went to continued rehabilitation of housing in the core area. ("Camera", February 16, Kevin Swan)

The Planning Board unanimously approved the plans for redevelopment of the old Arnett Hotel, built in 1874. The new structure will exceed the height limitation regulations and will require some modifications to its set-back and open space requirements. ("Camera", March 3, Steve Hoffman)

Nolan Rosall will leave his position as planning director to go to work for a local architectural firm on July 1. ("Camera", May 3)

In a 5 to 2 vote, the Planning Board agreed with neighborhood residents that the scale and orientation of the proposed Willow Springs II subdivision would have to be changed before it would be acceptable. A neighborhood shopping facility is allowed in a high density residential area. However, the proposed 75,000 square feet of commercial and office space is probably designed to serve automobile shoppers and not just neighbors. ("Camera", September 8, Linda Cornett)

At a "town meeting", held by the Council and the planning board, speakers strongly opposed changes in the zoning code that would allow more unrelated persons in single-family residences. Others presented petitions against the "superblock" shopping center proposal, and residents of a 21-unit apartment house on Harvard Lane urged that their building not be traded for land in North Boulder. ("Denver Post", October 27, Jane

Cracraft)

The Council approved an agreement with local developers which will allow them to buy city-owned property at Canyon Boulevard and Ninth Street for a condominium, office and retail complex.

The firm is already constructing a housing complex for elderly and handicapped residents on the same site. ("Camera", November 8, Linda Cornett)

Frank Gray, head of planning in Petaluma, California was named Boulder's new planning director. Growth control was a major criterium in the search, and Boulder's Danish plan is based on the Petaluma plan. ("Camera", November 21, Linda Cornett)

#### SHOPPING CENTERS

In April, the Denver group which had purchased 114 acres of farmland so they could build a Westglen Shopping Center, approached people in the Niwot area and offered to create a city for them.

The response was negative. The Niwot Civic Association began circulating a petition asking that the city of Boulder and the county government, the original owners of the land (the One-Fifty Corporation) and Westglen Venture not allow a regional shopping center on the site. The petition also asks that the city and county approve a contract as part of implementing the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan, that firmly establishes the boundaries beyond which Boulder will not annex. ("Camera", April 6, Steve Hoffman)

In October, a group of Gunbarrel Estates residents urged incorporation of an area east of the Longmont Diagonal. One of the first acts of the proposed new town "would be to re-zone the Perl-Mack property from light

industrial to commercial, to allow construction of a shopping park."

To bring the incorporation issue to a vote requires the signatures of at least 150 registered voters who are property owners in the area to be incorporated. An election for incorporation must be held within 90 days of the granting of a petition for an election. ("Longmont Times-Call", October 14, Pat Jorgenson)

The Perl-Mack Companies of Denver presented area residents with its plan to incorporate Gunbarrel Estates and Cottonwood Park. A consultant for the company admitted there would be no reason for incorporation without the proposed shopping center.

County officials told residents they would fight the development as being incompatible with the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan. Jason Brouillette, assistant Boulder County planner, said the county intends to downzone the area early next year.

If the major department stores locate in either Boulder or Longmont, the shopping center debate would be over. ("Colorado Daily", November 2, Stephen Foehr)

Several weeks ago Jack Jacobs and Company approached the city with a proposal for a shopping center to be built in the two block area of Canyon, 11th, Ninth and Walnut streets.

The City Manager asked Council for some authorization before continuing discussions with Jacobs. Because of the expense of downtown land, the city will be asked to come up with money for the project.

Planning Director Nolan Rosall told the Council that if they really want the regional center west of 30th Street, they must form a partnership with the developer.

The Council indicated a willingness to consider the proposal and asked

staff to explore ways of financing the city's share.

Roadblocks to the center would still be traffic impact. And one piece of property is due to be issued a building permit for a new restaurant-office complex on the site of the former Foodbank Lockers. ("Camera", June 29, Ken Frizell)

By July, construction had begun on the restaurant and office building.

In an effort to clear some negotiating room, the City Council ordered city Real Estate Director Don Walker to attempt to negotiate a sale or trade for the ... site.

At the same time, the city attorney's office has been told to look at the possibility of condemning the site, if necessary, to hold the superblock together.

A Minnesota developer contacted the city and promised a better financial deal than that offered by the Jacobs Company. ("Camera", July 19, Linda Cornett)

Cornett reported in the August 2 "Camera":

A memo released Monday reported owners of the (restaurant) site agreed, under threat of condemnation, to sell for \$675,000.

... construction on the site is continuing and so costs ... are also going up.

... Ericson (the new entrant) indicated to the city he would be willing to put up a portion of the purchase price for that property.

In a meeting with Council in August, Orrin Ericson said his center could be built around the restaurant and office building.

City Manager Westdyke said the Canyon Inn property might be available for about \$1 million.

Ericson assured the Council he could put together the land for the center which "could cost between \$25 and \$29 million; the city would be required to put up about \$4 million for a portion of the parking." ("Camera", August 4, Linda Cornett)

At a meeting in September, the Ericson Development Company presented sketches and a model of the structure they hope to build on the superblock. The public raised questions about the traffic impact of the center, parking

spaces, its size and height, and possible flood hazards. There were also objections to the city's subsidizing the developer. ("Camera", September 28, Linda Cornett)

Objections were repeated at another meeting in October, but the city's planning staff believes "the proposal could be successfully integrated into a downtown context." Richard Foy, president of the Downtown Business Association, reminded the Council that the DBA "has consistently stood in favor of locating a major shopping complex in the core area." And Rod Benson called the center a "rejuvenating" source of tax revenue. ("Camera", October 4, Linda Cornett)

At an October meeting, only two Council members flatly rejected the Ericson plans, but all nine agreed that Ericson should cut the size by about 40,000 square feet. ("Camera", October 14, Linda Cornett)

Councilman Greg Lefferdink responded to a revised plan presented by Ericson by saying the reduction would not change his vote.

If the revised dimensions do not satisfy the council, Ericson said, "we are at a point where we would have to drop the development ... ."
