Ericson indicated the reduction in size would be used to bring the center in from surrounding streets rather than lowering its height.

Acting Planning Director Ed Gawf raised another issue on the center -- "The big question now is, will the majors (department stores) accept it." ("Camera", October 17, Linda Cornett)

Sharon Massingill wrote in the "Camera" dated October 22 that petitions were being circulated in protest of the superbloc shopping center. If the Council approves the center, the petitions will be used to put the issue before the voters.

In the "Denver Post" of November 17, Jane Cracraft reported:

The Boulder Chamber of Commerce Thursday effectively vetoed developer Orrin Ericson's plans for a downtown "superblock" shopping center between Ninth and 11th streets along Canyon Boulevard.

The chamber not only took a stand against the ... center, but also announced that it wouldn't sell its ... land at 1001 Canyon Boulevard.

The chamber's building site sits in the middle of the proposed shopping center.

The Chamber would prefer development of a multi-purpose center on the site including retail and office space, a hotel and a performing arts center. " ... Ericson insisted he was still interested in the proposal, that he was willing to wait for the city to take some action or for a change of heart on the part of the chamber."

The city may take action, but it might be a fight to discourage a shopping center rather than to decide to build one.

A week after the death of the superbloc center, Jack Jacobs, who was replaced by Ericson in that sweepstakes, announced his plans to construct a regional center near Louisville. City of Boulder officials have met to discuss methods of preventing that development. ("Camera", December 10, Linda Cornett)

Gloria Bucco reported in the "Camera" of December 1:

Plans for a huge regional shopping center here (in Louisville) were unveiled Thursday by a Chicago developer, drawing a mixed and sometimes bitter response from Boulder officials.

The Centennial Fashion Mall would take up 160 acres ... at the northeast corner of the Superior interchange of the Boulder-Denver Turnpike.

The center will comprise 750,000 to one million square feet, according to Louisville City Administrator Leon Wurl.

Ed Gawf, Boulder's acting planning director, called the proposed center "a land use disaster" ...

Boulder Councilman Bob White ... discounted the notion that the Louisville plan might inspire the council to reconsider the policy not to locate a center east of 30th Street which has made it so difficult for potential developers to come up with a feasible plan.

"The thing that is the most disappointing is that this city can't get its head together and support anything. We seem only to be able to oppose things," he said.

"We also have council members who think everything stinks and who don't leave us with any opportunity to have a united front."

In the "Colorado Daily" dated December 14, Stephen Foehr wrote:

Political intrigue has swirled through city hall in the past 48 hours as Mayor Ruth Correll has been twisting the arms of recalcitrant council members to get support for her plan to entice Sears Roebuck & Co. to

locate a store in Boulder.

But, Council members Trenka, Boland, Lefferdink, Dooley and Hutchens "did not budge from their positions."

Some of the Council were opposed to any attempt to block the Louisville center. George Boland said, "This city has been playing around with shopping centers for 10 years and has screwed it up to the point where Louisville has it." Wayne Hutchens believes "They have as much right to a shopping center as we do."

Boland, Lefferdink and Dooley do not support subsidizing of private enterprise. Bob White is out of town.

Mayor Correll called Governor Lamm to notify him that Boulder might ask for state intervention to stop the Louisville center.

She said she does not intend for the city to "give the store away", but pointed out that the city was talking about \$600,000 assistance to the downtown proposal and believes that in order to lure a major store into Boulder west of 30th, a rather large carrot may be necessary. ("Camera", December 14, Linda Cornett)

City Manager Bob Westdyke warned that it may be too late for Boulder officials to do anything, but even so urged the City Council ... to change its restrictive shopping center policy.

The members of the council voted 6-2 to encourage the development of a 400,000 square-foot shopping center in the vicinity of 47th Street between Pearl Street and Valmont Road ... .

Westdyke said the proposed million-square-foot shopping center at Louisville ... "is going to affect us a whole lot worse than what I am proposing."

Councilman Paul Danish said he felt that he was being "blackmailed" and forced by the threat of development at Louisville to support a shopping center in Boulder. ("Denver Post", December 21, Jane Cracraft)

Linda Cornett wrote in the "Camera" dated December 28:

Boulder Mayor Ruth Correll has sent a letter to the president of Sears protesting the company's interest in a planned Louisville shopping center ... .

Correll acknowledged the complaints of Sears real estate scouts who have been attempting to slip a store into the Boulder area for more than 10 years.

"It is true that the city maintained a position which Sears and other major retailers have found difficult, if not impossible, to accomodate," Correll admits.

However, she continues, "it is now apparent to the community, both its business and environmental groups, that an accommodation must be made in order to forestall the land use disaster" of the Centennial Fashion Mall.

#### DOWNTOWN

In January, an eviction notice was served by the owners of the Colorado Building at 1919 14th St. on Joslin's, Boulder's only major downtown department store. Joslin's filed suit to halt its eviction from the space it has occupied since 1956.

In July, the District Court ruling upheld the eviction notice, and the store appealed.

The suit centered on a dispute over whether the lessor or the lessee was responsible for installing the automatic sprinkler system needed to bring the store into compliance with the city's Life-Safety Code. ("Camera", July 6, Sharon Massingill)

The parties resolved the matter out of court. Both would pay for the sprinkler system, but the annual rent would double. ("Camera", August 3, Sarah Hoover and Sharon Massingill) (Joslin's moved out of downtown in 1979.)

District Court Judge Murray Richtel ruled in March that the issue raised by the \$2 million lawsuit against the city and Council members who had approved the Downtown Boulder Mall had already been decided in the city's favor by the state Supreme Court in an earlier case.

John Leach wrote in the "Camera" of April 14:

The "Taj Mahal of restrooms" under construction on the Downtown Mall is the topic of much discussion ... . Most of the comments seem to be negative. A few are obscene.

At first, no restrooms were planned for the four-block mall ... .

City officials believed store owners would open their restrooms to shoppers.

But, following complaints from the County Board of Health and the City Human Relations Commission, city officials agreed to provide restrooms.

They insisted, however, that the facility be built so that it does not invite assaults, fits in with the architecture of the mall and can withstand the vandalism that has damaged many mall fixtures.

Cost overruns, blamed on the enlargement of the project and federal red tape, pushed ... (the cost) ... to the present \$67,000.

The Economic Development Administration paid for \$50,000 of that figure and the city added the remainder to complete "the County Seat".

In May, the weight of snow from a spring storm caved in the roof of the 106-year-old Arnett Hotel building in the 1000 block of Pearl Street. In 1969, it had been Keller's Inn Restaurant and Lounge, and most recently been known as Shannon's. ("Camera", May 7, Ken Frizell)

Sarah Hoover reported in the "Camera" of August 25 that businesses on the mall realized a 40 percent increase in sales tax collections in 1977 over 1976. Transients and parking were the chief concerns of the business community.

Linda Cornett reported in the "Camera" of September 20 that when the city made arrangements to grant families with low incomes or very large assessments a tax deferral in the Downtown Mall and the Whittier neighborhood, it did not include provisions for organizations who may fit the same criteria.

Three churches in the Whittier neighborhood raised the question and Council took action that will allow organizations defined as low-income, or those caught in two assessment districts to pay off their taxes over a longer period of time at low interest or to defer one assessment for several years.

#### HISTORIC PRESERVATION

Historic Boulder helped research and write the Historic Preservation ordinance under which the Landmarks Preservation Advisory Board was formed. The Board reviews applications for designation of historic buildings and districts. If a building fits the criteria, its exterior cannot be changed without permission from the Board. The criteria used are historical significance, architectural significance and environmental significance.

The Board works closely with Historic Boulder, and that organization is encouraging the designation of Chautauqua as a historic district. The downtown area is also being considered as a possibility.

Historic Boulder, Inc. started in a crisis situation, according to Joyce Davies, executive director of the group concerned with preservation of historic buildings.

"In 1972, Central School, Highland School and the old railroad depot were going to be demolished. Central School was ... , but Highland School was saved by being purchased by Historic Boulder and the railroad depot was saved by being moved to another location," Davies said.

... one of the functions of Historic Boulder is to provide a feeling for heritage. ("Camera", February 19, Sara Byrnes)

Chautauqua Park was accepted on the register of historic districts by the National Trust for Historic Places. Renovation projects are under way on the dining hall and the auditorium under federal grants administered by the city.

Historic Boulder has signed a contract to purchase the Culver-Bixby house at 17th Street and Canyon Boulevard. ("Town & Country, May 10, Phyllis Smith)

Linda Cornett reported in the "Camera" of December 15:

Historic Boulder is in the process of nominating the downtown area -- generally between 9th and 16th streets and Walnut and Spruce -- to the National Register of Historic Places.

(Joyce Davies) ... said the national designation, which puts a fairly light hand on remodeling or demolition within the district, may be a step toward designation as a local landmark district. The local designation provides more controls on alterations within the district.

### PLANNING AND ZONING

Sue Deans reported in the "Camera" dated May 9:

Construction at the Gold Run apartments in Sherrelwood Estates, along the creek between 28th and 30th streets, was halted last weekend by an emergency order from Gov. Dick Lamm.

Lamm and the state Land Use Commission have intervened because they say the apartments pose "a significant risk of serious danger of injury, loss or damage to the public health."

... the city cannot take independent action ... "as such action is precluded by an existing consent decree and court order presently under appeal ... ."

The project was originally approved in 1969, before the city's 1974 flood plain regulations went into effect.

When construction was not completed in 1974, the city filed suit against developer Jack Fowler.

But District Judge Richard Dana ruled in favor of Fowler, saying the city's original approval was valid. The city has appealed that decision ... .

The Colorado Court of Appeals ruled June 21 that the apartment project does not need to meet the city's newer, more stringent flood plain requirements. ("Camera", July 19, Sue Deans)

The Colorado Land Use Commission decided that a flood hazard exists at the Sherrelwood development. The City Council decided to appeal the flood plain case to the Colorado Supreme Court. ("Denver Post", August 22, Jane Cracraft)

In a meeting with representatives of the city and the developer in September, Governor Lamm asked that they try to reach some sort of compromise.

Assistant City Attorney Jane Greenfield worked out conceptual guidelines for an agreement between the city and Jack Fowler which the Council approved. She said, "it would definitely benefit the city" to reach a compromise rather than keep on with litigation.

Utilities Director Roger Hartman said the Urban Drainage and Flood

Control District had refused to help fund bridge improvements on the 28th, 30th and Folsom Street bridges unless the Sherrelwood buildings were removed from the floodway.

The proposed bridge improvements, a flood control wall along the north side of the apartment buildings, and stream channelization would remove Cordry Court, Sherrelwood and Crossroads from the flood plain. ("Camera", October 17, Sue Deans)

The agreement includes a \$130,000 payment to Jack Fowler to settle a lawsuit over a sewer easement which the developer claims delayed his project and made design changes necessary. It also includes the city's taking portions of the backyards of six residents in Cordry Court for a flood channel. ("Camera", December 19, Sue Deans)

The County Parks and Open Space Advisory Committee voted 8 to 4 to support the County Planning Commission which had denied the Pow Wow a special use permit for the site at 55th Street and the Longmont Diagonal. ("Camera", March 17)

The Boulder County Commissioners approved a special use permit for the new rodeo grounds, but denied an indoor arena. The rodeo is obviously compatible to a rural area, but the activities planned for the indoor arena included some that were more urban in nature, requiring better public facilities including water, sewer and police and fire protection. Boulder City Council would not authorize utility extension to the site. ("Denver Post", March 30, Jane Cracraft)

In addition to the denial of the indoor arena, other conditions were tacked on to the tentative approval, such as requiring Pow Wow to provide road improvements, landscaping, and utilities.

Marvin Sparn, Pow Wow board member, told the commissioners the arena

is essential, but they voted 2-1 to refuse the permit, so the Boulder Pow Wow is again a rodeo without a home. ("Camera", June 26, Marty Dee)

Community Development funds approved for 1979, recommended by the Citizens' Committee, include \$68,000 to provide a refuge for battered women, \$330,000 to complete street and utility improvements for the Goss-Grove neighborhood, \$125,000 for improvements to low-income housing, \$57,000 to develop a 2-acre park in the Whittier neighborhood and \$150,000 to purchase 12 acres of land for city-owned low-income housing. ("Camera", July 19, Linda Cornett)

The Whittier neighborhood was re-zoned from high to medium density established between 14th and 16th streets on Mapleton and between 16th and 21st streets on Pine Street. Lots on Spruce from 17th to 20th went from high to medium density redeveloping. The area is much smaller than the original request for downzoning, which has gone through three years of modification and public discussion. The matter came before Council because of a 3-3 vote by the Planning Board. ("Camera", January 17, Linda Cornett)

The third and last major re-zoning in the downtown area was completed when the Council downzoned the Highland Lawn neighborhood from established high density to redeveloping medium density. The area is on Marine Street between 6th and 9th Streets. ("Camera", February 7, Linda Cornett)

The City Council approved amendments to the land use regulations. A new type of dwelling unit called "limited living unit" which may include bath and kitchen facilities but is limited to 350 square feet is now allowable in single-family zones. A new quad dwelling unit also increases the possibilities for single housing. In these, four residents each would have separate living and sleeping quarters and access to a central kitchen.

The new amendments may help meet the demands of Boulder's large single population. ("Camera", April 20, Karen Chaney)

The Planning Board has approved a planned unit development which allows Memorial Hospital to build a new wing and retirement housing for 62 persons. The 1918 wing of the hospital, condemned by the Fire Department, would be torn down and replaced by the new wing. It would include a therapy pool available to all handicapped people in the community. The hospital also wants the Council to rezone part of their land so they may construct a building to house 15 doctors. ("Camera", April 29, Sue Deans)

The Council denied the rezoning by not moving approval of the zoning ordinance. ("Camera", May 2, Sue Deans)

The Planning Department and the Planning Board denied six major changes proposed for the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan. Four of these were attempts to move parcels of land from Area III to Area II. The other two came from the city to change the Boulder Airport and Boulder Reservoir in the same manner. ("Camera", October 19, Sue Deans)

A project approved in 1977 finally was given the go-ahead by Council. Speakers expressed their exasperation at the long delay in building two "pocket parks" on Fifth Street south of Pearl and at the intersection of Spruce, Fifth, and Mountain View. Both parks will be on closed-off street rights-of-way.

The CD Committee had allocated \$47,000 for the project with 1978 funds, but modification in the plans plus inflation, had increased the projected cost to \$65,000. ("Camera", November 14, Sue Deans)

#### SHOPPING CENTERS

Sears, Roebuck and Company replied to Mayor Correll's letter saying that "our proposed plan (to locate in Louisville) appears to be a viable business proposal which has been developed after many years of negotiations and rejection with the City of Boulder."

The Council had asked city staff to compile a list of potential obstacles to the Louisville mall. Acting City Attorney Steve Barnett named several possibilities. Among them was the process of acceptance of the plans by the Environmental Protection Agency, which is a prerequisite to acquiring federal funds to expand utility services and to improve the Superior interchange.

The mall plan would be subject to review under the Regional Water Quality Plan and the state's Land Use Commission. The mall site may be located in areas designated as mineral resource, or "hazard areas", where underground mining has occurred in the past.

Also, the plan may be in conflict with the Boulder County, the Louisville, and the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plans. ("Camera", January 3, Linda Cornett)

In the "Camera" of January 9, Linda Cornett reported:

A preliminary poll of the City Council shows reluctant support for one more last-ditch attempt to lure Sears and a regional shopping center back to Boulder -- this one offering a site just below Davidson Mesa at the south entrance of Boulder.

City Council members met individually with (City Manager) Westdyke and Mayor Ruth Correll ... to hear the proposal and register an informal vote on whether the site should be seriously considered.

... councilman George Boland ... walked out of the session. ... Boland said, "I wouldn't have anything to do with that proposal." He pointed to the often bitter arguments which raged back in 1975 when Flatiron proposed to mine the area. Environmentalists complained that the mining operation would ruin the land and destroy the scenic entrance to the city.

The proposal would require major modifications of the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan since the area was never considered for commercial

development.

Jack Jacobs & Co. released a letter charging that the City Council violated state law and the City Charter by holding secret sessions. The mayor and the city attorney were confident that no illegal meetings were held. Neither the Charter nor the Public Meetings Law define what constitutes a meeting. ("Camera", January 15, Linda Cornett)

In the "Colorado Daily" dated February 21, Melinda Green wrote:

In a surprise move, the Boulder City Council unanimously agreed that the rejuvenation of the Crossroads Shopping area was the best plan to stop the proposed Louisville Mall.

The council directed City Manager Bob Westdyke to devote all time and energy of the staff to composing a plan for the expansion and revitalization of the area bounded by 28th, 30th, Arapahoe and Pearl streets..

The decision is based on a letter from General Growth Development Corporation, representing May D&F, and indications from other major department stores that they would prefer the regional shopping center to be located in Boulder.

Linda Cornett reported in the "Camera" of January 21:

The proposal (to offset the announcement that Sears will go to Louisville, is to) -- turn Crossroads Shopping Center and the surrounding strip into a second core area.

"When the downtown was in trouble, we made it better than it was" with a successful pedestrian mall, City Manager Bob Westdyke said. "It's consistent, with the advent of the Louisville center, to go out to Crossroads and take care of that area."

The City Council may be ready to work together on shoring the city's economy, but they are far from decided on whether to go after the Louisville site.

Westdyke has asked the county's planning commission to consider whether it should involve the state in a review of the ... site.

The County Long Range Planning Commission decided a stand on the issue now would be premature. ("Longmont Times-Call" February 8, Tim Alger)

Councilman Bob White, who had proposed the rejuvenation of Crossroads, urged the city to "maximize traffic accessibility and flow to and through the area". He also proposed that Council, if necessary, condemn land in the Crossroads area to assure its development. ("Longmont Times-Call", February 21, Jolaine Freitas)

May D&F has put the preparations for revitalization of Crossroads under a deadline of April 15 at which time the department store will decide whether to join Sears at the Centennial Valley site.

City Planning Director Frank Gray is putting together a team to work full time on the project. Early plans include Crossroads, WoolCo and the Village Shopping area. They are talking about the possibility of a one-way couplet on 28th and 30th streets and on Canyon and Arapahoe, and a mono-rail system between Crossroads and downtown.

The element that gives this effort more teeth than past plans to expand retail space in the area, Gray said, is the power of condemnation and the authority to form a redevelopment district with city and business involvement.

The mechanism for forming a redevelopment authority ... has existed in the city for some time, Gray said. At present, the city's Planning Board sits as such an agency, but it is little more than a "paper tiger". However, by simply making appointments to a redevelopment board and defining the project area, the authority is in business ... . ("Camera", February 25, Linda Cornett)

A high-level team of experts in transportation, economics, urban planning and redevelopment finance will be coordinated by the Carl Worthington Partnership. They will attempt to meet the target date of April 15 for completion of the study. ("Camera", March 6, Linda Cornett)

Council approved \$60,000 for the "fast-track" study and took the amount from the contingency fund built into the city budget.

The City Council created the Boulder Urban Renewal Authority as an emergency measure on March 13, and named seven Boulder residents as its commissioners. To create the authority, Council had to agree that the city has areas of blight. The term may be applied because of transportation problems, inefficient use of land, and deterioration and title problems that might strangle development. ("Camera", March 14, Linda Cornett)

The city scheduled a joint public meeting of the Council, the Planning Board, the Task Force and the Urban Renewal Authority for March 28 to

address such topics as transportation, neighborhoods, land use and flood control. ("Camera", March 27)

The primary focus of preliminary proposals concentrated on transportation and how to phase out 28th Street if the entire area can be involved in redevelopment. ("Camera", March 27, Linda Cornett)

In the "Camera" of April 17, Linda Cornett reported:

The Crossroads area ... plan cleared two hurdles Monday on its way to tonight's crucial Boulder City Council vote.

The plan ... won unanimous support from the city's Urban Renewal Authority Monday afternoon.

Four hours later the Planning Board agreed unanimously that the redevelopment fits in with the valley's comprehensive plan.

And a redevelopment task force came up with a revised financing scheme it hopes will work both politically and economically.

According to the proposal, the renewal authority would issue bonds to be repaid from increases in property and sales taxes in the area as it is redeveloped.

The City Council followed suit with a rare unanimous vote. They also unanimously agreed to put the plan for financing to a public vote, probably in June. ("Camera", April 18, Linda Cornett)

In the meantime, over the mesa in Louisville, City Administrator Wurl made the charge that Governor Lamm's request for an in-depth study of the Centennial Valley Mall was nothing but a "stall tactic" so Boulder could get its act together. ("Camera", April 18, Gloria Bucco)

The governor has asked the Denver Regional Council of Governments and the Highway Department to look into the proposed mall's possible impact on air pollution, travel demands and the Boulder County Comprehensive Plan. ("Louisville Times", April 19, Percy Conarro)

City Planning Director Frank Gray gave the timetable for redeveloping Crossroads through mid-July including the crucial June 12 election when the voters will decide if the project is worth financing. ("Camera", April 29, Sue Deans)

The financial plan for the area is a complicated one, but its proponents say it will not raise taxes or leave the city with less operating funds. ("Camera", April 29, Sue Deans)

Crossroads was purchased by the Mace-Rich Company in a joint venture with the Aetna Life Insurance Company. Crossroads originally opened March 14, 1963. The developer was Gerri Von Frellick. The \$3 million center was 300,000 square feet and was later enlarged to 400,000 square feet. It now contains 43 stores. ("Camera", May 1, Sue Deans)

The Council approved June 12 as the election date for the Charter amendment to allow the financing of the urban renewal area. The bonds will be paid back from increased sales and use tax revenues coming from the area. The city's share of that revenue source would be frozen at the end of this year.

Money for the election is to come from the contingency fund and will be reimbursed from the Urban Renewal Authority's future income.

Donald Kahan, of the Jack Jacobs Company hired two CU professors to prepare an economic report based on the Urban Renewal Plan. The consultants said the plan is based on overestimated revenues and understated costs, and that poorer services will be provided by the city because of it. ("Camera", May 31, Sue Deans)

Opposition to the proposal has come mainly from two groups.

One, led by retired real estate salesman George Doty, is the Committee to Oppose Crossroads Redevelopment Proposal. The other is Jack Jacobs and Co., developer of the proposed Centennial Valley Mall.

Hanover Development Co., a Jacobs subsidiary, spent more than \$7,000 on a study ... which raised questions about the soundness of the Boulder plan.

Doty's committee ... organized just two weeks before the election. The group's campaign disclosure file lists only one contributor, veterinarian Don Tracy, who gave \$500.

The main organization working in favor of the proposal is the Coalition for Boulder's Future. That group, headed by Eugene Wilson ... , has concentrated on the community benefits of the redevelopment proposal. ("Camera", June 10, Sue Deans)

The approval of the financing plan by the voters gave a chance for a recap of all the proposals the city has dealt with since 1972 when the WestCor Company was turned down on its 600,000-square-foot Boulder Fashion Mall. ("Camera", June 17, Sue Deans)

The Urban Renewal Authority will help businesses in the Crossroads area in finding new locations and in obtaining financial assistance. Transportation and moving-related expenses will be considered. A private appraiser will be hired by the authority and purchases may begin next spring. ("Denver Post", June 27)

A lawsuit was filed July 6 asking the U. S. District Court in Denver to nullify the June 12 election. ("Camera", July 16, John Leach)

The lawsuit was filed as a class action by Don Tracy, Naomi Stoffle and Ethel Sadler. ("Camera", August 28)

Boulder has been recognized officially as a trend-setter in urban design.

... the National League of Cities ... announced Boulder's selection as a model city in an environmental design project.

The Crossroads project is recognized nationally as "the first attempt to transform a standard suburban shopping center into an intensive mixed-use environment, part of the urban core," (Planning Director Frank) Gray said. "We have an opportunity to set guidelines for the entire United States for re-use and adaptive use of shopping centers." ("Camera", September 27, Sue Deans)

Governor Lamm protested Sear's decision to locate at the Centennial Valley Mall and phoned two prospective tenants to inform them of the state's opposition to the project. State reluctance to locating shopping centers outside existing urban centers stems from air pollution and added costs to the state. ("Camera", October 3, Thad Keyes and Gloria Bucco)

Daryl Gibson wrote in the "Camera" dated November 21:

U. S. District Judge Fred Winner ... dismissed the only legal challenge to the Crossroads shopping area redevelopment ... .

Discussions continued during the fall with experts on transportation, traffic circulation, and parking, and with planners from Mace-Rich who are working on a complete renovation of the present center. City Transportation Director Bob Whitson said his department's ideas will have to be balanced against the developer's theories. ("Camera", December 10, Sue Deans)

In December, several Council members sought to make the Urban Renewal Authority more accountable to the Council in budget matters. City Attorney Joe de Raismes warned them that any future attempts to change the funding agreement could jeopardize the sale of the bonds. Too much Council control over the authority could eventually make the city liable for repayment of the authority's bonds. ("Camera", December 19, Sue Deans)

May D&F officials ... confirmed (that) ... they have signed a letter of intent to locate in the Crossroads Shopping Center.

... several conditions ... must be met, "before May D&F can open its doors in Boulder."

Among these were completion of traffic improvements, removal of the site from the 100-year-flood plain, and favorable action on their application for industrial revenue bonds to finance construction.

"About 25 acres of land north of the shopping center will be purchased by the Urban Renewal Authority, cleared, and resold to Mace-Rich for expansion of the center." ("Camera", December 22, Sue Deans)

While all the action was going on in the eastern section of Boulder, plans for the superblock came and went.

In January, Bill Wall was working with the Chamber of Commerce and Burlington Northern Railroad to put together a hotel, office and retail project. A theater could be included. ("Camera", January 28, Linda Cornett)

The theater was dropped from the plans and a health club was substituted. ("Camera", February 16, Linda Cornett)

Developer Wall has been working with the Central Area General Improvement (parking) District on financing the parking and tying it in to the Randolph Building. ("Camera", March 9, Sarah Hoover)

Business officials now think Wall has lost interest in the project. High land costs and inadequate parking have struck down two other potential developers of the "superblock". ("Camera", June 9, Sarah Hoover)

And in the southern sector of the city, the Table Mesa Shopping Center made a proposal to double its number of businesses. The redevelopment was approved by the Planning Board. ("Camera", July 13, Sue Deans)

A new shopping center in Gunbarrel, designed to serve community needs, does not conflict with the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan. It will be built on a 28-acre parcel of land which wraps around the supermarket on Lookout Road. ("Camera", December 26, Steven Wilhelm)

#### DOWNTOWN

In a "Camera" article dated February 2, Linda Cornett reported:

Merchants along Boulder's mall have joined the contest for a piece of the extra money left in the city's budget this year.

The Downtown Boulder Mall Commission wants about \$28,000 to repair damages ... and to beef up maintenance through the busy summer months.

The city ... budgeted \$56,576 for maintenance of the mall in 1979.

(Commission Chairman Richard Foy) ... said, use of the mall has been higher than any predictions. And, with the popularity of the television series "Mork and Mindy" which shows scenes shot along the mall, tourism will only increase ... .

In August, the Downtown Mall Commission began to grapple with mall permits, guidelines, regulations and liability insurance. Up to this time there had been a policy of non-interference with mall performers.

In October, the Planning Board approved a new building on the mall on

the corner of 14th Street where Kahn's building burned in 1978.

#### HISTORIC PRESERVATION

In February, the Boulder Theater was bought from the Mann Theatre Corporation, by six local investors who will lease it, with an option to buy after the first year, to Historic Boulder. Historic Boulder does not intend to follow through on the purchase, but is hoping that either the City of Boulder or a private theater group will buy the building. ("Town & Country", February 21, Alice Trembour)

Some groups, such as Historic Boulder Inc., want the theater preserved for its historical importance. Others, mainly performing artists, fear the theater may drain needed money and support from the proposed performing arts center.

The performing arts center proposal ... includes ... the renovation of the Boulder Theater ... .

Claire Lindgren, president of Historic Boulder, says her first concern is the theater's historic value. It is the only important example of art deco architecture in Boulder ... . ("Camera", November 25, Steven Wilhelm)

The Carnegie Library building and the Woodward-Baird house were added to the National Register of Historic Places. The Woodward-Baird house was formerly referred to as the Culver-Bixby house. It was acquired by Historic Boulder both to preserve it and to serve as offices for that organization. ("Camera", March 10)





## CHAPTER V

### ENVIRONMENT AND CULTURE

1975

### PARKS AND RECREATION

The Chautauqua Association requested the Council to renew their lease of lands in Chautauqua Park for an additional 20 years. Although the present lease does not expire until 1981, the Association has planned a fund drive to raise \$100,000 to finance the restoration of the auditorium and the dining hall facilities. They are reluctant to proceed with those repairs without a lease renewal.

Councilman Frank Buchanan raised questions about the city's legal right to lease park land for private uses and about privately owned dwellings on public park land.

City Attorney Walt Wagenhals advised Council that, in his opinion, Council does have the authority to execute the lease. However, Mr. Buchanan's motion to defer the decision until after a study session was adopted on a five to three vote.

In the "Town & Country" dated February 19, Parks and Recreation Director Leonard Ehrler was reported as saying that he does not wish to continue a dispute with a member of the Parks and Recreation Advisory Board who charges that a proposed Parks and Recreation Master Plan is misconceived.

Board member Katharine Welch objected that the plan is far more formal in its approach than desired by a majority of Boulder citizens.

Both the Parks Board and the Planning Board wanted more time to read

the plan and have not yet ratified it.

Mr. Ehrler said the plan was comprehensive and flexible enough to accomodate a variety of growth patterns. Portions of the plan could be dropped if predicted growth fails to materialize. ("Town & Country", February 19, Berny Morson)

In April, the Planning Board reviewed a revised version of the master plan and still was not pleased with it. Board member Tom Meier said the plan looked "more like a policy statement than a plan."

The plan's proposed new taxes to finance construction include a real estate transfer tax, an alcoholic beverage tax and an increase of one-third of a cent sales tax. It proposes construction of a cultural events center, two recreation centers, 109 tennis courts and eight softball fields. The facilities are to coincide with the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan's projection that Boulder Valley will have a maximum population of 140,000 by 1991. ("Camera", April 11, John Lannon)

The four members of the Parks and Recreation Advisory Board who were present, approved the 15-year, \$20 million master plan in July. ("Town & Country", July 30, Barbara Gigone)

In October, Katharine Welch submitted her resignation to Council after three years on the Advisory Board. In her letter, she called for a review of board and parks administration policies. ("Camera", October 22)

In March, the Advisory Board voted unanimously to endorse and co-sponsor the Boulder Community Gardens Pilot Project. Plots will be on the east side of North Boulder Recreation Center. ("Camera", March 21, Susan Blickman)

Barbara Gigone reported in the "Town & Country" on April 23 that:

Barbara Gigone reported in the "Town & Country" on April 23 that:

Plans for a 300-acre regional park on South Boulder Creek were unveiled Monday before the City Parks and Recreation Advisory Board. If the proposal is approved, the Flatiron Companies will first mine approximately 260 acres and the land will be reclaimed for public use over a 20 to 30 year period. The entire project includes 471 acres. In addition to the ... park and open space area, Flatiron will ask for residential zoning for much of the remaining 171 acres.

The Internal Revenue Service will be asked to rule that the gift of the land to the city for park use can be applied as taxes on the property. If approval is granted, Flatiron Companies plan to turn over the land (300 acres) at little or no cost after the mining is completed. If this cannot be done, the city will have to decide whether to purchase the land.

A caboose, to replace the one dynamited on June 3, 1958, for the train in Central Park has been found and purchased. The coach and caboose purchased and put in the park in 1952 were similar to those used in the Boulder area at the turn of the century. ("Camera", June 5)

Jane Cracraft reported in the "Denver Post" on July 14 that the Boulder Ranger Program is now in its 11th year.

The rangers were a volunteer group last year, when a federal agency ruled that the rangers should be paid the minimum wage, which was then \$2 per hour. The city had always paid 50 or 75 cents per hour, plus free swimming passes.

Finally, at the end of last summer, an exception was granted for the ranger program. About 25 "veterans" returned this year to work for \$1 an hour. Also there are 10 supervisors who earn \$1.40 and about 20 first-year workers (including 10 girls) getting 75 cents per hour.

The Parks and Recreation Advisory Board has recommended to City Council that park fees be raised to \$250 for each new home or apartment built and for each one annexed to the city.

The present charge is \$135 per multiple-family unit and \$145 per

single-family residences.

Parks and Recreation Director Leonard Ehrler told the board that his department is committed to the neighborhood park concept. The city is required to use park fees within three-quarters of a mile of the development or house which paid the fees. The fees are usually spent in developing new parks in new subdivisions and in improving parks in the established areas.

Ehrler said it costs approximately \$20,000 per acre to develop a good park. After it is completed, the general fund provides the maintenance and equipment costs.

He said developers have been "very receptive" to park planning and many have given land for parks, built swimming pools, tennis courts and other facilities which enable them to have some of the park fees waived. Developers can presently receive a waiver of up to three-fourths of the park fees by giving land and developing recreational facilities.

The parks director said the proposed \$250 charge would pay about two-thirds the cost of neighborhood park development. ("Town & Country", July 23)

#### OPEN SPACE

On March 18, the City Council voted unanimously to approve an ordinance authorizing the sale of \$2 million in open space bonds. The bulk of the money will be used to acquire land in the Davidson Mesa and Marshall Mesa areas. ("Camera", March 19, Ken Frizell)

The Open Space Board and the City Council voted to buy 17 acres of land in Pine Brook Hills, but the owners don't like the price. According to Don Walker, director of Real Estate Services, the land has already been platted and roads are being planned. He recommended initiating a

condemnation suit. ("Camera", April 2, Ken Frizell)

In October, the Council unanimously agreed to purchase 1200 acres of land consisting of Marshall Mesa north to the Eldorado Springs highway and east to Marshall Lake. (The seller was Sam Rudd and various partners.) The price of the land (\$1,300 an acre) does not include mineral rights. Payment will be spread over an 11-year period. ("Colorado Daily", October 9, David Gelenter)

#### LIBRARIES

The new Young Adult's Room in the Public Library opened in January. Its primary emphasis will be on participation in the creative process, according to Richard Varnes, young adult's clerk. Barbara Kennerly is the young adult's coordinator. ("Camera", January 17)

The Non-Print Media Center in the library is about 80 per cent operational according to its director, Jim Heckel. Patrons in the center will have access to carrels where they may listen to records, cassettes, radio, or watch television. TV programming through the center will include video tapes produced by the staff of selected city meetings, films of Boulder's history and interviews with Boulder's residents and well-known visitors. ("Town & Country", February 26, Pam Patrick-Davis)

Nadine Huff reported in the "Camera" dated April 17 that there had been a 71 per cent increase in the use of the library reference materials over the first three months of last year. "Anytime you find yourself in a recession you find people beginning to use the library services more," said Marcelee Galapp, director of the Boulder library. The economic situation has also affected the cost of magazines and books and so people read the library's copies. Ms. Galapp pointed out the irony that during a

period of increased library use "that ... service is looked at as a kind that can sustain a cut in the budget."

A prisoner rehabilitation library for the new Boulder justice center is now in the planning stages. It will be maintained by the Boulder Public Library.

According to Ron Green, administrative assistant to the library director, materials for educational, occupational and recreational rehabilitation will be provided. If approved, funding will come from the Law Enforcement Assistance Administration on a matching grant basis. ("Camera", May 11, Nancy Traver)

In the "Camera" of August 20, Barbralu Fried reported that:

The public library is housing non-circulating books and records on Boulder, Boulder County (and) Colorado ... in its new room on the second floor ... (The Colorado Room) ... houses a collection gathered from the library shelves and the Boulder Genealogical Society's library. ... the room is staffed by about 20 volunteers.

Several meetings were held in November between City Manager Archie Twitchell and members of the Library Commission amid rumors concerning the possible firing of Library Director Marcelee Gralapp.

Gralapp apparently fell into disfavor with the administration after publicly announcing her opposition to Twitchell's reorganization, a plan which would see the library, along with other city departments, subordinated to a new "super department" to be titled Human Services. Gralapp has objected, maintaining that the City Charter affords the library separate status. ("Camera", November 13, Ken Frizell)

Timothy Lange wrote in the "Colorado Daily" of November 21:

The Library Commission takes just as strong a stand, arguing among other things that the reorganization plan would weaken the effectiveness of the library administration, that information about the library would be filtered and that morale would drop.

Essentially, the commission and Gralapp want continued direct access to the city manager without having to deal with an intervening coordinator.

The dispute was resolved at a Sunday night meeting which included the free services of two University of Colorado "facilitators". The Library

Commission and the City Manager agreed that Gralapp and all other department heads will still have direct access to the manager on major policy. The Commission will continue to play a role in the evaluation of the library director. ("Camera", November 24, Ken Frizell)

#### COMMUNICATIONS

Ken Downes arrived back in Boulder in January with a new financing scheme which calls for leasing the entire operation and the sale of equity shares in the cable system. He also brought a letter from a multi-millionaire pledging "fine art" to help finance the cable system. A second letter was delivered to City Manager Archie Twitchell from a group of mortgage specialists who have agreed to proceed with arrangements to "provide proper funds" for the project based on loans using the art work as collateral. ("Camera", January 5, Ken Frizell)

The City Council gave Mr. Downes a 60 day extension to pursue all financing options available, including the non-profit corporation scheme the city has favored from the beginning. ("Camera", January 8, Ken Frizell)

The value of part of the art collection pledged to back Ken Downes cable system in Boulder was put in doubt by the Art Dealer's Association of America. They examined an appraisal of 360 of the paintings and could not identify any of the artists on the list. They then turned the list over to the Federal Bureau of Investigation (FBI). The investment firm which was to have arranged the financing pulled out of the deal. ("Camera", February 5, Ken Frizell)

The City Manager recommended, and the City Council agreed, that the latest financing scheme of Mr. Downes should be rejected. The Council decided to put cable on a back burner, to leave the permit open, and review any proposals that might be submitted. ("Camera", March 19, Ken Frizell)

The FBI probe into the possibility of fraud in connection with use of paintings to back Boulder's cable system was concluded nine months later. No charges will be filed. ("Camera", November 10)

Jan Tuchman of the "Colorado Daily" wrote in the November 24 issue that:

When your phone bill is \$4400 a month for service alone, you're naturally going to economize. That's the City of Boulder's phone tab, and in the interests of both economy and increased efficiency, the city will be converting to a Centrex II telephone system Dec. 1.

The Centrex system is a joint city/county project ... . Telecommunications Coordinator Tom Cross ... (said) ... the idea for the change ... came from a city/county communications study done early in 1974.

Cross expects the system to smooth out many current problems ... both internally and externally.

Externally, the system should eliminate the frustration of listening to the phone ring 20 times before the operator answers. "The city switchboard has two operator stalls," Cross said, "but we've had one operator for the past three years."

The contract with Mountain Bell was signed in February and the half million dollars worth of equipment will be installed by December.

#### CULTURAL CENTER

Citizens for a Boulder Cultural Center began presentations to the public after seven months of research and surveys. They prioritized the facilities needed and put the \$4.5 million project into a two-phase mode. The first phase would emphasize performing arts and the second, visual art. Chairman Charles Haertling said the committee favors land east of Central Park as a site for the center. ("Camera", January 31, Barbralu Fried)

City Manager Archie Twitchell appointed a task force of several administration experts to review the cultural center plan. They estimated that the total cost would be in the neighborhood of \$7.6 million. If the city were to assume the entire project, it would cost \$1.5 million annually in debt service and operating costs.

"The university has proposed a cooperative effort to provide a gallery, a concert hall, and two theaters," said Twitchell. "I would recommend that we join forces with the university in an effort to satisfy some of the existing needs in both the exhibiting and performing areas." ("Camera", August 19, Ken Frizell)

The Council approved a contract with Sasaki Associates for a feasibility study and site analysis for the proposed cultural center. The work is to be a joint arrangement between the city, the university and the consulting firm. ("Camera", October 22, Bill Jordan)

The City Council agreed to a three-year lease, for \$1 a year, on the old City Storage and Transfer Building at 1750 13th Street by the Boulder Fine Arts Center. The center had been at 1305 Pine Street for three years. Renovation is being done as a joint project with the Boulder Council on the Arts and Humanities. ("Colorado Daily", December 4, Jan Tuchman)

Under the terms of the lease, the center will use the building for three years rent free, but may be evicted in the event the city decides to use the land, which is part of a site being considered for a cultural center. ("Camera", November 26, Ken Frizell)

#### ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION

In a "Camera" article in the Focus section of October 5, Ken Frizell wrote:

... in the three and a half years since (Jim) Adams began his job (as the city's environmental protection officer), he has issued more than 2,600 noise summonses.

Adams is a great believer in giving people a chance to remedy their problem.

"Our objective is quiet in the city," he said, "not collecting money for the city's general fund."

The city budgeted about \$30,000 in 1975 for noise abatement and vehicular emissions control.

In April, area civic and environmental leaders attended hearings conducted by a special task force appointed earlier this year by Governor

Richard Lamm and Congressman Tim Wirth to study the Rocky Flats facility.

Councilwoman Ruth Correll called upon the federal government to create a buffer zone around the plant where development would be barred. Mary Taylor of the Boulder County Council on Environmental Quality said that flight paths over the plant should be eliminated until the plant can be turned into some other kind of facility.

In March, Boulder Ranger Bryan Peck said he has identified 1,300 pine trees in the Flagstaff area which should be removed because of beetle infestation. He said 26,000 trees have been killed in the mountain parks in the last four years. ("Camera", March 6, Bill Jordan)

Despite an elm beetle control program, Boulder has lost 77 trees and will probably lose most of its remaining 599 American elms in three years. ("Denver Post", August 26, Jane Cracraft)

1976

### PARKS AND RECREATION

The Council voted unanimously to authorize the lease of land for community gardens. The city plans to lease nearly 11 acres of land east of Olmsted Park for a three year period for approximately \$2,000 and to spend another \$3,000 to install a water system. Garden plots were rented to interested Boulderites for the first time last year at \$15 per plot. ("Camera", March 17, Ken Frizell)

The Flatirons Ice Arena was closed April 3 because its owner said it could not be operated profitably. Petitions with more than 15,000 signatures of persons interested in saving the facility were presented to

Council on April 20. Before the city made any recommendation, the arena was sold. City officials had indicated that the city was not in a position to buy anything and looked unfavorably on purchase of a facility which was estimated to cost more than \$550,000. ("Camera", May 25, Bruce Selcraig)

Negotiations are continuing between the city and the Colorado Chautauqua Association. Eugene Wilson, president of the association said, "(We are) attempting to arrive at the best solution for the renovation and restoration of Chautauqua." The park is owned and maintained by the city. The Association determines major policies, fixes fees and adopts rules and regulations. Federal funds have been requested to help with extensive repairs. ("Town & Country", December 22, Nan Pirnack)

#### OPEN SPACE

In August, the Council voted unanimously to approve the purchase of 220 acres for open space along Dakota Ridge near Lee Hill Road. The price includes partial development rights to another 33 acres in the same area. ("Camera". August 18)

Jane Cracraft reported in the "Denver Post" of November 18 that the agreement between the city and the operator of Hidden Valley Ranch is expiring. The land is open space property. Buddy Hays has run the ranch and its horseback riding stables since 1966 when it was privately owned. Real Estate Director Don Walker said the city wants to put out the ranch management for bids.

In December, the City Council directed the city administrators to negotiate a lease with Mr. Hays. "That action headed off a possible legal conflict ... but did little toward solving an apparent lack of communication between Hays ... (and city officials)" ("Camera", December 8,

Steve Hoffman)

#### LIBRARIES

A task force headed by Marcelee Gralapp, director of the Boulder Public Library, worked for two years collecting information on the problems of libraries in the state. They have completed a plan, now approved by the State Board of Education, which will act as a guide for the policies and budgets of the Colorado State Library. Key strategies for improving library service include continuing education for library personnel, the establishment of state guidelines, networking, and cooperative regional library service systems. ("Camera", March 17)

In 1975, a law was passed allowing all publicly-supported libraries to join regional library service systems. In the past year, the St. Vrain School District, the University and the University Educational Media Center joined Boulder County's previous members, the public libraries of Ward, Broomfield, Lafayette, Longmont, Boulder and Louisville. Programs include a rotating collection of films, interlibrary loan programs, a list of all periodicals in each system distributed to member libraries, reciprocal borrowing between libraries and consulting by state library staff members to local libraries. ("Camera", May 22)

The libraries in Boulder County chastised the County Commissioners for failing to live up to an agreement made in 1972 in which they agreed to a formula for determining how much money each of the six was to receive. The Commissioners traditionally have given the libraries less because persons from unincorporated areas shop in the municipalities and pay sales tax.

Commissioner Maggie Markey said a long range solution to the problem will be for the county to direct development into the cities. ("Camera",

March 25)

Barbralu Fried reported in the "Camera" of March 11 that:

A bilingual newsletter is being published by the Boulder Public Library's Center for Adult Learning to "make the lives of the Vietnamese adults a little easier" and to keep Boulderites aware of Vietnamese contributions to their new culture. (It) is being published under a three-part grant to the center from the Colorado State Library ... . Under the grant Violet Wagener has instituted independent study projects for adults, produced auto-tutorials to help adults "see themselves as learning throughout their lifetimes" and produced informative packets on special issues of community concerns.

Boulder Public Library's concert series was inaugurated with a concert by pianist Larry Graham. Coordinator Richard Varnes said the series is designed to be a "complete educational experience" with concerts and musicology lectures.

Joanne Arnold donated a new grand piano to the library in memory of her mother, Bernice Anna Easley. ("Camera", March 27)

Children's Librarian Judith Volc began telling stories to her young audience 12 years ago when there were no teachers or books to help her with the art of storytelling. Now, she says, "Everywhere I go, people are excited about it."

Not reading and not acting -- but something of both -- storytelling is being taught in Boulder and stories are being told by staff of the Boulder Public Library from 15 to 20 times a week. The library, in addition, is sponsoring a project wherein Vietnamese families newly transplanted here are taping traditional stories for translation, transcription and general circulation. ("Camera", April 4, Barbralu Fried)

Classics in children's literature, children's poetry, songs in Spanish and French are available on tapes in the children's room. This service is the latest initiated by the Public Library to provide children and adults access to information and literature through all media forms, serving readers, non-readers and those for whom reading is difficult. ("Camera",

November 3)

Marian Cooper has been in charge of the library's Homebound Services since its beginning in 1970. She takes books to private homes, apartments, retirement or nursing homes.

Sometimes she takes Talking Books or cassette tapes ... to individuals who can no longer read. Many of the books are published with extra large type. In fact she has about 600 of these, ranging from classics to contemporary ... .

Mrs. Cooper has seventy homebounds on her list and uses three or four volunteer assistants to help with selections and deliveries. ("Camera", December 8, Josephine Robertson)

Linda Cornett wrote in the "Camera" of December 30 that:

The generosity of one man who "loves film" has opened new programs for audiences at the Boulder Public Library's 1977 film series.

A continuing contribution by an anonymous film fan has already provided the library's media center with \$7,000 in classic films and created enough budgeting leeway to begin two new film series.

New Program Director Bob Grzenda said these will be a mixed schedule of films on the weekends and a series by independent filmmakers.

Usage of the Boulder Public Library on Sundays increased dramatically after the City Council authorized funding in November to keep the library open additional hours. Library hours were cut back last year when sales tax revenues began to falter. ("Camera", December 5)

#### CULTURAL CENTER

The team of consultants retained by the city and the university are recommending against the large cultural center project. They say that a less ambitious plan is more feasible and would enable the two participants to retain individual ownership and control.

Improvements to Macky Auditorium could cost the university from \$3 to

\$4 million. This price would include additional parking. Construction by the city of a multi-use facility could cost \$4 to \$5 million. ("Camera", February 10, Ken Frizell)

The Boulder Arts Center opened on May 21 after extensive renovation by Center advocates. Except for being located in a city-owned building, the group has received no grants or any other kind of funding from the city or state. ("Longmont Times-Call", May 14, Krystyna Strzelec)

#### ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION

A "Camera" article on February 22 by Caryl Buckstein said that Tom Le Mire of the Boulder County Health Department was not happy about the plan to put a parking lot for the Justice Center at 6th Street and Canyon Boulevard. The land around the area is radioactive in spots because it was the site of processing factories for some of the old mines above Boulder. The State Health Department determined the radiation levels were low and not dangerous.

1977

### PARKS AND RECREATION

The Chautauqua Association has suggested that the city take over its functions, responsibilities and liabilities. The future of the park became a matter of concern two years ago when the city balked at the Association's request for early renewal of a 20-year lease due to expire in 1981. Association members wanted to launch a renovation effort but were hesitant to do so without the renewal. ("Camera", February 16, Steve Hoffman)

The Council is considering the issue of taking over Chautauqua. At a study session in March, the proposal was debated, but the only decision

made was that the land would not be used as a convention center, and the buildings there would not be razed for a modern park.

There are 36 private cottages on the land. The other 59 are owned by the Chautauqua Association. The city already owns the auditorium, which is on the National Register of Historic Places, the dining hall, administration building and a few other smaller structures. ("Camera", March 23, Steve Hoffman)

In April, Chautauqua Board President Eugene Wilson and all but three of the association's 15 members resigned. New people were appointed and will tackle the problems of finance, programs and building use, and preservation and restoration of the structures. ("Camera", May 3)

A Boulder Parks and Recreation Foundation has been established to receive private donations for parks and recreation programs and facilities and for acquisition of park land. Members of the Parks and Recreation Advisory Board will serve as the board of directors.

Some people have been taking advantage of the seclusion of Coot Lake to do some nude sunbathing. The city attorney's office says the city has no law to prohibit the activity. A state statute prohibits nudity if there is the intent to cause affront or alarm, but the intention to be offended doesn't count.

The real crime at the lake, in the eyes of the city government is the violation of the code which makes it unlawful for any person to swim in any pond or reservoir owned by the city unless a lifeguard is present. The Parks and Recreation Department has asked for an interpretation of exactly what their liability is. ("Town & Country", July 20, Deborah Osment Ryan)

#### OPEN SPACE

Paul Johnston reported in the "Camera" dated March 17:

Five lots in the Pinebrook Hills subdivision won't be added to Boulder's greenbelt after all . . . .

Reasons given for deciding against the purchase of the lots were their high cost and the necessity to save this year's nearly \$1 million in greenbelt funds for land purchases south and east of Boulder. Homes already built in the area are visible from the city.

After three years of negotiation, the land along the Denver-Boulder turnpike near the intersection with Cherryvale Road was recommended for purchase by the Open Space Board of Trustees. Over the 20-year purchase period, the 475 acres will be leased. The sale price is below the appraised market value, but is attractive to the seller because of income tax benefits. ("Camera", September 15)

The Open Space Board of Trustees hopes the city will acquire 28 acres on either side of Boulder Creek north of the Ball Brothers industrial site as soon as possible. Known as Cottonwood Grove, the land is said to represent a valuable ecological resource that ought to be preserved. ("Camera", November 17, Steve Hoffman)

In December, the Council adopted a motion accepting the Arnold property along the north side of Arapahoe west of 47th Street near Boulder Creek as a gift of open space land. ("Camera", December 21)

#### LIBRARIES

The library sponsored free public musical programs beginning in January, and in February offered free classes in music appreciation. The concerts continued throughout the summer in the library auditorium.

Project Director Barbara Kennerly announced that nationally known writers will participate in the Writer's Residency Project. The program is

sponsored by the Boulder Public Library and partially funded by a \$5,000 grant from the National Endowment for the Arts. ("Camera", April 14)

James Heckel wrote in the Focus section of the "Camera" dated April 17 that since its opening in June 1976, the Non-Print Media Center and the Young Adult's Department of the Boulder Public Library have been attracting increasing numbers of people to the multitude of services and programs available.

Both Vi Wagener, head of the Media Center and Richard Varnes, director of the young adult's program see the library not only as a storehouse of information but as a personal growth center as well.

Theater, poetry, chess and art projects are sponsored by the Young Adults Department. Summer children's theater offered high school students an opportunity to perform in productions for children. The theater program is funded by the Library Foundation, small business advertising and private donations.

Poetry workshops, led by Richard Varnes, are held at the library and at Heatherwood Elementary School.

Chess players of any age may bring their boards to the Young Adults gallery on Saturdays. The gallery is decorated with displays of the area's young artists. ("Camera", June 12)

An auction was held on Colorado Day at McDonald's Restaurant to benefit the Boulder Public Library Foundation. President Doris Hass said that the funds will be used to supplement the children's film library. "Camera", July 24)

Michael Collard has arranged an exhibit of photographic prints of Boulder County Historic sites at the public library. The emphasis of the

show is on endangered and western county sites. The library has received a Public Service Employment grant for three persons to work on a county history project. ("Camera", May 21)

Use of the old Boulder city library building at 1125 Pine Street as a center for historical research has been proposed by a coalition of interested Boulder area organizations. The building was erected on city-owned land in 1906-07 with a \$15,000 grant from Andrew Carnegie. The Carnegie building housed the library until the present facility on Canyon Boulevard was opened in 1961. Since then it has housed the offices and some program facilities of the Parks and Recreation Department. ("Camera", July 26)

On August 9, the staff and members of the Library Commission gave City Council members a tour of the library. According to Doris Hass, Chairperson of the Commission, the purpose was to present goals and programs of the library to the Council as a group. Some of the statistics presented were that the Children's Department has 39,583 books, and the adult collection has 86,110. Judith Waller, head of reference services, pointed out that 38,000 reference questions are answered yearly. The Boulder library has high use compared to state and local trends. ("Town & Country", August 10, Elizabeth Jay)

Marty Dee reported in the "Longmont Times-Call" on September 28 that a panel discussion on the legal system was held at the library sponsored by the Boulder County Bar Association and the Boulder Public Library. Judith Waller, reference librarian, has prepared a bibliography on legal topics such as consumer protection, marriage, contracts, and landlord-tenant relationships.

Books from the Boulder Public Library are now available to the Heatherwood-Gunbarrel area at Heatherwood Elementary School.

Mike Parsons, adult services coordinator ... said the branch was opened in June as a result of interest in making books available to persons who "don't normally use the library." ("Camera", November 6, Linda Castrone)

In an article by C. M. Riley for the "Camera" dated November 13, the book shortage at Boulder Public Library was discussed. A library patron, Rick Walsh, conducted his own survey of library books and will protest the shortage at the next library commission meeting. He is convinced the shortage results from theft.

Under a system used by most medium and large libraries today, the only way to find out how many books have been stolen is to conduct an inventory. Boulder's last inventory was in 1966. Inventories are expensive and complex. The larger the collection, the more time-consuming the job. Boulder's book collection numbers 110,000 separate titles and 150,000 volumes, not quite two books per capita for an estimated 100,000 patrons in and near the city.

Library Director Marcelee Gralapp said she does not believe the amount of theft warrants hiring security guards. An electronic surveillance system would be even more expensive. A computerized library system would speed up all tracking procedures. But she is not hopeful that the Council that turned down her request for supplemental book money would be interested in a \$100,000 computer system for the library.

#### ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION

In February, the Council was given the United States Forest Service's series of options for Wilderness designation in the Indian Peaks area, located south of Rocky Mountain National Park. The prime concern to

Council was that no land use decision made would have a negative impact on Boulder's watershed and Park Reservoir site. After an extended public hearing, the Council unanimously passed a resolution recommending the largest possible area surrounding the Indian Peaks be protected by the Wilderness Act of 1972.

In April, the Nuclear Reaction Coalition, a group of Boulder citizens opposed to nuclear weapons, sponsored a demonstration at the Rocky Flats plant. Revelations about air shipments of plutonium and radioactive contamination of nearby soil have focused public concern on safety conditions at the plant.

Larry Caldwell wrote in the May 1 issue of the "Camera" that residents of Moorhead Avenue were organizing a campaign to combat the increase of noise and fumes in their yards from turnpike traffic. Suggestions included ending the turnpike at the Table Mesa exchange, reducing the speed limit to residential levels, and planting trees and shrubs possibly on top of an earth berm.

It was announced in December that the state Highway Department turned down a request to lower the speed limit on the turnpike between Baseline and Table Mesa Drive from 55 mph to 45 mph. ("Camera", December 9, Steve Hoffman)

On July 6, Sue Deans reported in the "Camera" that the city had strengthened its noise ordinance. The amendment was the result of a May 1 "rock" concert in Folsom Stadium which spawned numerous complaints. Under the new law, violations occur if noise exceeds any of three time and level combinations.

Thad Keyes wrote in the "Camera" of July 19 that the city had received

a complaint about the noise generated by planes at the Municipal Airport.

The Boulder Junction microwave relay station was built in 1963 to relay telephone calls, data signals and television signals along the front range north of Denver. Mountain Bell's installation consisted of one dish antenna ten feet in diameter and another, six feet in diameter. Both had protective white covers. Area residents in south Boulder complained that the antennae stuck out against the natural rock and pine tree setting and Mountain Bell agreed to change the covers to a dark green.

#### ENERGY

The city's energy conservation ordinance, which specifies minimum insulation standards for residential, commercial and industrial buildings went into effect in September.

1978

### PARKS AND RECREATION

The Parks and Recreation Advisory Board recommended that Coot Lake be drained into Boulder Reservoir through a man-made canal before skinny dipping season begins. Coot Lake is a small water-filled gravel pit near the IBM plant north of Boulder ("Colorado Daily", April 21)

Water level in the lake would be lowered to four feet. Concern over liability if someone is injured or drowned prompted the decision. However, the Council, after numerous calls protesting the drainage, decided to delay the plan until a public hearing could be held. ("Colorado Daily", April 26, Stephen Foehr)

On March 21, the entire area of Chautauqua was entered into the National Register of Historic Places. The auditorium was put on the register in January 1964. It is now joined by about 40 acres of land around it.

The registration places certain restrictions on changes that may be made in the area or buildings. It also makes Chautauqua eligible for participation in grant-in-aid funding under the National Historic Preservation Act. A \$30,000 grant from that fund has been made available to help with restoration and preservation.

About \$50,000 worth of work has been done or is under way this spring, funded from Boulder's share of Community Development funds. ("Camera, June 3)

In September, the Council gave Chautauqua historical landmark status.

The Parks and Recreation Department and the Boulder Jaycees are building an exercise trail at Harlow Platts Park. Plans for Eben G. Fine Park include improved parking, an automatic underground irrigation system, and new sod. Canyon Park will be expanded and the quonset huts, parking lot and inadequate watering system will be removed. ("Town & Country", March 22)

A joint city-university contract was signed in March after two years of negotiations to provide recreational use of a 21.5 acre site on the east campus of the University. The city will pay \$1 a year rent for the site and will construct four lighted ball fields and 16 lighted tennis courts. University intramural teams will share the facilities with city residents. Funds will come from the portion of the city's property tax which is set aside for parks and recreation capital improvements. ("Camera", August 24, Linda Cornett)

Leonard Ehrler, Parks and Recreation Director, is leaving to return to his home state after five years in Boulder.

His greatest pride, he said, is in the staff he has assembled, in "gaining the confidence and respect" of the University of Colorado for cooperative recreation projects, and in the senior center and recreation centers.

With unlimited resources, Ehrler said, he would first complete an ambitious plan for expanding facilities at the Boulder Reservoir. ("Camera", September 6, Linda Cornett)

Thad Keyes reported in the "Camera" of October 4 that the city had cancelled a permit that was granted for a rally at the Central Park grandstand. The gathering was being advertised in fliers headlined "Free Marijauna!". The man who had reserved the park in July said the wording was not an invitation for free pot, but a political slogan to "free" marijuana from repressive laws.

In November, Boulder Community Hospital told the city the game is over and asked it to give back the land it lent for recreational purposes west of the facility. The hospital now needs the volleyball and tennis courts for additional parking. ("Town & Country", November 22)

#### OPEN SPACE

Steve Hoffman wrote in the "Camera" dated April 26:

Since December 1976, the Boulder City Council has faced an off-again, on-again struggle over the future of Hidden Valley Ranch, a riding stable leased by the city to Bud Hays for about the last four years.

At a study session ... the council decided it will continue to lease at least 35 acres of the ... ranch to a private concessionaire such as Hays.

City Manager Bob Westdyke recommended a 10-year lease as the second of two options. His first choice was the outright sale of the 35 acres containing the ranch facilities. "This action would extricate the city from the horse business entirely."

Both the Open Space Board of Trustees and a strong majority of the City Council opposed the outright sale of city open space lands, fearing it would set a dangerous precedent.

According to Ann Goodhart, assistant to the Real Estate director, 2,000 of the 8,800 acres of open space land are currently leased to ranchers and farmers. She projects \$1.55 million from sales tax revenues will be collected this year for open space uses. ("Colorado Daily", May 15, Lynette Lo)

Ken Frizell reported in the "Camera" of June 21 that the City Council had approved the expenditure of \$2.9 million in open space funds for nearly 772 acres of ranch land along both sides of the Denver-Boulder turnpike. Negotiations to purchase the Van Vleet Arabian Ranch took four years to complete. The city will pay for the land in installments over the next 16 years. The land will be leased back to the ranch as long as they continue to use it for agricultural purposes.

In the "Camera" dated August 18, Linda Cornett wrote:

Don Walker, city real estate director for four years, has resigned to return to private business ... .

During ... (that time) ..., the city has acquired 9,200 of the 14,000 acres targeted for open space. The office has committed \$14 million for the purchases and negotiated another \$1.5 million in gifts and grants.

(Walker) ... says, the Boulder open space program is "one of the most successful in the country" because the flexible acquisition schedule has allowed him to negotiate for lands and to reject those which prove unworkable.

... On only four pieces of land, totalling 50 acres, has Walker's office resorted to condemnation.

#### LIBRARIES

A seven-day amnesty period for overdue library books preceded an increase on January 17 of charges on overdue adult library materials. "Over half the (loss of books) is not from people who rip them off," said Library Director Marcelee Gralapp, "but from people who are careless in returning them. Hopefully, this will help." ("Camera", January 8)

Richard Varnes wrote in the Focus section of the "Camera" on January 15:

Manifesto: It is a need of every citizen to have available a wide range of informational and cultural materials for education, instruction and recreation. The Boulder Public Library believes that the performing arts represent a vital aspect of our cultural heritage and should be made available to the public along with the "traditional" library services.

He listed the free concert programs from January through March which are now sponsored by the Boulder Public Library Foundation.

The library's Media Center made an attempt to help area musicians through a new Boulder Area Musician's Clearinghouse. ("Camera", February 17)

The children's department started discussion groups with volunteer leaders in the Junior Great Books program. ("Town & Country", February 22)

This year, plays for the summer Children's Theater will be written by rchildren from the same age group as that of the audience. Boulder Public Library held a writing workshop for those interested in submitting stories. ("Camera", May 15)

The history department of the University of Colorado and the Boulder Public Library Foundation jointly received a grant to present an all-day regional oral history workshop.

Tim Algar wrote in the "Longmont Times-Call" of June 2 that:
