As it stands now, the towns and cities in Boulder County contribute to the Health Department, which, by law, is a service provided by the county administration. In turn, the county helps fund each municipality's library system. During the comprehensive plan hearings held last fall, the (county) commissioners proposed that the county take over all funding for the Health Department. At the same time, the municipalities, as part of their responsibility to provide "urban services", would begin maintaining the libraries, even though they serve residents in unincorporated areas. Boulder ... (now) ... provides \$155,241 while getting back only \$84,000.

Reproductions of famous paintings were made available for circulation in the children's department in hopes of promoting interest in and increased knowledge of art. ("Town & Country", July 26)

The Public Library Foundation is sponsoring a "Bear Fair" in honor of 20-year-old Winnie the Pooh and 21-year-old Little Bear. ("Camera", August 6)

In September, the result of two years of work by members of the Colorado Council on the Arts and Humanities and the Boulder Public Library Foundation was put on display. The Colorado Visual Artist's Register was initiated with a grant from the National Endowment for the Arts. Housed in the Media Center, it was designed to bring recognition and employment to artists all over the state, according to Judi Shaw, project director. ("Camera", February 18, 1979, Jan McCoy)

J. Edward Murray wrote about Boulder's library in the "Camera" of September 10:

What, I asked long-term library director Marcellee (sic) Gralapp, during an interview in her office, does Boulder read?

"Anything and everything," she replied. "For that reason, it's a pleasure to buy books because they'll be read." The library ... spends \$100,000 a year from its \$800,000 annual budget to add about 10,000 new volumes (not titles) to the roughly 165,000 it already has. Another \$10,000 is spent annually for cassettes, films and other media center materials.

A 55-member library staff serves 40,000 regular borrowers each year, plus another 20,000 who use the \$117 million facility for reference work, study and other purposes. The library wants a \$200,000 computer for more efficient handling of materials; the Public Library Commission is interested and Ms. Gralapp hopes the City Council will be.

In a preliminary discussion of the 1979 budget, the City Council debated automation vs. people.

City Manager Bob Westdyke proposed buying a \$200,000 pretested computer program to handle circulation and overdue book accounts at Boulder Public Library. "A computer doesn't cost me 10 percent more

every year in salaries, plus added fringe benefits and an expensive retirement fund. When it wears out, I just kick it out and get a new one, as opposed to people I have to take care of," said Westdyke. ("Denver Post", October 11, Jane Cracraft)

A rental housing forum, sponsored by the library and the County Bar Association became a sounding-board for community opinion on landlord-tenant relations. ("Colorado Daily", September 20, Michael Ross)

The "Camera" of December 10 reported that Nederland residents can now use two check-out and return stations in Nederland for Boulder Public Library books.

Mike Parnell, working with other library personnel, is drawing up a feasibility report on using the 7,000 square feet within the Carnegie Library as a local history research center. Material from the public library's overcrowded Colorado Room would be used as a core of historical materials. ("Camera", December 30, Linda Cornett)

#### COMMUNICATIONS

City Manager Bob Westdyke said ... that cable television is "dormant" as far as he is concerned and will probably stay that way in the immediate future. "It is not ... on my list of priorities."

A discouraged (Marcelee) Gralapp agreed, saying a studio that was built several years ago in the main library when the city was more optimistic about its cable prospects is being used, but only for in-house productions. ("Camera", September 11, Ken Frizell)

#### CULTURAL CENTER

Marcelee Gralapp told a study session of the City Council that there are currently some 50 active visual and performing arts organizations in the city and that the Council should re-examine its role in supporting local artists.

The city administration proposed that the study done for the city and the university in 1976 should be dusted off and scaled down.

Specifically, Gralapp recommended that the city ... make use of existing facilities ... .

She said that later, the city should investigate the feasibility of constructing a 600 to 700 seat performing arts center and a smaller recital hall downtown.

She also suggested council set aside a percentage of the city budget for the arts and that an arts advisory commission be established. ("Camera", May 22, Ken Frizell)

In a lengthy article in the "Camera" of October 15, J. Edward Murray discussed the state of the arts in Boulder.

Frankly, there has been a shortage, not of artists, but of unity, communication, organization, money and a central identifiable focus.

... the City Council (should) accept in full the recommendation of a recent thorough, and conservative, study of how the city should support the visual and performing arts.

The study was done by Marcellee Gralapp ... (and) ... projected a budget proposal of \$136,464, or 1 percent of the unrestricted general fund ... .

The proposal included money for remodeling the Arts Center, for program support, for preliminary planning and design of a performing arts center and for a contingency fund for other arts-related needs. "In presenting the Gralapp recommendation to City Council as a budget item ... City Manager Bob Westdyke scaled it down to provide (only) program support for artists ... ."

The Boulder Chamber of Commerce also has submitted a proposal, with funds to be divided between establishing a central cultural office and remodeling the Arts Center.

Alice Trembour wrote in the "Town & Country" of December 18 that:

A study on the costs and feasibility of renovating Macky Auditorium on campus has been published by Midyette Associates ... . In the study, the architects proposed "an improved concert hall ... (to) ... be used for university and city performing arts, saving both the expense of building a new facility." J. Midyette ... said, "Let's face it. The city cannot afford a grand hall, and neither can the university. Joint ownership is difficult, so joint usership is the answer."

#### ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION

Steve Hoffman wrote in the "Camera" dated April 19:

The Rocky Flats nuclear weapons plant, located eight miles south of Boulder, poses questions the City Council isn't able to ignore.

But finding answers to those questions is something that, for the most part, lies outside the province of local government.

... the council re-examined its position on the giant government-owned facility. It passed a statement to the Department of Energy outlining two major areas of concern -- the importance of making the plant as safe as possible for both workers and those living in the surrounding area and the need to at least consider ending the nuclear processing there and replacing it with a less hazardous industry.

The council's action was spurred by plans for a protest against Rocky Flats set for April 29-30 ... .

In August, Drum Corps International was given permission to violate the city's ordinance limiting noise levels providing they practiced only between 9 a.m. and 8 p.m. Boulder residents told the City Council to deny any such variances in the future.

Marty Dee reported in the "Camera" of November 3 that:

Boulder's second battle over loud music ended ... with a one-year deferred prosecution for Feyline Presents Inc. and Shalom Maximon, charged with violating the city's noise ordinance July 16 during the Rolling Stones concert.

Feyline's Boulder attorney Bob ... Miller said any law, even if valid, is unconstitutional when selectively enforced ... .

The ordinance is ignored when other noise is present, Miller said, citing specific examples of the drum and bugle corps competition and the nightly music from ... the Hilton Harvest House.

Sharon Massingill reported in the "Camera" on August 29 that:

The battle against Dutch Elm disease in Boulder County has greatly reduced the spread of the disease in all areas of the county but one: the city of Boulder.

Hundreds of trees have been lost ... countywide since (the disease) appeared in 1968.

Of the 108 diseased elms found this year in the county, 70 ... are in Boulder.

Trees lost this year in Boulder account for 7.8 percent of Boulder's 897 elm trees.

Ron Donahue, superintendent of the city's Parks Department ... noted the city does have a year-round program to trim trees of dead and broken limbs that has helped curb the disease.

#### ENERGY

In June, Rich Lopez, head of the Creative Land Use firm, delivered the city's proposal for an energy conservation plan to Argonne Laboratories.

Argonne, acting as a funnel for money from the United States Department of Energy, will decide on 15 of the 150 applicants who will receive a federal grant so they may provide an education program and gather data on energy use.

With the data gathered, a task force and staff would turn to projecting the city's energy future and determining how that future could be modified. ("Camera", July 9, Linda Cornett)

Solar panels were installed on the roof of the city's housing project at 1940 Walnut Street. The pilot project was a cooperative effort of the city, the university, private contractors and the U.S. Department of Housing and Urban Development. ("Denver Post", April 19)

1979

### PARKS AND RECREATION

Mike Segrest, an assistant parks director from Austin, Texas, will take over Boulder's Parks and Recreation Department on May 4. ("Camera, April 11)

Sue Deans reported on May 9 in the "Camera" that:

Persistent soccer fans were rewarded ... when Boulder City Council agreed to spend \$127,000 to build at least four fields in north Boulder. The city Recreation Department has recommended constructing 19 more fields over the next six years, an issue which will be considered in a proposed recreation bond this fall.

The council took no action on a request made last week by Michael Aisner, director of the Red Zinger Classic bicycle race, for a \$5,000

cash contribution from the city.

City Manager Bob Westdyke pointed out that the city contributes to the race about \$4,800 in in-kind services such as public assistance and traffic control ... .

Nancy Traver wrote in the "Longmont Times-Call" dated April 23 that:

Warm spring weather has brought nude sunbathers back to Coot Lake, much to the displeasure of area residents.

... officials from the city of Boulder say no action is planned this year regarding nudity at the lake.

"We don't encourage people to go to Coot Lake and we're not going to spend money to provide services there, ... " (Paul) Swoboda said. "A drowning occurred there last summer. We've got signs that warn people they are swimming at their own risk ... (to reduce the city's liability as a land owner).

An addendum to a 1976 contract between the Police and Sheriff's Departments is expected to solve law enforcement problems at Coot Lake. City officers will now have full enforcement powers, though they must act under state rather than city laws.

A major complaint of nearby residents is the traffic congestion caused by lake users parking along 63rd Street.

Two residents met with Assistant District Attorney Bill Wise asking his office to sue the city and force closure of the lake. ("Camera", June 27, Marty Dee)

District Attorney Alex Hunter urged the city to control access to the lake and provide a parking area near it. City Manager Westdyke said he believes members of Council "do not wish to close the lake ... . ... what they want is a minimum of supervision ... consistent with the mitigation of some of the more serious problems." ("Camera", June 29, Marty Dee)

Marty Dee wrote in the "Camera" dated July 1:

(Coot Lake) ... has been a thorn in the side of neighbors ... for three years. Failure of either the police or sheriff's department to enforce the law is the main complaint. ... residents presently are waiting on the city's promises to act on ... problems around the lake.

After a shooting incident at the lake in July, three city and three

sheriff's officers were on duty during a weekend. The patrol will be maintained, but reduced, until a lifeguard is stationed there. City Council voted for a package of improvements which should make Coot Lake safer, cleaner and less rowdy. One improvement would be to provide limited off-street parking on the spillway of the Boulder Reservoir dam. ("Camera", July 18, Sue Deans)

In February, the City Council granted a 10-year lease contract to Doyle Eaton to manage Hidden Valley Ranch. When Eaton took over in May, he continued using the name Hidden Valley.

Meanwhile, Hays, (the prior concessionaire) opened another riding and boarding stable just four miles down the road to the north and claimed "Hidden Valley Ranch" as his business name.

The City Council voted to authorize the city attorney to go to court, if necessary, to stop the practice. ("Camera", June 6, Sue Deans)

Boulder District Judge Richard Dana ruled in favor of Hays, who requested a temporary restraining order to prohibit the city of Boulder and Doyle Eaton from using the name ... in their business. ("Camera", July 12, Marty Dee)

In court, Hays won exclusive rights to the name. Eaton was forced to use the name Boulder Valley Ranch.

Eaton said he has talked to the city about amending the lease because they did not deliver Hidden Valley Ranch and its business to him. He said the ranch is not producing the income expected and the ranch was in disrepair when he moved in.

Assistant City Manager Jim Piper said the city's position is that the terms of the lease probably cannot be changed because it is the result of a competitive bid process. ("Camera", October 31, Steven Wilhelm)

A master plan for parks was prepared in 1974, but was never adopted by the City Council. Boulder Parks and Recreation Director Michael Segrest said the Council was "concerned about its fiscal implications and about what was reasonable."

Mr. Segrest is currently preparing a new master plan outlining future park development in the city. He asked the City Council for a set of standards for where and how large parks should be, and suggested increases in park fees. ("Camera", August 1, Sue Deans)

The Parks and Recreation Master Plan was in an initial stage of approval in November. It outlines park development through 1992, in line with the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan. "It's more a policy statement and guide for the department and the policy-making bodies ...," according to Mike Segrest. ("Camera", November 29, Sue Deans)

Extensive lobbying from citizens led the Council to add a recreation therapist position to the 1980 budget. Rather than cut programs or raise fees across the board, the decision was to make selective fee increases in order to pay for a full-time person. The Special Recreation Services Program now operates with two full-time staff members. ("Camera", September 9, October 17, Sue Deans)

Jane Cracraft reported in the "Denver Post" of October 27 that a five-member committee had been named to advise the Boulder Parks and Recreation Department on the future of "Old Engine 30". Some of the committee members had helped with restoration work done last year on the engine, tender and cars.

#### OPEN SPACE

Lakewood Property Manager Jim Crain has been hired as Boulder's real estate and open space director. Boulder's open space is reserved for only "passive" recreation ... ("Camera", March 25, Linda Cornett)

In December the Council agreed to buy 120 acres of open space land that is "highly visible" from the Longmont Diagonal. The Open Space Board of Trustees moved up the priority on the land when rezoning was requested from the county which would have allowed its development. ("Camera", December 5, Sue Deans)

#### LIBRARIES

The City Council unanimously approved an agreement with the County Commissioners which calls for the county to provide health services to city and county residents through the Boulder County Health Department, and for the city to provide library services to county and Boulder residents. ("Camera", January 3, Linda Cornett)

There were many suggestions of how to distribute the "windfall" of excess funds which became available to the city when Gunbarrel decided against annexation.

Among these were the Boulder Historical Society's desire to reopen its Pioneer Museum at 1655 Broadway. The building, built in 1941 for use as a Safeway store, has never been remodeled. Operated as a museum for 19 years, it was closed down by the Fire Department a year ago because of an inadequate sprinkler system and fire exit, highly flammable artifacts and a lack of accessibility for the handicapped. The Society would like a contribution from the city as a "seed" for a fund-raising campaign. ("Camera", January 2, Linda Cornett)

The City Council approved the use of Carnegie Library to house documents, books, oral history, and photographs related to Boulder's history.

(Marcelee Gralapp said) "the library proposes to rehabilitate the facility with the use of federal funds." ... it will take a year or two to renovate the building and get the collection into usable form. The "centerpiece of the collection" would be a photographic resource center ... . Included would be the Paddock collection of photographs, including 38,000 original prints and 17,000 negatives ... . ("Camera", June 13, Sue Deans)

The Boulder Public Library Foundation and the Boulder Historical Society cooperated in holding a book sale on June 30 in the library auditorium.

An exhibition of Chinese art, gathered from the collections of Boulder residents and sponsored by the Boulder Public Library Foundation and the Colorado China Council went on display in March at the Boulder Arts Center. ("Camera", March 14)

A free Children's Concert series was sponsored by the library throughout the summer.

In June, the 25th annual Western Writer's Conference was held with the cooperation of the Boulder Public Library.

In September, the Boulder Public Library Foundation sponsored a weekend in San Francisco to view the King Tut exhibit. ("Camera", August 16)

The library paid for the printing of 500 bibliographies entitled "Women Work" which lists 15 books about women's work experience in Boulder. Reference librarian Lynn Dyba originated the idea and Sharon Akridge compiled and annotated the bibliography. ("Camera", October 7)

A series of programs for adults interested in children was held in October. It was sponsored by the Boulder Committee for the International Year of the Child, Boulder Public Library, and the professional staff of Boulder Valley Schools. ("Camera", October 9)

The library resumed its "Sunday Salons with the Masters" in October.

#### COMMUNICATIONS

Ken Frizell wrote in the "Camera" dated May 28:

Telecommunications Inc. (TCI), the Lakewood-based company which operates Boulder Cable TV recently disclosed plans to bring cable television to Boulder in a big way -- complete with home movies, sports broadcasts and a public access channel to be operated through Boulder Public Library.

City officials granted a revocable ... permit to Colorado Televents Inc. ... in 1964. In 1966, the permit was transferred to Community Communications and later to TCI. Boulder Cable has operated in the city for several years, but only to improve reception on University Hill.

In the "Camera" dated July 1, Ken Frizell reported:

A group of local residents ... announced plans to construct a new city-wide cable television system.

The partnership, Boulder Communications Co. said it intends to ask the Boulder City Council for a permit to operate a 36-channel, two-way cable network to feature public access, local origination programs, educational and governmental access channels as well as "pay" programming. In return for the permit, the city would receive 3 percent of the company's gross revenues. An additional 2 percent ... would be paid the city for support of the public access channel.

Sue Deans wrote in the "Camera" dated November 29 that:

Everybody on the Boulder City Council seems to agree the city should have cable television.

What they can't agree on is the best way to decide who should provide that service.

Paul Danish and Greg Lefferdink, with some support from George Boland, advocated a "laissez-faire" approach, where permits would be granted to anyone requesting them and the competition would ensure quality service.

Other council members, including Mayor Ruth Correll, seemed to support a city staff recommendation which would solicit proposals from various cable companies, evaluate them and allow the council to select one or more which best filled the city's needs.

Danish argued against the staff recommendations mainly because it would further delay expansion of the system and might cause problems with First Amendment rights or anti-monopoly regulations.

City Manager Bob Westdyke contended the high cost of wiring the city, estimated at about \$3 million, would raise the cost to consumers if several firms were involved.

Another concern expressed by several council members was the head start TCI, as present permit holder, would have on wiring the city.

Danish suggested a moratorium on TCI expansion, but City Attorney Joe de Raismes warned that could cause legal problems.

Bob Sample, former city telecommunications director, now acting as a consultant, said it would take at least six months either to select a firm through the proposal process or to issue detailed permits.

But simpler permits, not enumerating services the city expected, could be issued in a month to six weeks, he said.

Sue Deans again interviewed Bob Sample in the December 9 "Camera":

Sample said cable prospects are more optimistic now than earlier in the '70s.

"The economics have changed," the consultant said. "There's a larger array of services immediately available, beyond improved reception."

Recently installed satellite networks make it cheaper to provide special programming "at a low cost to the individual ... ."

Both Sample and Rich Varnes, director of media and programming at Boulder Public Library, agreed it would probably be best to license only one, or at most two, cable companies to operate in the city

On December 18, the Council approved an amendment to Ordinance 2846 to impose a temporary geographic limitation on the expansion of cable television facilities within the city for three months.

TCI officials claimed they had invested \$600,000 in the expansion and planned to start wiring homes in Table Mesa and Martin Park beginning in January. They indicated that serious legal problems would be the result of a moratorium. ("Camera", December 19, Sue Deans)

#### CULTURAL CENTER

The Chamber of Commerce has told the City Council that the \$750,000 left in their budget as a result of the defeat of the Gunbarrel annexation proposal should be set aside to help build a 750-seat theater as part of a city/university performing arts center.

The city-owned and operated theatre would be part of a hotel and convention center planned "on the old superblock site" bordered by Canyon, 9th, Walnut and 11th streets.

Arthur Everett, chairman of the Chamber Cultural Affairs Council, ... (wrote) ... that "if the multi-use development does not prove feasible,

then the theatre should be built on the original cultural center site between 13th and 14th streets south of Canyon Boulevard."

Architects Phillip J. Tabb and J Nold Midyette prepared plans for a proposed cultural center that incorporated three projects. A new \$4.5 million community performing arts facility, devoted to space for smaller productions; a \$1.6 million renovation of the Boulder Theater to accommodate concerts, musical productions and lectures; and a \$300,000 renovation of the Boulder Arts Center.

Both expressed concern that the proposal might be buried under other issues facing the city this year -- shopping centers, recreation facilities and pine beetles.

Cutting their proposal would most likely eliminate the Boulder Theater renovation. ("Camera", June 26, Sue Deans)

The City Council decided informally not to seek a bond issue to finance a cultural center this year. After that decision the Boulder Theater was put on the market by Historic Boulder Inc.

Festival Two architects J Nold Midyette and Phillip Tabb will do preliminary design work for the city's proposed performing arts center.

The two were approved by ... City Council ... to get plans into shape for a possible bond issue a year from now. ("Camera", October 3, Sue Deans)

Larry Caldwell wrote in the "Camera" of November 16 that:

The Boulder Arts Center opened this week with a new interior, a new name and ambitious plans ... . The center was remodeled during the past several weeks with \$80,000 of city money.

The remodeling increased the exhibition space to 5,000 square feet, which is twice what was available before.

The new name is the Boulder Center for the Visual Arts.

#### ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION

In May, a noise control ordinance variance was again granted to Feyline Presents Inc. for a Folsom Field concert.

Stunt pilots were asking the Federal Aviation Commission for more room to do aerobatics over Boulder County. Jim Adams is recommending an area south of Baseline Lake over the Marshall landfill.

Todd Malmsbury reported on February 22 in the "Camera" that radiation levels more than 40 times above normal were discovered at an old dumping site near the intersection of Pearl Street and Canyon Boulevard. Radioactive wastes were dumped at many sites in the early part of the century when radium-refining was common in the Denver-Boulder area.

Pam Novotny reported in the "Denver Post" on August 1 that Dick Shannon, the city's forester, said that the west side of Flagstaff Mountain is virtually denuded and that about 6,000 trees on 80 acres on the summit were lost this year to pine beetles.

Shannon and Jim Crain, director of Real Estate and Open Space for the city, are proposing a forest management program ... that would prevent heavy ... infestation by reducing the amount of "desireable habitat" available to the beetle.

The city decided to spend \$439,000 to try to stop the pine beetle and reduce the potential hazard of fire. A city crew will work in some areas and the state forest service will supervise private contractors in others. ("Camera", November 30, Todd Malmsbury)

#### ENERGY

Boulder did receive the \$242,000 two-year contract from the Department of Energy, hired Creative Land Use to develop the plan and appointed a citizen task force to oversee its implementation.

Susan Stolz is director of the Boulder Office of Energy Conservation. Her office will find the best ways to achieve energy savings and educate the public in what they can do to help meet the goal. ("Camera", October 28, Todd Malmsbury)

Ken Frizell wrote in the "Camera" dated March 9 that the city was told to expect a cut in supplies of diesel fuel. During the 1973 energy crisis,

the city fuel tanks occasionally ran dry ... .

Phil Stern, director of Colorado Project/Technical Information Project received a grant from the Department of Energy to study the economic feasibility of tapping the energy potential in Boulder's water supply. If water pressure were utilized for power generation, it could produce 5,000 kilowatts of electricity. ("Camera", November 25, Polly Powell)





## CHAPTER VI

### HOUSING AND HUMAN RELATIONS

1975

### HOUSING

In September, 1974, the city's contract with the firm it had hired to manage 292 low-income housing units was cancelled.

The Housing Authority selected another property management group. Before they could take over, the regional office of the Department of Housing and Urban Development (HUD) held up the new contract while they investigated the quality of past management. "HUD regards the Boulder authority as a renegade (for not managing the units itself)."

Housing Authority Chairman Swede Johnson said that he gave HUD an ultimatum that if they had not approved the new contract by a certain date, he would recommend that the Authority execute the contract without HUD's approval even if the city lost some federal subsidies. ("Camera", February 2, Ken Frizell)

Although eight housing sites with a total of 350 units have been built or purchased during the last four years, there are still hundreds of people needing low-income housing. The Housing Authority may be able to expand their program under the Housing and Community Development Act of 1974. Under that Act the federal agency will pay the difference between what a family can afford to pay for housing (usually 25 percent of their net income) and the market rent of the unit.

In addition, the Act provides funds for physical improvements to existing public housing.

The Housing Authority also sponsors Tenant Services funded jointly by the city and HUD. Family Outreach and Youth Outreach workers set up programs for young people and have helped set up day care facilities. Homemakers who visit and assist the elderly in low-income housing is another function of Tenant Services. (Town & Country Review, August 13)

In October, the Housing Authority announced they would participate in a new subsidized housing program which permits participants to seek housing on the open market. Known as the Section 8 Housing Assistance Payments Program, it will provide funds in the form of rent supplements as long as the selected units are within the Fair Market Rents established by HUD.

The Housing Authority received approval from the City Council to administer a program to rehabilitate low-income households. ("Camera", September 24, Ken Frizell) They received a grant of \$87,500 from the Colorado Division of Housing. Housing rehabilitation director Joe Cavanaugh said the money will be divided into 25 individual grants ... (and) is to be used to eliminate sub-standard housing conditions in the target areas (central "old" Boulder). ("Camera", December 9)

Ken Frizell reported in the "Camera" of August 19 that:

Boulder's on-again, off-again rental housing arbitration board is off again... . The board, which was first proposed in 1973 to handle disputes between landlords and tenants, was originally scheduled for a public hearing at tonight's regular council meeting.

But the Council decided to cancel it, wanting a study session on the topic first. The "administration and the Human Relations Commission who have been studying the board idea for the past several months have recommended Council take no action."

In the "Camera" dated October 14, Ken Frizell wrote:

A proposed rental housing arbitration board bit the dust once again this morning at a meeting of the Boulder City Council.

A joint committee of both landlords and tenants came close to agreeing ... last spring, but the idea collapsed after the Boulder Rental Property Owners Association withdrew its support and later formed its own board. The level of complaints (handled by that board) differs ... from figures supplied the city by the Boulder County Tenant's Organization, the District Attorney's office and other groups. According to those figures, there were a total of 1,215 complaints and inquiries made by landlords and tenants in the first six months of the year. Council elected ... to give the association's board at least another six months time to see if it is meeting the problem in Boulder.

Jane Shattuck reported in the "Colorado Daily" of December 3 that "The first major rent strike in Boulder in five years took place ... (when) ... twenty-six women ... withheld rent checks until promises were made by the landlord to make repairs the women feel are necessary for their safety and comfort." After arbitration between the two parties, led by a member of the Boulder County Tenant's Organization, they agreed to a truce.

### HUMAN RELATIONS

According to a report by the Human Relations Commission subcommittee on family stability, the incidence of divorce and separation in Boulder is extremely high and more services to help people through these family crises are needed. One of the recommendations is that the city should make resources available to help in assessing the need for more services, then provide seed money for their development and support based on demonstrated effectiveness. ("Camera", January 5)

The Womens Resource Center (WRC) opened last September (1974) as an outgrowth of the study done by the Task Force on Women and Child Care in Boulder.

The main objective of the WRC is to serve community women who need any kind of help, although "our particular concern is the single heads of households," explained board member Josie Heath.

The City Council allocated \$17,000 to be given to the WRC this year.  
("Camera, February 23, Jennine Heitman)

In April, the Human Relations Commission sent a resolution to City Council requesting the creation of a citizen board to mediate landlord-tenant disputes.

In May, reacting to pressure from local landlords, the Commission withdrew the proposal for the arbitration board.

In the spring, a survey was conducted of residents of seven low-income housing sites. Households without children were omitted from the survey. The seven sites were found to contain over four times the number of divorced and separated persons, and six to seven times the number of minorities than is found in the normal Boulder population.

Day care homes or centers were available to 25-30 percent of the respondents. However, only 9 to 13 percent used such facilities. If available and affordable, 40 percent would use day care homes or centers.

The study, initiated by the Human Resources Department, also indicated that the city's Chicanos see the police as their greatest threat. This part of the study was undertaken for several reasons, not the least of which was last year's bombing incidents in which six Chicanos were killed.  
("Camera", June 5)

The annual social report done by the Human Resources Department said that:

... there may be as many as 100 "street people" in Boulder currently, down ... from an estimated 350 year-round in 1973. They have "generally moved from the hill business area to the central city ... and have taken to alcohol as a preferred drug." Many "have expressed their desire to be jailed in the new Justice Center when the facility opens, as the accommodations are considered to be excellent."

The Boulder Communications Center will close its doors on December 31. Gayle Mertz, president of its board of directors and in on its beginning in 1969, has found it more and more difficult to get public and private support for the center. Volunteers were harder to recruit as the nature of the center's clients changed from young transients to a few intransigent hardcore hippies who may be beyond rehabilitation. ("Denver Post, December 28, Jane Cracraft)

The Council voted in December to allocate \$21,000 from the federal Community Development funds for design of a new senior citizen's center. ("Camera", December 17, Ken Frizell)

Ever since its founding in June 1969, the center had occupied the basement of the Courthouse Annex at the corner of 13th and Spruce Streets.

Its 5,000 square feet, which once had been the bar for the Boulder Elks Club, is now inadequate to serve more than 3,700 members. A flight of stairs makes access difficult for many elderly persons, and there is no nearby parking.

The center's director since its founding has been LuAnne Michelson who is leaving the job. She has encouraged members to lobby for a new facility. ("Denver Post", December 15, Jane Cracraft)

1976

### HOUSING

In a study done for the County Housing Authority, it was reported that Boulder had the lowest vacancy rate and the tightest housing market in the county. This was despite the construction of more than 1200 units a year during the past six years. "Vacancy rates and average rents were obtained

through a ... survey of 10,655 rental housing units in the county with four or more units that are at least one year old." ("Camera", February 8, John Leach)

#### HUMAN RELATIONS

About 350 people are booked into and released from the Boulder County jail every month. Many find freedom a workless and homeless situation. The release services office of the Human Resources Department tries to find them food, clothing and a temporary place to stay. Hill House and the Boulder Society of Friends usually offer their services on a temporary basis. The office has sometimes placed people with "Loren Schnell, the proprietor of the city's unofficial crash pad." ("Colorado Daily", February 24, Jeff Morgan)

The social report of the Human Resources Department indicated that almost 93 percent of all Boulderites are white. Less than five per cent are Chicano. Less than one per cent are black. More than half are women. And in the past year there were more marriages, more divorces and more reported cases of child abuse. ("Camera", April 16, Ken Frizell)

Susan Townsend reported in the "Town and Country Review" of August 18 that:

When Boulder's city administration began its affirmative action plan in 1974, 24.2 percent of the city's employees were women and over half of them held clerical jobs. Little has changed in two years: 26.5 percent of the employees are women and the majority still hold clerical jobs. At the same time, the percent of minorities working for the city has risen from 8.9 percent in 1974 to 10.3 percent in 1976.

The City Council authorized the City Manager to sign a contract for architectural services for a new senior citizens' center. Several Council members questioned awarding a contract before selecting a site. ("Camera",

May 26)

Both the Planning Board and the Parks and Recreation Advisory Board will recommend to City Council that a new senior citizens' center be located on land behind the Park Central building. The major consideration in the decision was the amount of money available for the center which meant that it had to be located on city-owned land. ("Camera", July 23)

In October, the Planning Board voted unanimously to recommend to Council a senior center site at 21st Street and Canyon Boulevard. Local senior citizen groups had approved the location because it is convenient to public transportation and directly east of Walnut Place, a city-owned apartment complex for low-income elderly. ("Camera", October 1, Renda Dale)

Added land was necessary at that location in addition to city parkland to make the building more accessible and to lessen its impact on the park. The lots to the east could not be acquired without condemnation. As a result, in December, the site for the center was returned to the city employee parking area at 11th and Arapahoe. ("Town & Country", December 22)

1977

### HOUSING

Jane Cracraft reported in the "Denver Post" dated February 27:

Suddenly the average price of a house listed for sale in Boulder is \$75,100.

Prices have been going up nearly 8 per cent per month ever since Councilman Paul Danish's slow-growth plan was narrowly approved by voters last November.

The Danish plan ... won't take effect until March 10. But the psychology of it has caused an unprecedented housing panic here.

"The most interesting thing I see is that more and more people are being priced out of the market, with some people buying four, five, six, even 10 homes and renting them out to the people who can't buy them," said Duane Fry, who has sold real estate and managed rental property in

Boulder for more than 15 years.

In the "Camera" of May 1, Steve Hoffman reported that:

Housing prices in the Boulder area have been increasing steadily since the passage of the Danish plan last fall but remain fairly consistent with national trends, according to studies recently completed by PLAN Boulder and the Boulder Board of Realtors.

In June, the Chamber of Commerce urged the City Council to consider actions leading to repeal of the Danish plan and to review existing government regulations and fees pertaining to construction. They called for a new version of Martin Acres.

It is our contention that a mixture of apartment-condominium dwellers and high-cost home occupants doesn't represent a favorable population mix. It is instead an "elitist" approach to the future development of Boulder, at the expense of the middle income person. ("Denver Post", June 21, Jane Cracraft)

The 1977 social report of the city's Department of Human Resources discounts the importance of growth control in increasing the cost of housing.

Researcher Bill Conger said that housing prices seem to be increasing at about 21 per cent a year in Boulder, compared with 19 per cent for the western states.

The Conger report makes it clear that Boulder's growth rate has been held well below that of other towns in the county in the 1970s. ("Denver Post", October 28, Jane Cracraft)

Sarah Hoover wrote in the "Camera" of November 14 that:

A growing number of apartments in Boulder are being taken off the rental market and sold as condominiums in a move by building owners to cut expenses and cash in on a demand for middle-income housing.

A squeeze on the number of apartments available will not only make it more difficult but also more expensive for students, elderly persons and other renters to live here.

"Obviously it's making a dent in the rental market," said Ed Gawf in the city planning department. "But look at it the other way. It fills a need for moderately priced housing."

Stephen Foehr wrote in the "Colorado Daily" dated December 8:

The cost of housing for low- and moderate-income Boulder residents has worsened in the last year and no improvement is anticipated for the near future.

Reasons for the spiralling housing costs are varied. Limited supply of land, inflated construction costs, higher interest on home loans, stagnant wages and strict city land-use regulations have contributed to keeping the supply of new housing well below the demand in Boulder.

The city took out a 90-day option on January 27 to buy the western portion of the block bounded by Ninth Street, Canyon Boulevard and Walnut Street. The Boulder Housing Authority is planning up to 100 apartments for the low-income elderly and the handicapped on the site.

It would be privately owned and managed, but receive substantial financial help from the federal and state governments.

An added incentive is that the ... units would be exempt from the Danish growth control plan.

The eastern end (the old Arnold Brothers Ford site) is already owned by the city, but is leased to RTD (Regional Transportation District) for its present bus terminal until the end of 1978.

Mayor Frank Buchanan led council to a consensus ... that the city ... renew the present ... option for a year when it expires. ("Camera", February 23, Steve Hoffman)

After a change in the Army Corps of Engineers flood study which expanded the Boulder Creek floodway, the Canyon housing site was found to be at risk. Final approval would have to depend on coming up with a way to remove the land from the floodway. ("Camera", June 1)

The Boulder Housing Authority has grants available to residents of the core area so they may bring their homes up to the standards in the city code. If owners continue to occupy their homes the money need not be paid back. If they sell it within five years, they must pay back a pro-rated percentage of the grant. ("Camera", July 31)

#### HUMAN RELATIONS

Steve Hoffman wrote in the "Camera" dated February 16 that the City Council voted unanimously to use its condemnation powers to acquire four lots surrounding the Green Mountain Grainery on Arapahoe for a municipal employee parking lot. The acquisition would free the present parking acreage so a senior citizens' center can be constructed. "The irony of the whole situation," noted Councilman Wayne Hutchens, "is that the city avoided locating the center at Canyon Park ... because it would have involved the condemnation of owner-occupied housing."

City Manager Bob Westdyke recommended a change for the site of the center from 11th and Arapahoe because of a recently released Army Corps of Engineers report that placed that location in the path of flood waters in a 100-year flood. ("Town & Country Review, June 1)

With little discussion and without a dissenting vote, the City Council selected a new site on the northeast corner of Ninth and Arapahoe for the senior center.

The Human Relations Commission angrily rejected City Manager Bob Westdyke's solution to provide public restrooms for the Downtown Boulder Mall. The proposal was that restrooms be made available at the County Courthouse, at a parking garage proposed for Walnut Street, and at downtown businesses.

Reasons given for rejecting the City Manager's proposal were that the courthouse building is locked after 5 p.m., that restrooms of local businesses are inaccessible to the handicapped, and that the parking garage, even if approved, would take two years to build.

Commission members were in favor of a freestanding building owned and maintained by the city. Another possibility mentioned was converting a

storefront into a public restroom. ("Camera", April 12)

The city agreed to participate in the construction of a facility for the Alcohol and Drug Abuse Division of the Boulder County Health Department. Boulder committed \$50,000 in federal Economic Development Administration funds. ("Longmont Times-Call", July 12, Tim Lowry)

The Human Resources Department has set up a program to provide temporary emergency housing to assist small families, abused wives or singles in need of refuge. It is an alternative to the more than \$30,000 in public funds that have been spent to house families in public accommodations. ("Town & Country Review", August 17, Nan Pirnack)

The city of Boulder and the school district each provide \$11,000 a year toward the funding of the Community School Project. County-controlled Public Service Employment grants pay the salaries of coordinators at three junior high schools. ("Camera", December 14, Linda Castrone.)

1978

### HOUSING

Linda Cornett wrote in the "Camera" dated August 13:

(City Housing Director John) Hooyer ... says the machinations of the Danish plan are probably keeping prices down. ... heavy weight is given to the number of low and moderate income units included in each proposal.

"I personally see one of the advantages of the growth control ordinance is that there are probably more units that will be available to moderate income families than if we had a different system," Hooyer said.

... aside from insisting that the homes are sold for the projected (moderate) price to a first buyer, the city is helpless to keep prices down. "Once a house is sold a second time it is basically out of our sphere of influence."

A report on Boulder's housing prices by the city's Department of Human Resources indicated that the increase in housing prices has dropped to less than four percent in recent months. ("Camera", October 1, Linda Cornett)

A trade was made by the Housing Authority which reached an agreement to sell a city-owned apartment building in south Boulder for a sum of money plus a three acre piece of land on North Broadway. The northern site would eventually be used for low-income apartments. ("Camera", November 5, Linda Cornett)

In July, the Denver Regional Council of Governments (DRCOG) approved the Regional Housing Opportunity Plan (RHOP) which sets goals for federally-funded housing projects in the Denver area, covering five counties.

Among other things, RHOP includes a proposal to create a metropolitan housing district. The district, an expansion of the Denver Housing Authority, would administer federal grants for low-income housing projects, while DRCOG's "A-95 Review" process would allow the DRCOG to make negative comments on recommendations or denial of funding for local city and county housing authorities.

The Boulder City Council approved RHOP in December but attached stiff conditions recommended by Housing Director John Hooyer which, in effect, "gutted" the plan.

Hooyer's conditions, which were approved unanimously by the nine-member council, ... included outright rejection of the metropolitan housing district, A-95 Review's right to deny a community funding and the precedence of RHOP goals and policies over city goals and policies. ("Longmont Times-Call", December 20, Tim Alger)

According to the Boulder County Tenant's Organization (BCTO), rents have increased at an exorbitant rate this year. Owners of rental property blame higher property taxes and utility bills.

The tenants union says the rent increases are caused by two factors: "There has been a significant decline in the number of rental units available due to conversion of existing rental properties to

condominiums and businesses," they said. To date approximately 600 existing rental units have been converted ... .

The second factor is "Rampant speculation on existing rental income properties results in highly inflated purchase prices ... ."

The BCTO proposed several actions which they felt the city must take to address the rental housing crisis. ("Town & Country", September 6)

In December the Council approved an ordinance which would require owners of apartment buildings to give their tenants 120 days' notice before selling the apartments as condominiums. ("Camera", December 6, Linda Cornett)

#### HUMAN RELATIONS

In the "Camera" of March 9, Steve Hoffman wrote:

City plans for building a new senior citizens center hit another snag ... when construction bids came in at 50 percent and more over the \$745,000 that had been budgeted.

Architect Bill Bowen, a member of the local firm hired by the city to design the new center, said that he saw three options for the City Council to consider in light of the cost overruns -- abandon the project, find some extra money, or scale down the building.

He said that because the project is being backed by federal Community Development funds, contractors must comply with regulations governing wages and benefits, and must also devise and administer an affirmative action program for hiring minority workers.

He also pointed to the fact that, due to the small size of the Ninth and Arapahoe site, parking had to be provided in spaces made available by putting part of the building on stilts.

That added about \$150,000 in extra construction costs ... .

Steve Hoffman reported in the "Camera" dated April 5 that:

A new activity center for Boulder's senior citizens finally got off the ground ... -- partly by being put back on the ground by the City Council.

... in a compromise solution aimed at avoiding a horrendous cost overrun, the council decided to build the new center on grade.

(This) ... will mean less parking for seniors ... . By not elevating the building 14 parking spaces will be lost ... .

Likely sources (to get the extra money needed to build the center) include ... federal money (which) has been committed to completing the remodeling now underway in the municipal building, but has not been spent.

At their next meeting, the Council unanimously decided the best place to find the extra money is in the 1978 general fund balance.

Community Development funds from the city were granted for the purchase of a Safehouse. The Women's Resource Center took possession on February 7.

A public meeting sponsored by the Police Department was held in June to discuss the transient problem. A number of merchants complained of drunkenness, panhandling, shoplifting and general disorderly conduct in Central Park and on the Downtown Mall. They requested more patrols and greater diligence in enforcing the laws.

Assistant City Attorney Phil Palmer said most of the violations are petty ones and urged the business people to be firm in signing complaints.

Lieutenant Bob Diezi of the Police Department said that officers would "continue to arrest and re-arrest these people as many times as necessary, until they have the kinds of records that will make judges sentence them." ("Denver Post", June 29, Jane Cracraft)

"Our House", opened in 1970 by the city and taken over in 1973 by the Boulder County Mental Health Center, had its federal funding cut in half this year. The National Institute of Drug Abuse changed its criteria for funding to one for documentable drug treatment cases rather than for overall teen-age counseling. ("Camera", March 21, Linda Castrone) The City Council granted the facility \$22,000 thus "preventing its threatened demise." ("Colorado Daily", April 19, Stephen Foehr)

Linda Cornett reported in the "Camera" dated September 23 that the Human Relations Commission is not satisfied with the progress made in reaching the city's Affirmative Action goals.

Overall, since the city set its goals in 1975, the percentage of women employed ... has increased from 15.8 to 30.5 percent ... . The

goal is 38.5 percent women.

Minorities increased from 9.8 to 12.4 percent of the city's labor force, short of the 14.1 percent goal.

The city's poorest showing is in the area of officials and administrators. There, the percentage of women has decreased by almost five percent, to 8.3 percent ... . Minorities now make up 5.6 percent of administrators, a 1.9 percent increase.

1979

#### HOUSING

Canyon Pointe, the city's new housing for the elderly and handicapped between Walnut Street and Canyon Boulevard, is being built with federal grant money. It provides 82 one-bedroom units along with community rooms, a greenhouse, and covered parking. Eight apartments are specially fitted for the handicapped. The building will be solar-heated for both hot water and space heating needs under an additional grant. The solar energy is expected to provide approximately 50 percent of total energy needs.

The project constitutes Phase I of Canyon Pointe ... . The remainder of the project ... will be devoted to mixed use, with 63 condominium units and various office and commercial enterprises, including a neighborhood shopping facility. ("Town & Country", January 10 and "Camera", December 17, Sue Deans)

The Free Inn, which offered free shelter to hundreds of displaced persons last year, has a new name, a new direction, a new staff and is working on a new reputation.

The building was operated at no charge since April 1978 by members of a loosely-knit Christian organization calling itself the Christ Brotherhood.

It was closed after complaints rolled in that it was becoming a "crash pad".

The Boulder Housing Authority signed for a loan on the building and agreed to pick up monthly payments when the Emergency Coalition could not continue payments.

The Emergency Coalition, five social service agencies, will call the facility ECHO (Emergency Coalition Housing Opportunity) House. ("Camera", March 29, Linda Cornett)

The results of a survey of the city's housing situation, conducted by Colorado University's Business Research Division, were released. Sponsored by the city and the university, the survey results will be used in an effort to relieve the housing crunch. Among other findings was the statistic that almost half the households in the Boulder area are occupied by renters and of those twenty-two percent are rented by students.

A survey by the Colorado Public Interest Research Group indicates that during the last year, rents on efficiency units jumped 16 percent and those on one- and three-bedroom units rose 11 percent. ("Camera, May 15)

The most blatant example of discrimination against students in Boulder is the city's occupancy ordinance, according to a study by the Colorado Public Interest Research Group (COPIRG).

(The ordinance) also may be the law students violate more than any other.

(It) provides that no more than three unrelated persons may live together in one dwelling unit. Its purpose is to preserve the character and quality of life in low-density residential areas.

It is based on the concept that more than three persons will add to the amount of traffic, noise and clutter. ("Camera", May 17, Marty Dee)

The study by COPIRG, a student-funded consumer group, estimates that 975 students were breaking the occupancy law. If students become aggressive against the occupancy ordinance, it could cause confrontation between them and neighborhood groups. Ted Tedesco, CU vice chancellor of administration and planning said:

"Politically speaking, city government has made a strong pitch for preservation of neighborhoods", noting it would be hard now for council to turn around and push for higher occupancy.

(Ken) Ziebarth (of the Martin Acres Neighborhood Association) suggested that rent control might pick up support from both students and homeowners. Students want stable rents, he said, and homeowners who prefer owner-occupied homes for neighbors would support rent control if they think it will discourage further conversion of homes to rentals.

#### HUMAN RELATIONS

John Torres, director of the new Senior Center at Ninth and Arapahoe said that when the new building opens this week, it will be a welcome transition from the old one. There will be new programs and improved food service. The food service center will provide lunches and dinners and Meals on Wheels will contract for its food preparation for the homebound.

The center has about 4,000 members now, compared with 300 when the program started in 1969. ("Camera", June 2, Sue Deans)

The Chamber of Commerce released the results of a six-month study by their representatives, the Police and Sheriff's Departments, the Parks Department and downtown merchants. It recommends writing and enforcing a city law against public drunkenness, an increase in police patrols, putting city money behind an accelerated court schedule, and an increase in lighting in the parks. ("Camera", March 22, Linda Cornett)

A report from a subcommittee of the Human Relations Commission on ways to deal with transients recommended recruiting experienced or specially trained police to patrol the areas where transients congregate, and educating the public on its rights in dealing with the transients. ("Camera", March 20, Linda Cornett)

Sue Deans wrote in the October 21 issue of the "Camera" that there is a strong possibility of a confrontation between the street people and local youths. There were complaints from both groups claiming assaults and drug-dealing. The City Manager said he had not received any calls about street people problems for a month.

"In fact, I would say that this summer we've had less of a problem than in any other year," he added. "And I think there's a direct correlation with increased police patrols downtown."

Kathleen Wieck reported in the "Colorado Daily" of October 24 that the Safehouse is now open and houses 12 women and their children. The Safehouse attempts to provide a variety of services to give women the courage, skills and self-esteem necessary to make it on their own.

"Our House", a drop-in center for teenagers that was to close August 31 because of lack of funding, has been closed early due to vandalism. ("Camera", August 9)

Boulder's Youth Action Center (YAC) is moving from the former garage at Broadway and Arapahoe which has housed it since 1977. The YAC was formed to deal with youths arrested for a first or minor offense, offering them counseling or activities at the center rather than a trip through the judicial system. ("Camera", January 3)

The YAC moved to a small house near the new Senior Center. Since it started, the Youth Center has dealt with 850 teenagers, many of them needing counseling with family-related problems. It is funded through the city and the state. ("Camera", July 19, Sue Deans)

Penfield Tate, a member of the board of directors of San Juan del Centro, a privately owned, low-income housing project, said the board is seeking funding from city and county sources in order to continue the project's day care center, called the Little School.

San Juan has been promised federal funds for repairs only if the project stops funding an after-school day care center on the premises. ("Denver Post", September 7, Pam Patrick-Novotny)





## CHAPTER VII

### PUBLIC SAFETY

1975

#### OFFENSES

In January, the Council equalized dog license fees, ending a four-year-old policy of charging \$10 for unspayed female dogs. They also authorized the City Manager to execute a contract with the Boulder County Humane Society for them to continue to provide facilities for dogs impounded by city wardens. ("Camera", January 8)

In April, the Council voted to have the administration proceed with arrangements whereby the Humane Society will take over enforcement of the city's dog control ordinance. ("Camera", April 2)

### FIRE AND POLICE

In the "Camera" dated January 10, Barry Koltnow reported:

Boulder police officials, as recently as six weeks ago, were maintaining extensive intelligence files on City Council members and other persons involved in local controversies . . . .

The files, initiated by former Police Chief Donald Vendel, apparently were destroyed at the orders of Acting Chief Lowell Friesen shortly after he took office in November.

According to members of the Police Department, the files were maintained in order to protect school board members and other individuals involved in controversy. There were increased patrols after an arson fire at the school board meeting room, and similar procedure was followed during the sexual preference ordinance controversy when "names, addresses and other information were collected on Council members and Recall Committee leaders."

The City Manager announced in January that out of the 80 applicants who originally applied for the police chief's position, six finalists have been chosen. The lists of applicants had included four members of the department, but two, Acting Chief Lowell Friesen and Detective Captain Kelly Gaskill, withdrew their names. The other two, Captain C. H. Fullen and Lieutenant Larry Williams were informed that they were not among the finalists.

Chosen for the top position in the Boulder Police Department was John Barber who assumed his office March 1. He advocated the "team" concept of management and the revision of the controversial rules and procedures which had restricted the personal lives of police officers. He vowed "that no secret files ... or surveillance would be conducted on public officials or private citizens."

Barber said the key to any firearm use by an officer is not the (hollow-point) bullet but training ... . I ... want them to be proficient in their human relations skills as well. ("Camera", February 9, Barry Koltnow)

Chief Barber wants to develop a neighborhood "team policing" plan in which foot patrols would supplement auto patrols. He wants a greater role for women on the force and more coordination among law enforcement agencies. ("Colorado Daily", April 3, Jennifer Gavin)

Early in his tenure, Chief Barber received a grant from the Law Enforcement Assistance Administration to start his team policing concept.

Part of the grant money will be used to develop human relations and communications skills among the officers. ("Camera", May 27, Ken Frizell)

The University Hill Task Force, which was formed in 1973 to clean up that high-crime area, moved into the downtown. Lieutenant Larry Williams, who had been chosen to head the Task Force by former Chief Donald Vendel,

was still its leader, but not its commander.

According to Williams, the rank structure has been dropped and all 10 members have an equal role in decisions of policy. "Traditionally law enforcement has been a quasi-military program. But we use the collective management system. Those on the force are face-to-face with the problem, so they've got input and we don't dictate to them." ("Camera", January 26, Barbara Vobejda)

Lieutenant Williams was the commander of an elite Special Enforcement Unit (SEU) which was set up by former Chief Vendel in 1968. Chief Barber revealed the squad's existence.

The work is strictly volunteer, provides no extra pay and produces anything but the spectacular and exciting life that viewers see on the screen ... .

There are four situations which would immediately come under the jurisdiction of the SEU: hostage situations, barricaded gunmen, snipers and visiting dignitaries.

In the squad's seven years, it has been called out only five times. ("Camera", April 27, Barry Koltnow)

A study of the Police Department initiated last year was delivered to the City Council in April. Among changes it recommended were that team-policing be adopted (already begun by Chief John Barber), that no files be kept because of an individual's association or membership in groups, and that animal control and parking enforcement functions be removed from the department.

The study, headed by Louis Dow ... was conducted as a means of assessing the department budget requests and, more importantly, because some council members felt certain administrative and procedural changes were needed in the department in view of Boulder's changing social environment. ("Camera", April 6, Barry Koltnow)

The City Council felt the report was inadequate and should be expanded because recommendations in the study frequently bore no relation to the rest of the report. One section contained the results of a survey which indicated citizens want a voice in policy-setting and civilian review. This was not included in the recommendations.

The review board idea (in which some council members expressed interest) is not favored by police officers, and was criticized by

Barber ... . ("Camera", April 30, Ken Frizell)

Another Law Enforcement Assistance Administration grant was used to set up a juvenile offender's program for the local city and county governments in 1973. For first offenders and minor offenses, the whole case may be handled within the Police Department with counseling. Another option is the work program. Only in severe cases are juveniles referred to court. ("Town & Country", May 21)

Dog control was removed from the jurisdiction of the Police Department when the City Council signed a contract with the Boulder Humane Society. The Society will assume the majority of operational responsibility for animal control for an annual fee of \$59,000. The police officers who are animal control officers will be transferred within the department. ("Town & Country", June 4)

Jane Cracraft reported in the "Denver Post" of June 4:

As a part of the Boulder Police Department's campaign to get officers closer to people, the force has added a mounted patrolman. Lloyd Durfee ... rides one of his own horses daily along Boulder Creek from 6th Street to 30th Street.

In the "Camera" dated September 21, Barry Koltnow wrote:

The City of Boulder's self-proclaimed war on drugs has been forced into a hasty retreat -- a victim of a tight budget and a soaring crime rate.

(Police Chief) Barber has disbanded his new 10-man narcotics squad and reassigned the team to help in combating a summer-long crime wave ... .

In 1974, the city basked in the warmth of a 9 per cent decrease in serious crime. During the first nine months of 1975, Boulder's crime rate has been about 28 per cent over last year.

Mr. Koltnow covered the police beat again on October 1:

Boulder's police chief revealed ... that the city's new experimental airplane has been sent back to its manufacturer because of a lack of federal funds.

The single-engine Cessna ... had been loaned to the city in anticipation of funding from the ... Law Enforcement Assistance Administration.

Ken Frizell wrote in the "Camera" dated October 15:

The Boulder City Council ... clipped the budding wings of the Police Department by flatly refusing to even consider allowing the department to buy a helicopter.

A week later, Councilman George Boland attempted to get Council to take a second look at Chief Barber's proposal to lease a helicopter, but was voted down 5-4.

In the October 30 "Camera", Bill Conti wrote:

A man who was fired from the Boulder Police Department in June, 1973 has been reinstated to the force with back pay.

Philip F. DiLeo filed a suit against the City of Boulder in December 1973 ... DiLeo's attorney attacked the constitutionality of the police department's regulations.

District Court Judge Horace B. Holmes ruled ... that the reasons for DiLeo's dismissal ... were not justified.

Barry Koltnow reported in the "Camera" of October 31:

The Boulder Police Benefit Association (BPBA) voted unanimously ... to oppose the court-ordered reinstatement of Philip DiLeo and to ask City Council to appeal that reinstatement.

After working for the Police Department for 11 days in November, DiLeo resigned.

In a meeting with Chief Barber, the City Manager authorized him to replace six police officers who had left the force during the year. Evidently the Police Department budget had been depleted so the added cost had to come from the city hall addition fund.

According to the city manager, the department went over budget this year because of unanticipated overtime caused by a manpower shortage, and early retirements of former Police Chief Don Vendel and Capt. Donald Hopkins.

The city manager emphasized that the ... meeting had been planned in advance to discuss the budget but it took on added significance when rumors of a massive police sick-out ... began circulating.

The rumors started after two police officers were suspended for refusing to patrol ... in protest against the department's officer

shortage.

Members of the Boulder Police Benefit Association ... would not support the idea of a "blue flu". However, they announced they would fight for up to 25 more officers who they feel are needed. ("Camera", November 2, Barry Koltnow)

Barry Koltnow reported on December 13:

The City of Boulder's banana-colored patrol cars have turned out to be lemons in disguise.

Based on the performance record of the cars during their first six months on the city's streets, Barber ... told the Camera ... that the department intends to move to an intermediate-sized patrol car ... .

In March, the City Council met to consider several money-saving measures in order to keep the city within its budget. Among the items cut would be the construction of a fourth fire station in south Boulder, the transfer of the fire dispatch operation to the emergency number, and the deletion of the money for remodeling of existing fire stations to accomodate women firefighters. Boulder Fire Department members were out in force to protest the deletion of funds. ("Camera", March 12, Ken Frizell)

Budget difficulties continued to escalate the city's problems. In the "Camera" of May 25, Mr. Frizell reported:

The dispute ... (between the city manager and the fire department) ... involves the city's current budget problems and a plan by Twitchell and Assistant City Manager Bob Westdyke to have Boulder firemen perform additional, non-fire-related duties "in exchange for additional 1976 salaries."

Boulder City Councilman George Boland ... has interceded on behalf of the firemen. (He) angrily said the plan, which would see firemen performing such tasks as repairing water and parking meters ... is "the most ridiculous thing I've ever heard."

Boland warned, and the head of the firefighters' union has agreed, that having firemen perform other duties will seriously damage morale.

Both Twitchell and Westdyke ... argue that the additional duties will help save the city money and thus avoid more cut backs in all departmental budgets, including the fire department. (They) plan to bring the matter before the council ... .

The City Council, however, declined to become involved in the dispute.

In June, a new regional fire training center was approved by the County Commissioners. It will provide numerous fire training facilities for more than 20 fire departments across the county.

It will be financed by contractual agreements from the City of Boulder which will donate \$10,000 per year for eight years and by Boulder County which will appropriate \$2,500 for eight years to the \$100,000 project. ("Longmont Times-Call", June 10)

#### CORRECTIONS

By January, the Justice Center was about 65 percent completed. Hobart Wagener Associates were chosen to design and handle construction, and Mead & Mount Construction of Denver is the general contractor. The roof of the building is flat to permit easy expansion vertically if needed in the future. ("Town & Country", January 1, Barbara Gigone)

On July 17, Mayor Penfield Tate signed a quarter million dollar check over to Boulder County Commission Chairman Wally Toeve after which both signed a lease agreement allowing the city approximately 20,000 square feet in the new justice complex. Boulder's check was the final installment for its \$1 million share of costs for the \$8 million center. ("Longmont Times-Call", July 17)

A study of the parking facilities at the Justice Center showed a shortage of almost 300 parking spaces even before it was open.

By the end of the year some of the offices in the Justice Center were occupied and the Sheriff's Department was in transition. However, the late arrival of locks and the need to test electronic equipment delayed the transfer of jail inmates.

#### FLOODS

In January, the criteria for delineation of the Boulder Creek floodway was being discussed. At issue was the interpretation of the floodway area and the meaning of the terms "significant depths" and "significant velocities". Local environmentalists feared that further encroachment into the area needed to convey floodwaters would necessitate channelization.

The City Council adopted strict criteria defining the floodway in February. Flood Control Director Ted Dieffenderfer told Council that the replacement of the Ninth Street bridge and possible replacement of the 17th Street bridge would be necessary to change the floodway to any degree. He suggested that bridge replacement might be accomplished through bonding of the transportation fund monies. ("Town & Country", February 19)

The Planning Board voted in July to approve a map of the Boulder Creek floodway. Some changes suggested by the Planning Department were not approved, such as those related to non-conforming uses, flood-proofing requirements, and the expansion of structures already within the floodway. ("Camera", July 18)

Ken Frizell reported in the "Camera" of September 3 that the Council had finally adopted official floodway maps for Boulder Creek.

But that action did not come until after three hours of debate over the accuracy of the maps and a series of amendments to the city's floodplain regulations.

The amendments ... will restrict development in the area of the floodplain.

But exactly where the floodplain lies was the subject of argument ... as representatives of both the Flatiron Companies and Arapahoe Chemicals ... accused the city of adopting inaccurate maps.

City Manager Archie Twitchell admitted that the maps may not reflect recent topographical changes ... (but) ... recommended council approve the maps with the understanding that they will not become effective until the new amendments to the floodplain regulations take effect in several weeks.

In the "Denver Post" dated June 11, Jane Cracraft reported:

In the Boulder city budget cutback, one of the departments affected most sharply is the flood-control utility, which ran \$170,000 into the red in 1974.

City Manager Archie Twitchell said the budget overrun was caused by three factors: the new flood-control utility was slow in assessing monthly fees (\$1 for a typical house) and didn't collect any revenue in the first two months of 1974; the estimate of the amount of private land was 20 per cent too high; the utility moved right ahead with detailed planning on flood-control projects before enough money had come in to finance the planning and engineering work.

He noted that a half-million dollar project to control flooding in south and east Boulder is under construction now on the Viele Lake channel in cooperation with the Urban Drainage ...(and Flood Control) ... District.

In July, the Council voted to authorize City Attorney Walt Wagenhals to initiate actions to collect the city's flood control fee from about a dozen people who have not paid.

The city has been reluctant to force the collection of delinquent accounts until final adoption of specific floodway criteria.

The city was also buoyed by a recent court decision which upheld an earlier dismissal of a (lawsuit) filed by four residents who charged the city with illegally allowing construction in the flood plain. ("Camera", July 2, Ken Frizell)

### WEATHER

High winds hit the county in April, but were marked at only 68 miles per hour in Boulder. A gust of 100 mph was recorded at Southern Hills Junior High in October. The school's meter only records as high as 100 mph. The wind gauge on top of the National Center for Atmospheric Research was down for repairs at the time.

#### EMERGENCY MEDICAL SERVICE

The A-1 Ambulance was given 30 days to vacate its location at Ninth Street and Canyon Boulevard, the former site of Arnold Brothers Ford and the proposed location for the RTD garage. Both the city and the county lease ambulance services from A-1. ("Camera", August 7, Ken Frizell)

1976

### FIRE AND POLICE

Bill Jordan wrote in the "Camera" of January 2:

The Boulder Police Benefit Association has launched a verbal anti-aircraft barrage at the helicopter hopes of Police Chief John Barber.

Barber has arranged for several council members, including some just elected, to take rides over the city in the Hughes helicopter during its recent visits.

The (Police Benefit Association) asked council to "reject Chief Barber's capricious scheme to purchase a helicopter. Perhaps Chief Barber can be directed to use our funds more intelligently."

The City Manager and the police chief evidently agreed not to put the question before the new Council.

"Camera" staffer Larry Caldwell reported on March 15:

The city's police liability insurance expires today and to get a new policy will cost \$61,200. Last year the same policy cost only \$8,515.

The policy covers law enforcement officers against damage suits for false arrests, personal injury, bodily injury and property damage.

Such policies are being called malpractice insurance for policemen because the dramatic increase in premiums are directly related to large claims against law officers.

Police Chief John C. Barber resigned in May because "The city government is just not confident with the way the Police Department is being managed." Acting City Manager Bob Westdyke said that the dissatisfaction of the Police Benefit Association was "one of a series of factors" that precipitated his request that Barber resign. ("Camera", May 18, Larry Caldwell)

Within ten days, Acting City Manager Westdyke had announced the appointment of Boulder County Undersheriff David J. Voorhis to become Acting Police Chief. In October, Voorhis was named permanent chief.

On June 19, officers from the Boulder Police Department, the Denver Police Department, the United States Treasury Department, and the FBI executed a raid on an Arapahoe Avenue apartment. The search warrant authorized the men to look for rifles, grenades and a sawed-off shotgun which an informant had reported to be in the building.
