No weapons were found and the two occupants said they anticipated filing a lawsuit against several law enforcement agencies. ("Camera", July 1, Larry Caldwell and Bill Conti)

Bill Conti reported in the "Camera" of February 24:

The firing of former Boulder police officer Philip DiLeo in June, 1973 was justified, according to a new ruling ... by District Court Judge Horace B. Holmes.

Holmes' new decision reversed his own ruling of Oct. 20 ... .

The department rule DiLeo was accused of violating ... (and given as reason for the reversal) ... requires that policemen "be sure that the people and the places we are associated with are of good reputation."

DiLeo's attorney ... intends to appeal the ... (latest) ... decision to the Colorado Court of Appeals.

Linda Cornett reported in the "Camera" dated June 2 that the City Council approved unanimously the settlement of a lawsuit filed by Philip DiLeo allowing him about one-third of his back pay.

In October, Mr. DiLeo filed a \$1.75 million libel suit in District Court against the "Camera", six Boulder police officers and the Boulder Police Benefit Association. ("Camera", October 13)

Serious crimes reported to the Boulder Police Department decreased by 12 percent in 1976 compared to 1975's 30 per cent increase. ("Camera", January 21, 1977, Larry Caldwell)

#### CORRECTIONS

On February 15, Bill Conti reported in the "Camera" that 64 inmates

were transferred from the old jail at 13th and Spruce to new quarters at Sixth and Canyon. Jail Captain Paul Katsampes said, "No matter how much we tried, there wasn't much that could be done at the old facility." The old jail, built in 1961 "was obsolete the day it opened."

By May, the county was planning to purchase a 2-acre parcel near the Justice Center for \$375,000 and convert it to a 230-space parking lot for use by the public and employees.

#### FLOODS

In August, the county sponsored a meeting on "What Boulder County Can Learn from the Big Thompson Flood". There were specific recommendations dealing with improvements in flood warning and evacuation systems, with restrictions on development in flood plains, and reduction in the objects that could become flood-borne debris, and with the need for property owners to buy federal flood insurance.

Warnings now come from the National Weather Service and from a sensor three miles up Boulder Canyon which informs about a slowly rising creek but not a flash flood.

The Urban Drainage and Flood Control District is developing a warning system in Fourmile Canyon as a pilot project. ("Camera", August 25, John Leach)

### WEATHER

An out-of-season wind storm struck Boulder on Monday, June 14. The last major June wind storm was in 1969. More than 20 gusts over 60 miles per hour were measured during a two-hour period in south Boulder. ("Town & Country", June 16.

Boulder Reservoir Manager Richard Marlowe said a total of 24 boats

were damaged or overturned. Winds up to 89 mph hit the reservoir.  
("Camera", June 15, C. M. Riley)

1977

#### OFFENSES

A group of Boulder High School parents want to meet with City Council members to discuss possible action against the News Stand, an adult bookstore that opened a block away from Boulder High School.

City Attorney Wagenhals said in a memo to the city council that "the legislature by repealing the earlier granted zoning powers seems to have expressed an intention that cities throughout the state not use zoning to deal with the problem.

The 1977 Colorado statute on pornography contains no reference to the location of an adult store.

Previous laws did regulate the proximity of such stores to schools and churches. ("Camera", August 18, Linda Castrone)

In September, District Attorney Alex Hunter filed an injunction against the two adult bookstores in Boulder. The Boulder Valley Board of Education expressed their support of his efforts. ("Camera", September 9, Linda Castrone)

In November, District Judge Richard Dana ruled that Colorado's anti-obscenity statute is constitutional. The case will be appealed to the Colorado Supreme Court. ("Camera", November 29)

An ordinance designed to prevent prostitution in massage parlors and protect the reputation of legitimate therapists ... may put most of Boulder's legitimate operators out of business.

The director of the only state-approved massage school in Colorado termed the ordinance discriminatory because it bans visits to client's homes by masseurs and masseuses. ("Camera", November 3, C. M. Riley)

### FIRE AND POLICE

Police Chief David Voorhis has been reorganizing the department. Deputy Chief Lowell Friesen was appointed to the newly created post of Inspector in Charge of Fleet Management and Internal Affairs. Captain Theodore Kozanecki was transferred to Liaison Officer to the District Attorney's office. Captain Clinton Fullen was moved to Director of Parking Services Coordination. ("Camera", February 4)

In March, Chief Voorhis combined the detective and patrol divisions into a single unit under Captain Kelly Gaskill. Officers were returned to a four-day, 10-hour work week. ("Camera", March 19, Larry Caldwell)

In the "Camera" of April 9, Mr. Caldwell reported:

Eight patrolmen and one detective have resigned from the Boulder Police Department since the first of the year, the highest turnover in the history of the organization.

Several patrolmen ... tied the high turnover to the improved national economy. (But other) police officers cite a variety of frustrations: three chiefs of police during the past three years; constant political unrest in the department; continuously dealing with "problem people"; mechanical problems with the squad cars; personality conflicts and lack of respect by patrolmen for some staff officers; and court decisions.

Police Chief David J. Voorhis ... fired two police officers who have been accused of using excessive force and engaging in other unprofessional conduct while booking a prisoner into jail four weeks ago.

Both officers ... are four-year veterans ... . They were suspended without pay following the May 21 incident. ("Camera", June 28, Larry Caldwell)

In the "Rocky Mountain News" of July 10, Al Kamen wrote:

A protracted and increasingly bitter struggle between Boulder Police Chief David Voorhis and the police union has put the department in shambles. Simmering opposition to Voorhis' attempts to shake up the department reached a peak in March, when he appointed Tom Hindman, his administrative assistant, to the position of captain.

Voorhis, Hindman and others familiar with the department's history ... traced the problems back to (former Chief) Vendel's administration. Vendel ruled the department by imposing his own rigid moral standards on officers even when they were off duty.

Vendel's administration ... left captains, lieutenants and sergeants who never learned how to manage and a union formed to protect officers

from what they saw as Vendel's arbitrary discipline, knowledgeable observers say.

That assessment is shared for the most part by City Manager Robert Westdyke. Westdyke said Vendel's replacement, John Barber, couldn't provide the leadership and direction the department needed.

Other sources said the police union stepped in to fill the void created by weak leadership. The union was virtually running the department, some believe.

Voorhis has sought to regain control of the department. It's the tension over that attempt which has been at the root of much of the bitterness of the past few months.

In July, a professional fact-finder was called in to hear both sides after negotiations between the city and the police union went to impasse on non-economic portions of the 1978 contract. ("Camera", July 30)

In August, fourteen new police officers, selected from almost 300 applicants, were added to the under-staffed department.

Ironically, just as the selection procedure was drawing to a close, City Council approved annexation of 400 acres of commercial and industrial property in Gunbarrel and instructed the police department to add six more officers to patrol that area. ("Camera", August 16, Linda Cornett)

Jane Cracraft reported in the "Denver Post" of October 14:

City Manager Bob Westdyke said that most of the dissension in the Police Department is being provoked by ... union negotiator Steve Burton.

Westdyke added that Burton has vowed to "get rid of the chief by Dec. 31."

Westdyke's attack against Burton ... drew a quick response from union members, who said their "no-confidence" vote ... (in Chief Voorhis) ... was taken by secret ballot, and that Burton didn't exert undue pressure on anyone.

In October, one of the officers fired by Chief Voorhis last May was given back pay and ordered reinstated by an arbitrator. The lawyer for the Boulder Police Benefit Association said the officer's "overdiscipline" was an example of the complaints that union members have about the administration of Voorhis. ("Denver Post", October 14)

Jane Cracraft reported in the "Denver Post" of October 22 that Captain Tom Hindman had resigned saying the department's management had been hindered "by harmful and malicious allegations and rumors about me."

In September, Steve Burton had taken his allegations about Hindman to the City Manager who appointed the police chief from Englewood to conduct an investigation. The report was not released. Burton's actions are being reviewed by his supervisors.

Nine months after a major reorganization, the Boulder Police Department is again involved in administrative changes and space shuffling. Lowell Friesen will be returned to the deputy chief post he held almost two years ago. ("Camera", December 2)

Serious crime in Boulder rose 14 percent during 1977. ("Camera", March 21, 1978, Ken Frizell)

In a feature article in the Focus section of the "Camera" of September 25, Linda Castrone wrote:

For years city and fire department officials have stressed the need for change. Not until last year, however, did the two bodies agree about the direction of change.

When city council authorized a study of the fire protection needs of the community in March 1976, fire fighters asked to be included in the review committee.

The group came up with a proposal that represents a drastic shifting of gears.

The plan provided for increased protection in north and south Boulder without hiring any new fire fighters, and the establishment of two new satellite stations at less than half the cost of traditional full-size stations.

The fire department must be able to respond to life-saving calls within three minutes for a 70 per cent chance of success. Those response times also apply to fires.

Review committee members decided that full-size stations at both ends of town would be a duplication, since heavy fire fighting equipment could be brought to fires from existing stations. What was needed was equipment to reach outlying areas quickly and contain fires until heavy equipment could arrive.

Small, easy to maneuver, four-wheel drive engines called Attack Squad Pumpers (ASP) were chosen to fill that need.

Mini-stations, really just houses with oversized garages, could be built to house the ASP units and crew required to operate them ... .

Chief (Jake Ringleman) ... pointed out the resale advantages of the mini-stations.

The ... substation ... (at 4100 Darley) ... was scheduled to open this month, and property at 21st and Norwood has been purchased for a northern station.

A redistribution of existing fire department staff, accomplished by the purchase of more efficient heavy equipment for the three main stations, has freed six men to man the new substations.

In October, the City Council made a supplemental appropriation to acquire property for a fire station in the Gunbarrel area.

Linda Cornett wrote in the "Camera" dated October 30:

Thursday Jake Ringleman packed up the accumulated paraphernalia of a 20-year-career and walked out of the Boulder Fire Chief's office which has been his for the past eight years.

Ringleman joined the fire department in 1958... . (He) will not officially retire ... until July of 1978 ... (but) ... will spend the next few months enjoying an accumulation of vacation time and bonus and sick days.

#### CORRECTIONS

In January, the Boulder District Attorney's office was reviewing the 1972 purchase of the Park Allen Hotel foundation and nearby land as the site for the Justice Center.

In a "Camera" article on January 9, John Leach and Bill Conti wrote:

The investigation centers on an apparent \$260,000 profit by architect James M. Hunter in the sale of the property to the county.

Hunter bought the seven-acre site from United Founders Life Insurance Co. on March 13, 1972, for \$510,000. He sold it to the county four days later for \$770,000, according to county records.

In 1972, the Park Allen Hotel site consisted of seven acres at the southwest corner of Sixth Street and Canyon Boulevard and the foundation and boilers for the planned eight-story luxury hotel. The foundation and boilers were incorporated into the design and construction of the Justice Center.

Construction of the multimillion dollar hotel was begun in 1958 by Boulder financier Allen J. Lefferdink. Hunter was the architect for the building.

But work on the project was stopped in 1960 ... because of legal and financial difficulties which eventually led to the collapse of Lefferdink's 14-company Colorado empire.

The Park Allen property was acquired by a Boulder insurance company in foreclosure proceedings in 1964, and it was eventually sold to United

Founders for \$560,000 in 1970.

A series of articles, many by Leach and Conti for the "Camera", during the first few months of 1977 recount the complex story of how Sheriff Brad Leach, while checking on some structural problems at the Justice Center, prepared and filed a report with the District Attorney on the sequence of land deals since the transfer of the Sixth and Canyon site from the Colorado and Southern Railroad in January 1959. This led to a federal investigation in addition to one conducted by the Boulder District Attorney's office into possible misuse of public funds and possible misconduct by public officials in the county's purchase of the property. No indictments were returned by either the federal or the county grand juries.

#### FLOODS

In the February 20 edition of the "Camera", Linda Castrone wrote:

Boulder is a sitting duck in the case of a flood like the one that ravaged Big Thompson Canyon last summer.

But a substantial wave of water, the kind engineers and weather experts call "the 100-year flood", or now, "the one per cent flood" has not struck since 1894.

The projected one per cent flood of Boulder Canyon has been described in Gilbert White's book, "Assessment of Research on Natural Hazards."

In the 1930s, the Army Corps of Engineers supported a plan to leave Canyon Boulevard (then called Water Street) undeveloped and to build levees on either side of Boulder Creek.

The plan was ignored, and Boulder's floodplain, encompassing 914 acres or a fourth of the city, continued sprouting buildings. Now that solution is impossible.

Regulation of new building on the floodplain is the next best answer.

In the "Camera" of April 12, Steve Hoffman reported:

The new 100-year flood standard established by the Army Corps of Engineers has put a temporary halt on plans to construct a new senior citizens center on city-owned land at 11th and Arapahoe next to Boulder Creek.

Since 1969, the flood standard has been 7400 cubic feet per second (cfs). Now it has been revised to 12,000 cfs.

The flood standard forms the basis of the city's regulation of construction in the floodway and the wider floodplain.

In May, the City Council voted unanimously to ask the Corps of Engineers to design a wide, flat channel from the mouth of Boulder Creek to just past the 17th Street bridge. ("Camera", June 1, Steve Hoffman)

The Sixth Street bridge was scheduled for demolition in December. It will be replaced by a new, higher bridge that would allow the city to build its low-income housing project on Canyon. The site would be out of the floodway after the bridge is completed. ("Camera", November 24, Steve Hoffman)

In December the Council adopted a motion adjusting the South Boulder Creek flood map to reflect channel modifications made by the Flatiron Companies in the area of 55th Street and Valmont Road.

In the "Camera" dated December 30, John Leach reported:

Radio-controlled gauges to measure stream levels and rainfall will be installed in 1978 to give Boulder residents early warning of a major flood in ... Boulder Canyon.

The new system will provide up to 45 minutes warning of a slowly rising creek and at least 15 minutes warning of a flash flood.

It will supplement the efforts of 80 volunteers equipped with rain gauges and portable radios to report above-average rainfall in the mountains west of Boulder.

The ... system ... will be financed by the city ... , Boulder County, and the Denver-based Urban Drainage and Flood Control District.

At the Justice Center, employees of the Sheriff's Department ... (will) ... coordinate evacuation of residents living in Boulder's floodway and floodplain.

#### WEATHER

Boulder winds topped the 100-mile-per-hour mark for the third time in 10 windy days. A gust of 103 mph was recorded at Davidson Mesa. The same atmospheric circulation pattern that was responsible for wind storms November 22 and 26 caused this one, according to National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration scientists. ("Town & Country", December 7)

#### EMERGENCY MEDICAL SERVICE

Feyline Productions and the University of Colorado Program Council requested a change in the city ordinance regulating ambulance companies.

The city code requires that an ambulance service be on duty 24 hours a day, seven days a week, and have direct radio contact with the emergency room at Community Hospital.

The request would have allowed an ambulance company to operate in Boulder when contracted to provide service for gatherings of more than 5,000 people.

The special permit proposal was dismissed by the Council when a motion to bring it up failed to gain a second.

The city staff said that a special permit would rob A-1 of revenues and provide a lower level of service. The city subsidizes A-1 with about \$19,000 a year. ("Camera", August 17, Steve Hoffman)

1978

#### OFFENSES

Three years after Boulder's ban on smoking, only one citation had been issued. ("Camera", October 16, Ken Frizell)

### FIRE AND POLICE

A second Boulder police officer fired last June on charges he used excessive force on a prisoner has been reinstated. ("Camera", January 6)

When the officer reported for duty January 18, he was told he must submit to psychological testing. He refused and was placed on suspension until he agrees to the tests.

In May, six Boulder Police Benefit Association officials signed a letter to City Manager Westdyke detailing the union's complaints against Chief Voorhis. The letter was accompanied by results of a 34-5 union vote asking that Voorhis be fired. The six were charged with insubordination and a month-long disciplinary hearing was held. City Personnel Director Mike Patton, serving as hearing officer, said in a finding of fact ... that the officers had violated departmental rules and that their "statements and their specific language are highly inflammatory, made without any factual basis to support them, and intentionally abusive to the Chief of Police." ("Camera", August 2, Sue Deans)

City Manager Westdyke suspended four of the officers without pay for periods ranging from four to 24 working days. ("Denver Post", August 14, Jane Cracraft)

Members of the Boulder Police Benefit Association (BPBA) voted to file suit against the city to overturn the suspensions. ("Camera", August 25, Ken Frizell) However, the suit had not been filed by the time all four of the officers went back on active duty in September. ("Camera", September 21, Ken Frizell)

During October, Chief Voorhis, City Manager Westdyke and BPBA President Steve Burton met periodically in an effort to cool the friction. On October 20, Chief Voorhis announced his resignation, effective December 1. ("Camera", October 22, Sue Deans and Ken Frizell)

Deputy Police Chief Lowell Friesen was appointed acting chief.

In the "Camera" dated October 25, Ken Frizell wrote "It isn't exactly a case of forgive and forget, but the Boulder city administration and dissident elements of the Boulder police union ... admitted they may have taken some inappropriate actions in recent months."

Marty Dee reported in the "Camera" of December 23:

A six-member District Court jury awarded \$90,000 ... to two residents of a Boulder apartment raided by police June 19, 1976.

Insurance agencies for the cities will pay at least part of the money awarded.

An insurance company attorney said he will request Boulder District Court Judge Richard Dana to reduce the amount of damages awarded by the jury and plans an appeal to the Colorado Court of Appeals as well. If both tactics fail, he said he will request that the Boulder City Council help two police officers pay the punitive damages awarded.

The jury declared that Detective Bill Spotts and former Chief of Detectives Kelly Gaskill illegally searched an apartment and illegally arrested its two occupants. ("Camera", December 30, Marty Dee)

In November, the Council was given a report on the possibility of a civilian board to review discipline imposed on Boulder police officers. City Manager Bob Westdyke did not recommend the board, but some Council members wanted to continue the discussion. ("Camera", November 10, Linda Cornett)

Serious crime in Boulder increased 10 percent over 1977. ("Camera", January 31, 1979, Ken Frizell)

Ken Frizell wrote in the "Camera" dated May 31:

For several years now most Boulder firemen have insisted on being called fire fighters. As of June 12, that genderless term will be even more appropriate.

On that date, for the first time in the history of the Boulder Fire Department, women will join the force as fire fighters.

Acting Chief Dean Carpenter said ... (the two women) ... will be sent to the new Gunbarrel Station, ... (which) ... has been designed to accommodate women.

Originally scheduled to be completed in July, Fire Station No. 6 was delayed when the contractor was terminated due to default.

Charles E. Boyes was named fire chief on July 5.

#### FLOODS

At a study session in June, the Council was told that changes are necessary in the city's floodplain regulations if Boulder property is to be eligible for federally sponsored flood insurance. Without the insurance, no building can receive a mortgage from a federally insured lender. ("Camera", June 14, Linda Cornett)

A citizen's advisory committee was organized to aid in the floodplain planning project for Boulder Creek.

For the past several months, according to ... (Boulder Utilities Director Doug) ... Smith, the city has been working out a detailed study of the benefits and costs of that plan ... (calling for an excavation of Boulder Creek's floodway from Sixth Street to 17th Street).

The study, Smith said, should be handed over to the advisory committee by August. ("Camera", August 1, Linda Cornett)

In the September 13 issue of the "Camera", Linda Cornett reported:

Questions on the controversial plan to create an excavated floodway along the western portion of the creek were raised by members of the citizen's committee, a consulting firm and council members.

The University of Colorado, with major student housing facilities downstream, is particularly concerned about what the city plans to do.

United Research Systems (URS) researchers suggested ways of eliminating potential hazards along Boulder Creek from Eben Fine Park to 17th Street. They suggested bridge improvements downstream from 17th Street.

The Council echoed concerns expressed by Dick McLean, head of the advisory committee and by Ken Wright whose consulting firm acted as a research source, that the preliminary report left too many gaps. They delayed any decision and sent the study back to URS for more work.

#### WEATHER

A massive spring snowstorm has caved in buildings, clogged streets created giant potholes, stranded cars, destroyed trees and knocked down powerlines in Boulder.

Ten inches of snow were added Friday night to the 10 inches that fell the previous 24 hours.

About 14 more inches fell Saturday. ("Camera", May 7, Marty Dee)

The snow began falling on Friday, May 5 and didn't stop until there were 31 inches on the ground in west Boulder.

The heavy snow collapsed roofs on the Arnett Hotel building on Pearl Street, recently known as Shannon's, and the Colorado Division of Employment and Training building at 2950 Walnut Street. ("Camera", May 7, 1979, Sharon Massingill)

A freak August wind storm with gusts as high as 88 mph were recorded at the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration laboratory. A record low temperature of 44 degrees was recorded by the National Weather Service in Denver. ("Camera", August 15, Todd Malmsbury)

The summer of 1978 has been the hottest in more than 100 years of record-keeping in Denver, and the hottest in Boulder since 1960.

But, Boulder, with 42 days of 90 degree or above temperatures probably won't approach the record year of 1952 ... . ("Camera", September 7, Todd Malmsbury)

Temperatures Thursday night in Boulder came within five degrees of the all-time record of 19 below zero, making it the coldest December evening since 1972.

The record low for Boulder in December is 19 below in 1932. In 1972, the temperature dropped to 16 below. ("Camera", December 8, Todd Malmsbury)

On December 9, the roof over the Student Center at Fairview High School collapsed under a heavy load of snow. During construction, eight trusses had been shortened at one end, then the elements were welded back together. ("Camera", December 22, Linda Castrone)

The State Division of Public Safety is responsible for inspecting new school buildings. City building inspectors were not called in to review plans during the construction of the high school. The state thinks the school was inspected in 1972 on its completion, but files dealing with Fairview since early 1970 are missing. ("Camera", January 16, 1979, Linda Castrone)

1979

### FIRE AND POLICE

Ken Frizell and Linda Cornett wrote in the "Camera" dated January 12:

Boulder County Sheriff Brad Leach ... proposed that the City of Boulder contract with his office for police protection -- effectively eliminating an often troublesome city Police Department.

Leach said ... that given present levels of funding, the consolidation would be especially beneficial to the city. (There would be) ... the consolidation of functions now being duplicated by the two departments -- record keeping, for example -- and the elimination of some supervisory level positions.

... he also acknowledged that there are several obstacles to be overcome first -- not the least of which is the police union, which would no longer exist under his plan. The Sheriff's Department is non-union.

Equally serious obstacles would be the consolidation of salary, benefit and pension packages. The city's pension plan, which is in deep financial trouble, would be a major problem.

Other problems would involve contracts now held between the city and the unions, changing the Charter which designates the City Manager an ex-officio director of public safety, and the fact that the City Manager had already begun to interview applicants for the position of police chief.

Council members agreed with the City Manager that a chief for the department be hired immediately, thus setting aside Sheriff Leach's proposal. ("Camera", January 17, Linda Cornett)

City Manager Westdyke selected Jay H. Propst to become the fourth

police chief in four years. He will assume his duties on March 5.  
("Camera", February 18, Ken Frizell)

By July, Chief Propst had made some changes in the allocation of money and personnel. The Council had voted a special allocation for improvement of police services. ("Denver Post", July 16, Jane Cracraft)

To bring pension contributions for fire and police officers in line with recent state legislation, the Council approved an amendment increasing the limit on employee contributions. ("Camera", June 20, Sue Deans)

Ken Frizell reported in the "Camera" of February 6:

In 1975, Boulder paid approximately \$8,500 for false arrest insurance. In 1976, the same coverage cost more than \$61,000. In 1977 the city couldn't buy the insurance at any price and began putting money into a self-insurance fund.

The reason had to do with the fact that the Boulder Police Department averaged about two complaints against officers every month. About 20 percent of the time, the complaints involved physical abuse.

Most damaging, however, were judgements against the city ... .

Bob Keatley, a legal advisor to the Boulder police chief, says the decision in the Arapahoe Avenue raid case has had a profound impact on the department. He said he has been approached by several officers fearful that they, too, might some day be defendants in a false arrest suit ... and might have to put ... personal assets on the line.

Sergeant Bill Spotts and former Chief of Detectives Kelly Gaskill were denied a motion for a new trial. They had argued that they were not defended properly by a Denver insurance lawyer who recently died in a car accident. ("Camera", April 7, Marty Dee)

The City Council unanimously decided to pay the punitive damages assessed against the officers for the illegal search and arrests. The aggrieved parties agreed to accept \$50,000 if the city would settle the case. Special City Attorney Paul Snyder recommended approval of the settlement and suggested the city might be able to recover the money by suing the law firm or estate of the lawyer hired to defend Spotts and

Gaskill. ("Camera", May 2, Marty Dee)

In a Golden District Court, the city charged that the lawyer had a "conflict of interest making it impossible to adequately represent the interests" of the police officers and the city. ("Camera", August 16, Marty Dee)

Marty Dee reported in the "Camera" dated June 27:

A compact between the Police and Sheriff's departments is expected to solve law enforcement problems at Coot Lake, a haven for nude sun-seekers northeast of the city.

Law enforcement agencies have had jurisdiction disputes when dealing with crimes around the lake because it is owned by the city but located outside city limits in the county.

Now, city officers will have full enforcement powers, though they must act under state rather than city laws.

Both Boulder Police and County Sheriff's officers patrolled the Coot Lake area during the summer, requesting people to leash their dogs and dump their open containers of alcohol.

Serious crime in Boulder continued to climb in 1979. Statistics showed a rise of 11 percent over 1978.

#### CORRECTIONS

By August of this year, overcrowding at the jail was forcing some prisoners to sleep on the floor.

Captain Al Staehle said Thursday that several times in recent weeks the jail ... situation ... has increased tension among the inmates.

(He said) the problem ... developed earlier this summer because not as many prisoners are bonding out of jail as quickly as usual, more prisoners are serving weekend sentences and because there have been more arrests.

"It is really something that is catching up to us because of the county's growth."

Staehle ... estimated that within the next three to four years, the capacity at the jail would somehow have to be expanded. ("Camera", August 10, Ken Frizell)

#### FLOODS

All the studies activated after the Big Thompson flood in 1976 indicated that a multi-faceted early warning system was necessary.

The Urban Drainage and Flood Control District placed the Boulder Creek drainage as the most dangerous in terms of flood potential in the state. The District was established by the state legislature to deal with flood and water problems and Boulder County is a member of the district. Through funds from the district and matching funds from the city and the county totaling \$150,000, an early warning system has been developed. ("Denver Post", January 31, Pam Patrick Novotny)

In March, the final report on excavating Boulder Creek, prepared by Dr. Daryl Simons and Dr. Ruh-Ming Li of Colorado State University, was completed.

It concluded that the \$10 million project could lower the level of a flood and reduce the area flooded.

Based on the report, Water Utility Director Doug Smith told the citizen's committee that excavating the flood channel would work.

However, it will probably be another two to three years before the Corps of Engineers decides whether the cost of the project is worth the damage it would prevent.

If ... the benefits are equal to or less than costs, the city could simply decide to improve bridges the length of the creek to allow more flood water and debris to pass through.

(The Folsom, 28th and 30th Street) bridges and the flood plain which fans out around them are a serious concern to a task force looking into redevelopment of the ... (Crossroads) area. ("Camera", March 20, Linda Cornett)

Sue Deans wrote in the "Camera" dated May 13:

An apartment building under construction in the Boulder Creek floodway has focused the attention of Gov. Dick Lamm and the state Land Use Commission on the possibility of a Boulder Creek flood.

The Gold Run apartments, being built in Sherrelwood Estates, along the creek between 28th and 30th Streets, are nearly completed, despite

several years of efforts by Boulder's city government to stop the project.

Lamm ... issued an emergency order stopping work until the Land Use Commission can investigate whether the buildings will hold up in a flood.

City Utilities Director Doug Smith said in his opinion Sherrelwood would not last through a 10-year flood, let alone a 100-year flood.

But Smith qualified that opinion by admitting that the developer, Jack Fowler, has not provided him with engineering evidence from the builder's point of view.

On August 17, the state Land Use Commission declared the buildings a public hazard and asked the governor if a cease and desist order barring further development could be issued.

Commission members asked that the buildings not be occupied until major modifications are made.

The project has been mired in controversy and red tape nearly 10 years.

It originally was approved before Boulder's flood plain regulations, prohibiting construction in the floodway, went into effect in 1974.

A court action by the city of Boulder trying to force compliance was unsuccessful.

But the City Council last week authorized a further appeal to the Colorado Supreme Court, with the Urban Drainage and Flood Control District as a party. ("Camera", August 28, Sue Deans)

Mark Thomas reported in the "Camera" dated October 20 that the Council approved conceptual guidelines for a settlement with Sherrelwood, Inc. Six property owners in Cordry Court could lose some of their property to a flood control channel. Along with a flood control wall, the channel would remove both the Sherrelwood Apartments and the Cordry Court neighborhood from the creek's floodplain.

#### WEATHER

A chinook wind hit Boulder December 4, causing an estimated \$1.2 million in damage. Unlike most Boulder wind storms, wind speeds varied dramatically from 90 mph gusts to only 10 mph and back. ("Camera", December 5, December 6, Todd Malmsbury and Mike Stone)

Boulder's average annual snowfall is 84 inches; the old record was 123.8 inches in 1959. In 1979, it was anywhere from 123.8 inches to 162.2 inches according to the location of the gauges.

All-time high temperature records were broken six times and there was one record low in August.

Snowfall in November dropped 31.4 inches, more than any other November storm in the city's history. A storm just after Christmas dropped another 25 inches.

The city had \$72,000 left in its budgeted snow removal fund when the storm hit on November 19 and 20. That cost \$110,000 in city equipment use, contracts with independent contractors and employee overtime.

The cost estimates of the second storm on December 28 were \$25,000. Street Maintenance Director Larry Grauberger says the snow removal plan is to keep the major arterials passable, but to leave the side streets alone, despite complaints from residents.

Environmental Protection Officer Jim Adams received 447 complaints about dangerous sidewalks during the November storm. City ordinances require property owners to shovel their walks by noon of the day after a snowfall stops. The city has the power to do the work and charge the property owners for the service. ("Camera", January 6, 1980, Daryl Gibson)
